<seg id="513">Historically, the device that has evolved most frequently in many different places and over the course of centuries is a commodity standard;</seg>
<seg id="514">i.e., the use as money of some physical commodity such as gold or silver, brass or tin, cigarettes or cognac, or various other goods.</seg>
<seg id="515">If money consisted wholly of a physical commodity of this type, there would be, in principle, no need for control by the government at all.</seg>
<seg id="516">The amount of money in society would depend on the cost of producing the monetary commodity rather than other things.</seg>
<seg id="517">Changes in the amount of money would depend on changes in the technical conditions of producing the monetary commodity and on changes in the demand for money.</seg>
<seg id="518">This is an ideal that animates many believers in an automatic gold standard.</seg>
<seg id="519">Actual commodity standards have deviated very far from this simple pattern which requires no governmental intervention.</seg>
<seg id="520">Historically, a commodity standardˇŞsuch as a gold standard or a silver standardˇŞhas been accompanied by the development of fiduciary money of one kind or another, ostensibly convertible into the monetary commodity on fixed terms.</seg>
<seg id="521">There was a very good reason for this development.</seg>
<seg id="522">The fundamental defect of a commodity standard, from the point of view of the society as a whole, is that it requires the use of real resources to add to the stock of money.</seg>
<seg id="523">People must work hard to dig gold out of the ground in South AfricaˇŞin order to rebury it in Fort Knox or some similar place.</seg>
<seg id="524">The necessity of using real resources for the operation of a commodity standard establishes a strong incentive for people to find ways to achieve the same result without employing these resources.</seg>
<seg id="525">If people will accept as money pieces of paper on which is printed "I promise to payˇŞˇŞˇŞunits of the commodity standard," these pieces of paper can perform the same function as the physical pieces of gold or silver, and they require very much less in resources to produce.</seg>
<seg id="526">This point, which I have discussed at somewhat greater length elsewhere, seems to me the fundamental difficulty with a commodity standard.</seg>
<seg id="527">If an automatic commodity standard were feasible, it would provide an excellent solution to the liberal's dilemma:</seg>
<seg id="528">monetary framework without the danger of the irresponsible exercise of monetary powers.</seg>
<seg id="529">If, for example, an honest-to-goodness gold standard, in which 100 per cent of the money in a country consisted literally of gold, were widely backed by the public at large, imbued with the mythology of a gold standard and with the belief that it is immoral and improper for government to interfere with its operation, it would provide an effective guarantee against governmental tinkering with the currency and against irresponsible monetary action.</seg>
<seg id="530">Under such a standard, any monetary powers of government would be very minor in scope.</seg>
<seg id="531">But, as just noted, such an automatic system has historically never proved feasible.</seg>
<seg id="532">It has always tended to develop in the direction of a mixed system containing fiduciary elements such as bank notes and deposits, or government notes in addition to the monetary commodity.</seg>
<seg id="533">And once fiduciary elements have been introduced, it has proved difficult to avoid governmental control over them, even when they were initially issued by private individuals.</seg>
<seg id="534">The reason is basically the difficulty of preventing counterfeiting or its economic equivalent.</seg>
<seg id="535">Fiduciary money is a contract to pay standard money.</seg>
<seg id="536">It so happens that there tends to be a long interval between the making of such a contract and its realization.</seg>
<seg id="537">This enhances the difficulty of enforcing the contract and hence also the temptation to issue fraudulent contracts.</seg>
<seg id="538">In addition, once fiduciary elements have been introduced, the temptation for government itself to issue fiduciary money is almost irresistible.</seg>
<seg id="539">In practice, therefore, commodity standards have tended to become mixed standards involving extensive intervention by the state.</seg>
<seg id="540">It should be noted that despite the great amount of talk by many people in favor of the gold standard, almost no one today literally desires an honest-to-goodness, full gold standard.</seg>
<seg id="541">People who say they want a gold standard are almost invariably talking about the present kind of standard, or the kind of standard that was maintained in the 1930's;</seg>
<seg id="542">a gold standard managed by a central bank or other governmental bureau, which holds a small amount of gold as "backing"ˇŞto use that very misleading termˇŞfor fiduciary money.</seg>
<seg id="543">Some do go so far as to favor the kind of standard maintained in the 1920's, in which there was literal circulation of gold or gold certificates as hand to-hand currencyˇŞa gold-coin standardˇŞbut even they favor the coexistence with gold of governmental fiduciary currency plus deposits issued by banks holding fractional reserves in either gold or fiduciary currency.</seg>
<seg id="544">Even during the so-called great days of the gold standard in the nineteenth century, when the Bank of England was supposedly running the gold standard skilfully, the  monetary system was far from a fully automatic gold standard.</seg>
<seg id="545">Even then it was a highly managed standard.</seg>
<seg id="546">And certainly the situation is now more extreme as a result of the adoption by country after country of the view that government has responsibility for "full employment."</seg>
<seg id="547">My conclusion is that an automatic commodity standard is neither a feasible nor a desirable solution to the problem of establishing monetary arrangements for a free society.</seg>
<seg id="548">It is not desirable because it would involve a large cost in the form of resources used to produce the monetary commodity.</seg>
<seg id="549">It is not feasible because the mythology and beliefs required to make it effective do not exist.</seg>
<seg id="550">This conclusion is supported not only by the general historical evidence referred to but also by the specific experience of the United States.</seg>
<seg id="551">From 1879, when the United States resumed gold payments after the Civil War, to 1913, the United States was on a gold standard.</seg>
<seg id="552">Though closer to a thoroughly automatic gold standard than anything we have had since the end of World War I, the gold standard was still far from a 100 per cent gold standard.</seg>
<seg id="553">There were government issues of paper money, and private banks issued most of the effective circulating medium of the country in the form of deposits;</seg>
<seg id="554">the banks were closely regulated in their operations by governmental agenciesˇŞnational banks by the Comptroller of the Currency, state banks by state banking authorities.</seg>
<seg id="555">Gold, whether held by the Treasury, by banks, or directly by individuals as coins or gold certificates, accounted for between 10 per cent and 20 per cent of the money stock, the exact percentage varying from year to year.</seg>
<seg id="556">The remaining 80 per cent to 90 per cent consisted of silver, fiduciary currency, and bank deposits not matched by gold reserves.</seg>
<seg id="557">In retrospect, the system may seem to us to have worked reasonably well.</seg>
<seg id="558">To Americans of the time, it clearly did not.</seg>
<seg id="559">The agitation over silver in the 1880's, culminating in Bryan's Cross of Gold speech which set the tone for the 1896 election, was one sign of dissatisfaction.</seg>
<seg id="560">In turn, the agitation was largely responsible for the severely depressed years in the early 1890's.</seg>
<seg id="561">The agitation led to widespread fears that the United States would go off gold and that hence the dollar would lose value in terms of foreign currencies.</seg>
<seg id="562">This led to a flight from the dollar and a capital outflow that forced deflation at home.</seg>
<seg id="563">Successive financial crises, in 1873, 1884, 1890, and 1893 produced a widespread demand for banking reform on the part of the business and banking community.</seg>
<seg id="564">The panic of 1907, involving the concerted refusal by banks to convert deposits into currency on demand, finally crystallized the feeling of dissatisfaction with the financial system into an urgent demand for governmental action.</seg>
<seg id="565">A National Monetary Commission was established by Congress, and its recommendations, reported in 1910, were embodied in the Federal Reserve Act passed in 1913.</seg>
<seg id="566">Reforms along the lines of the Federal Reserve Act had the backing of every section of the community, from the working classes to the bankers, and of both political parties.</seg>
<seg id="567">The chairman of the National Monetary Commission was a Republican, Nelson W. Aldrich;</seg>
<seg id="568">the Senator mainly responsible for the Federal Reserve Act was a Democrat, Carter W. Glass.</seg>
<seg id="569">The change in monetary arrangements introduced by the Federal Reserve Act turned out in practice to be far more drastic than was intended by its authors or its supporters.</seg>
<seg id="570">At the time the Act was passed, a gold standard reigned supreme throughout the worldˇŞnot a fully automatic gold standard but something far closer to that ideal than anything we have experienced since.</seg>
<seg id="571">It was taken for granted that it would continue to do so and thus narrowly limit the powers of the Federal Reserve System.</seg>
<seg id="572">No sooner was the Act passed than World War I broke out.</seg>
<seg id="573">There was a large-scale abandonment of the gold standard.</seg>
<seg id="574">By the end of the war, the Reserve System was no longer a minor adjunct to the gold standard designed to insure the convertibility of one form of money into others and to regulate and supervise banks.</seg>
<seg id="575">It had become a powerful discretionary authority able to determine the quantity of money in the United States and to affect international financial conditions throughout the world.</seg>
<seg id="576">A Discretionary Monetary Authority</seg>
<seg id="577">The establishment of the Federal Reserve System was the most notable change in United States monetary institutions since at least the Civil War National Banking Act.</seg>
<seg id="578">For the first time since the expiration of the charter of the Second Bank of the United States in 1836, it established a separate official body charged with explicit responsibility for monetary conditions, and supposedly clothed with adequate power to achieve monetary stability or, at least, to prevent pronounced instability.</seg>
<seg id="579">It is therefore instructive to compare experience as a whole before and after its establishmentˇŞsay, from just after the Civil War to 1914 and from 1914 to date, to take two periods of equal length.</seg>
<seg id="580">The second period was clearly the more unstable economically, whether instability is measured by the fluctuations in the stock of money, in prices, or in output.</seg>
<seg id="581">Partly, the greater instability reflects the effect of two world wars during the second period;</seg>
<seg id="582">these would clearly have been a source of instability whatever our monetary system.</seg>
<seg id="583">But even if the war and immediate postwar years are omitted, and we consider only the peacetime years from, say, 1920 through 1939, and 1947 to date, the result is the same.</seg>
<seg id="584">The stock of money, prices, and output was decidedly more unstable after the establishment of the Reserve System than before.</seg>
<seg id="585">The most dramatic period of instability in output was of course the period between the two wars which includes the severe contractions of 1920¨C21, 1929¨C33, and 1937¨C38.</seg>
<seg id="586">No other twenty-year period in American history contains as many as three such severe contractions.</seg>
<seg id="587">This crude comparison does not of course prove that the Federal Reserve System failed to contribute to monetary stability.</seg>
<seg id="588">Perhaps the problems that the System had to handle were more severe than those that impinged on the earlier monetary structure.</seg>
<seg id="589">Perhaps those problems would have produced an even greater degree of monetary instability under the earlier arrangements.</seg>
<seg id="590">But the crude comparison should at least give the reader pause before he takes for granted, as is so often done, that an agency as long established, as powerful, as pervasive as the Federal Reserve System is performing a necessary and desirable function and is contributing to the attainment of the objectives for which it was established.</seg>
<seg id="591">I am myself persuaded, on the basis of extensive study of the historical evidence, that the difference in economic stability revealed by the crude comparison is in fact attributable to the difference in monetary institutions.</seg>
<seg id="592">This evidence persuades me that at least a third of the price rise during and just after World War I is attributable to the establishment of the Federal Reserve System and would not have occurred if the earlier banking system had been retained;</seg>
<seg id="593">that the severity of each of the major contractionsˇŞ1920¨C21, 1929¨C33, and 1937¨C38ˇŞis directly attributable to acts of commission and omission by the Reserve authorities and would not have occurred under earlier monetary and banking arrangements.</seg>
<seg id="594">There might well have been recessions on these or other occasions, but it is highly unlikely that any would have developed into a major contraction.</seg>
<seg id="595">I clearly cannot present this evidence here.2 However, in view of the importance which the Great Depression of 1929¨C33 played in formingˇŞor, I would say, deformingˇŞgeneral attitudes toward the role of government in economic affairs, it may be worth indicating more fully for this episode the kind of interpretation suggested by the evidence.</seg>
<seg id="596">Because of its dramatic character, the stock market crash in October, 1929, which terminated the bull market of 1928 and 1929 is often regarded as both the start and the major proximate cause of the Great Depression.</seg>
<seg id="597">Neither is correct.</seg>
<seg id="598">The peak of business was reached in mid-1929, some months prior to the crash.</seg>
<seg id="599">The peak may well have come as early as it did partly as a result of relatively tight money conditions imposed by the Federal Reserve System in an attempt to curb ''speculation"ˇŞin this indirect way, the stock market may have played a role in bringing about the contraction.</seg>
<seg id="600">The stock market crash in turn undoubtedly had some indirect effects on business confidence2 See my A Program for Monetary Stability and Milton Friedman and Anna J. Schwartz, A Monetary History of the United States, 1867¨C1960 (forthcoming by Princeton University Press for the National Bureau of Economic Research) and on the willingness of individuals to spend which exerted a depressing influence on the course of business.</seg>
<seg id="601">But by themselves, these effects could not have produced a collapse in economic activity.</seg>
<seg id="602">At most, they would have made the contraction somewhat longer and more severe than the usual mild recessions that have punctuated American economic growth throughout our history;</seg>
<seg id="603">they would not have made it the catastrophe it was.</seg>
<seg id="604">For something like the first year, the contraction showed none of those special features that were to dominate its later course.</seg>
<seg id="605">The economic decline was more severe than during the first year of most contractions, possibly in response to the stock market crash plus the unusually tight monetary conditions that had been maintained since mid-1928.</seg>
<seg id="606">But it showed no qualitatively different characteristics, no signs of degenerating into a major catastrophe.</seg>
<seg id="607">Except on na?ve post hoc ergo propter hoc reasoning, there is nothing in the economic situation as it stood in, say, September or October, 1930 that made the continued and drastic decline of the following years inevitable or even highly probable.</seg>
<seg id="608">In retrospect, it is clear that the Reserve System should already have been behaving differently than it did, that it should not have allowed the money stock to decline by nearly 3 per cent from August 1929 to October 1930ˇŞa larger decline than during the whole of all but the most severe prior contractions.</seg>
<seg id="609">Though this was a mistake, it was perhaps excusable, and certainly not critical.</seg>
<seg id="610">The character of the contraction changed drastically in November 1930, when a series of bank failures led to widespread runs on banks, which is to say attempts by depositors to convert deposits into currency.</seg>
<seg id="611">The contagion spread from one part of the country to another and reached a climax with the failure on December 11, 1930 of the Bank of the United States.</seg>
<seg id="612">This failure was critical not only because the Bank was one of the largest in the country, with over $ 200 million in deposits, but also because, though an ordinary commercial bank, its name had led many both at home and even more abroad to regard it as somehow an official bank.</seg>
<seg id="613">Prior to October, 1930, there had been no sign of a liquidity crisis, or any loss of confidence in banks.</seg>
<seg id="614">From this time on, the economy was plagued by recurrent liquidity crises.</seg>
<seg id="615">A wave of bank failures would taper down a while, and then start up again as a few dramatic failures or other events produced a new loss of confidence in the banking system and a new series of runs on banks.</seg>
<seg id="616">These were important not only or even primarily because of the failures of the banks but because of their effect on the money stock.</seg>
<seg id="617">In a fractional reserve banking system like ours, a bank does not of course have a dollar of currency (or its equivalent) for a dollar of deposits.</seg>
<seg id="618">That is why "deposits" is such a misleading term.</seg>
<seg id="619">When you deposit a dollar of cash in a bank, the bank may add fifteen or twenty cents to its cash;</seg>
<seg id="620">the rest it will lend out through another window.</seg>
<seg id="621">The borrower may in turn redeposit it, in this or another bank, and the process is repeated.</seg>
<seg id="622">The result is that for every dollar of cash owned by banks, they owe several dollars of deposits.</seg>
<seg id="623">The total stock of moneyˇŞcash plus depositsˇŞfor a given amount of cash is therefore higher the larger the fraction of its money the public is willing to hold as deposits.</seg>
<seg id="624">Any widespread attempt on the part of depositors to "get their money" must therefore mean a decline in the total amount of money unless there is some way in which additional cash can be created and some way for banks to get it.</seg>
<seg id="625">Otherwise, one bank, in trying to satisfy its depositors, will put pressure on other banks by calling loans or selling investments or withdrawing its deposits and these other banks in turn will put pressure on still others.</seg>
<seg id="626">The vicious cycle, if allowed to proceed, grows on itself as the attempt of banks to get cash forces down the prices of securities, renders banks insolvent that would otherwise have been entirely sound, shakes the confidence of depositors, and starts the cycle over again.</seg>
<seg id="627">This was precisely the kind of a situation that had led to a banking panic under the pre-FederalReserve banking system, and to a concerted suspension of the convertibility of deposits into currency, as in 1907.</seg>
<seg id="628">Such a suspension was a drastic step and for a short while made matters worse.</seg>
<seg id="629">But it was also a therapeutic measure.</seg>
<seg id="630">It cut short the vicious cycle by preventing the spread of the contagion, by keeping the failure of a few banks from producing pressure on other banks and leading to the failure of otherwise sound banks.</seg>
<seg id="631">In a few weeks or months, when the situation had stabilized, the suspension could be lifted, and recovery begin without monetary contraction.</seg>
<seg id="632">As we have seen, one of the major reasons for establishing the Federal Reserve System was to deal with such a situation.</seg>
<seg id="633">It was given the power to create more cash if a widespread demand should arise on the part of the public for currency instead of deposits, and was given the means to make the cash available to banks on the security of the bank's assets.</seg>
<seg id="634">In this way, it was expected that any threatened panic could be averted, that there would be no need for suspension of convertibility of deposits into currency, and that the depressing effects of monetary crises could be entirely avoided.</seg>
<seg id="635">The first need for these powers and hence the first test of their efficacy came in November and December of 1930 as a result of the string of bank closings already described.</seg>
<seg id="636">The Reserve System failed the test miserably.</seg>
<seg id="637">It did little or nothing to provide the banking system with liquidity, apparently regarding the bank closings as calling for no special action.</seg>
<seg id="638">It is worth emphasizing, however, that the System's failure was a failure of will, not of power.</seg>
<seg id="639">On this occasion, as on the later ones that followed, the System had ample power to provide the banks with the cash their depositors were demanding.</seg>
<seg id="640">Had this been done, the bank closings would have been cut short and the monetary debacle averted.</seg>
<seg id="641">The initial wave of bank failures died down and in early 1931 there were signs of returning confidence.</seg>
<seg id="642">The Reserve System took advantage of the opportunity to reduce its own credit outstandingˇŞwhich is to say, it offset the naturally expansionary forces by engaging in mild deflationary action.</seg>
<seg id="643">Even so, there were clear signs of improvement not only in the monetary sector but also in other economic activities.</seg>
<seg id="644">The figures for the first four or five months of 1931, if examined without reference to what actually followed, have all the earmarks of the bottom of a cycle and the beginning of revival.</seg>
<seg id="645">The tentative revival was however short-lived.</seg>
<seg id="646">Renewed bank failures started another series of runs and again set in train a renewed decline in the stock of money.</seg>
<seg id="647">Again, the Reserve System stood idly by.</seg>
<seg id="648">In the face of an unprecedented liquidation of the commercial banking system, the books of the "lender of last resort" show a decline in the amount of credit it made available to its member banks.</seg>
<seg id="649">In September 1931, Britain went off the gold standard.</seg>
<seg id="650">This act was preceded and followed by gold withdrawals from the United States.</seg>
<seg id="651">Although gold had been flowing into the United States in the prior two years, and the U.S. gold stock and the Federal Reserve gold reserve ratio were at an all time high, the Reserve System reacted vigorously and promptly to the external drain as it had not to the previous internal drain.</seg>
<seg id="652">It did so in a manner that was certain to intensify the internal financial difficulties.</seg>
<seg id="653">After more than two years of severe economic contraction, the System raised the discount rateˇŞthe rate of interest at which it stood ready to lend to member banksˇŞmore sharply than it has within so brief a period in its whole history before or since.</seg>
<seg id="654">The measure arrested the gold drain.</seg>
<seg id="655">It was also accompanied by a spectacular increase in bank failures and runs on banks.</seg>
<seg id="656">In the six months from August 1931 through January 1932, roughly one out of ten banks in existence suspended operations and total deposits in commercial banks fell by 15 per cent.</seg>
<seg id="657">A temporary reversal of policy in 1932 involving the purchase of $ 1 billion of government bonds slowed down the rate of decline.</seg>
<seg id="658">Had this measure been taken in 1931, it would almost surely have been sufficient to prevent the debacle just described.</seg>
<seg id="659">By 1932, it was too late to be more than a palliative and, when the System relapsed into passivity, the temporary improvement was followed by a renewed collapse terminating in the Banking Holiday of 1933ˇŞwhen every bank in the United States was officially closed for over a week.</seg>
<seg id="660">A system established in large part to prevent a temporary suspension of convertibility of deposits into currencyˇŞa measure that had formerly prevented banks from failingˇŞfirst let nearly a third of the banks of the country go out of existence and then welcomed a suspension of convertibility that was incomparably more sweeping and severe than any earlier suspension.</seg>
<seg id="661">Yet so great is the capacity for self-justification that the Federal Reserve Board could write in its annual report for 1933, "The ability of the Federal Reserve Banks to meet enormous demands for currency during the crisis demonstrated the effectiveness of the country's currency system under the Federal Reserve Act. . . .</seg>
<seg id="662">It is difficult to say what the course of the depression would have been had the Federal Reserve System not pursued a policy of liberal open market purchases.</seg>
<seg id="663">"All told, from July 1929 to March 1933, the money stock in the United States fell by one-third, and over two-thirds of the decline came after England's departure from the gold standard.</seg>
<seg id="664">Had the money stock been kept from declining, as it clearly could and should have been, the contraction would have been both shorter and far milder.</seg>
<seg id="665">It might still have been relatively severe by historical standards.</seg>
<seg id="666">But it is literally inconceivable that money income could have declined by over one-half and prices by over one-third in the course of four years if there had been no decline in the stock of money.</seg>
<seg id="667">I know of no severe depression in any country or any time that was not accompanied by a sharp decline in the stock of money and equally of no sharp decline in the stock of money that was not accompanied by a severe depression.</seg>
<seg id="668">The Great Depression in the United States, far from being a sign of the inherent instability of the private enterprise system, is a testament to how much harm can be done by mistakes on the part of a few men when they wield vast power over the monetary system of a country.</seg>
<seg id="669">It may be that these mistakes were excusable on the basis of the knowledge available to men at the timeˇŞthough I happen to think not.</seg>
<seg id="670">But that is really beside the point.</seg>
<seg id="671">Any system which gives so much power and so much discretion to a few men that mistakesˇŞexcusable or notˇŞcan have such far-reaching effects is a bad system.</seg>
<seg id="672">It is a bad system to believers in freedom just because it gives a few men such power without any effective check by the body politicˇŞthis is the key political argument against an "independent" central bank.</seg>
<seg id="673">But it is a bad system even to those who set security higher than freedom.</seg>
<seg id="674">Mistakes, excusable or not, cannot be avoided in a system which disperses responsibility yet gives a few men great power, and which thereby makes important policy actions highly dependent on accidents of personality.</seg>
<seg id="675">This is the key technical argument against an "independent" bank.</seg>
<seg id="676">To paraphrase Clemenceau, money is much too serious a matter to be left to the Central Bankers.</seg>
<seg id="677">Rules Instead of AuthoritiesIf we can achieve our objectives neither by relying on the working of a thoroughly automatic gold standard nor by giving wide discretion to independent authorities, how else can we establish a monetary system that is stable and at the same time free from irresponsible governmental tinkering, a system that will provide the necessary monetary framework for a free enterprise economy yet be incapable of being used as a source of power to threaten economic and political freedom?</seg>
<seg id="678">The only way that has yet been suggested that offers promise is to try to achieve a government of law instead of men by legislating rules for the conduct of monetary policy that will have the effect of enabling the public to exercise control over monetary policy through its political authorities, while at the same time it will prevent monetary policy from being subject to the day-by-day whim of political authorities.</seg>
<seg id="679">The issue of legislating rules for monetary policy has much in common with a topic that seems at first altogether different, namely, the argument for the first amendment to the Constitution.</seg>
<seg id="680">Whenever anyone suggests the desirability of a legislative rule for control over money, the stereotyped answer is that it makes little sense to tie the monetary authority's hands in this way because the authority, if it wants to, can always do of its own volition what the rule would require it to do, and in addition has other alternatives, hence "surely," it is said, it can do better than the rule.</seg>
<seg id="681">An alternative version of the same argument applies it to the legislature.</seg>
<seg id="682">If the legislature is willing to adopt the rule, it is said, surely it will also be willing to legislate the "right" policy in each specific case.</seg>
<seg id="683">How then, it is said, does the adoption of the rule provide any protection against irresponsible political action?</seg>
<seg id="684">The same argument could apply with only minor verbal changes to the first amendment to the Constitution and, equally, to the entire Bill of Rights.</seg>
<seg id="685">Is it not absurd, one might say, to have a standard proscription of interference with free speech?</seg>
<seg id="686">Why not take up each case separately and treat it on its own merits?</seg>
<seg id="687">Is this not the counterpart to the usual argument in monetary policy that it is undesirable to bind the hands of the monetary authority in advance;</seg>
<seg id="688">that it should be left free to treat each case on its merits as it comes up?</seg>
<seg id="689">Why is not the argument equally valid for speech?</seg>
<seg id="690">One man wants to stand up on a street corner and advocate birth control;</seg>
<seg id="691">another, communism;</seg>
<seg id="692">a third, vegetarianism, and so on, ad infinitum.</seg>
<seg id="693">Why not enact a law affirming or denying to each the right to spread his particular views?</seg>
<seg id="694">Or, alternatively, why not give the power to decide the issue to an administrative agency?</seg>
<seg id="695">It is immediately clear that if we were to take each case up as it came, a majority would almost surely vote to deny free speech in most cases and perhaps even in every case taken separately.</seg>
<seg id="696">A vote on whether Mr. X should spread birth control propaganda would almost surely yield a majority saying no;</seg>
<seg id="697">and so would one on communism.</seg>
<seg id="698">The vegetarian might perhaps get by though even that is by no means a foregone conclusion.</seg>
<seg id="699">But now suppose all these cases were grouped together in one bundle, and the populace at large were asked to vote for them as a whole;</seg>
<seg id="700">to vote whether free speech should be denied in all cases or permitted in all alike.</seg>
<seg id="701">It is perfectly conceivable, and I would say, highly probable, that an overwhelming majority would vote for free speech;</seg>
<seg id="702">that, acting on the bundle as a whole, the people would vote exactly the opposite to the way they would have voted on each case separately.</seg>
<seg id="703">Why?</seg>
<seg id="704">One reason is that each person feels much more strongly about being deprived of his right to free speech when he is in a minority than he feels about depriving somebody else of the right to free speech when he is in the majority.</seg>
<seg id="705">In consequence, when he votes on the bundle as a whole, he gives much more weight to the infrequent denial of free speech to himself when he is in the minority than to the frequent denial of free speech to others.</seg>
<seg id="706">Another reason, and one that is more directly relevant to monetary policy, is that if the bundle is viewed as a whole, it becomes clear that the policy followed has cumulative effects that tend neither to be recognized nor taken into account when each case is voted on separately.</seg>
<seg id="707">When a vote is taken on whether Mr. Jones can speak on the corner, it cannot allow for the favorable effects of an announced general policy of free speech.</seg>
<seg id="708">It cannot allow for the fact that a society in which people are not free to speak on the corner without special legislation will be a society in which the development of new ideas, experimentation, change, and the like will all be hampered in a great variety of ways that are obvious to all, thanks to our good fortune of having lived in a society which did adopt the self-denying ordinance of not considering each case of speech separately.Exactly the same considerations apply in the monetary area.</seg>
<seg id="709">If each case is considered on its merits, the wrong decision is likely to be made in a large fraction of cases because the decision-makers are examining only a limited area and not taking into account the cumulative consequences of the policy as a whole.</seg>
<seg id="710">On the other hand, if a general rule is adopted for a group of cases as a bundle, the existence of that rule has favorable effects on people's attitudes and beliefs and expectations that would not follow even from the discretionary adoption of precisely the same policy on a scries of separate occasions.</seg>
<seg id="711">If a rule is to be legislated, what rule should it be?</seg>
<seg id="712">The rule that has most frequently been suggested by people of a generally liberal persuasion is a price level rule;</seg>
<seg id="713">namely, a legislative directive to the monetary authorities that they maintain a stable price level.</seg>
<seg id="714">I think this is the wrong kind of a rule.</seg>
<seg id="715">It is the wrong kind of a rule because it is in terms of objectives that the monetary authorities do not have the clear and direct power to achieve by their own actions.</seg>
<seg id="716">It consequently raises the problem of dispersing responsibilities and leaving the authorities too much leeway.</seg>
<seg id="717">There is unquestionably a close connection between monetary actions and the price level.</seg>
<seg id="718">But the connection is not so close, so invariable, or so direct that the objective of achieving a stable price level is an appropriate guide to the day-to-day activities of the authorities.</seg>
<seg id="719">The issue what rule to adopt is one that I have considered at some length elsewhere.</seg>
<seg id="720">Accordingly, I will limit myself here to stating my conclusion.</seg>
<seg id="721">In the present state of our knowledge, it seems to me desirable to state the rule in terms of the behavior of the stock of money.</seg>
<seg id="722">My choice at the moment would be a legislated rule instructing the monetary authority to achieve a specified rate of growth in the stock of money.</seg>
<seg id="723">For this purpose, I would define the stock of money as including currency outside commercial banks plus all deposits of commercial banks.</seg>
<seg id="724">I would specify that the Reserve System shall see to it that the total stock of money so defined rises month by month, and indeed, so far as possible, day by day, at an annual rate of X per cent, where X is some number between 3 and 5.</seg>
<seg id="725">The precise definition of money adopted, or the precise rate of growth chosen, makes far less difference than the definite choice of a particular definition and a particular rate of growth.</seg>
<seg id="726">As matters now stand, while this rule would drastically curtail the discretionary power of the monetary authorities, it would still leave an undesirable amount of discretion in the hands of Federal Reserve and Treasury authorities with respect to how to achieve the specified rate of growth in the money stock, debt management, banking supervision, and the like.</seg>
<seg id="727">Further banking and fiscal reforms, which I have spelled out in detail elsewhere, are both feasible and desirable.</seg>
<seg id="728">They would have the effect of eliminating present governmental intervention into lending and investing activity and of converting governmental financing operations from a perpetual source of instability and uncertainty into a reasonably regular and predictable activity.</seg>
<seg id="729">But, though important, these further reforms are far less basic than the adoption of a rule to limit the discretion of the monetary authorities with respect to the stock of money.</seg>
<seg id="730">I should like to emphasize that I do not regard my particular proposal as a be-all and end-all of monetary management, as a rule which is somehow to be written in tablets of stone and enshrined for all future time.</seg>
<seg id="731">It seems to me to be the rule that offers the greatest promise of achieving a reasonable degree of monetary stability in the light of our present knowledge.</seg>
<seg id="732">I would hope that as we operated with it, as we learned more about monetary matters, we might be able to devise still better rules, which would achieve still better results.</seg>
<seg id="733">Such a rule seems to me the only feasible device currently available for converting monetary policy into a pillar of a free society rather than a threat to its foundations.</seg>
<seg id="734">Chapter IVˇŞ International Financial and Trade ArrangementsThe problem of international monetary arrangements is the relation among different national currencies</seg>
<seg id="735">the terms and conditions under which individuals are able to convert U.S. dollars to pounds sterling, Canadian dollars to U.S. dollars, and so on.</seg>
<seg id="736">This problem is closely connected with the control of money discussed in the preceding chapter.</seg>
<seg id="737">It is connected also with governmental policies about international trade, since control over international trade is one technique for affecting international payments.</seg>
<seg id="738">The Importance of International Monetary Arrangements for Economic FreedomDespite its technical character and forbidding complexity, the subject of international monetary arrangements is one that a liberal cannot afford to neglect.</seg>
<seg id="739">It is not too much to say that the most serious short-run threat to economic freedom in the United States todayˇŞaside, of course, from the outbreak of World War IIIˇŞis that we shall be led to adopt far-reaching economic controls in order to ''solve" balance of payments problems.</seg>
<seg id="740">Interferences with international trade appear innocuous;</seg>
<seg id="741">they can get the support of people who are otherwise apprehensive of interference by government into economic affairs;</seg>
<seg id="742">many a business man even regards them as part of the "American Way of Life";</seg>
<seg id="743">yet there are few interferences which are capable of spreading so far and ultimately being so destructive of free enterprise.</seg>
<seg id="744">There is much experience to suggest that the most effective way to convert a market economy into an authoritarian economic society is to start by imposing direct controls on foreign exchange.</seg>
<seg id="745">This one step leads inevitably to the rationing of imports, to control over domestic production that uses imported products or that produces substitutes for imports, and so on in a never-ending spiral.</seg>
<seg id="746">Yet even so generally staunch a champion of free enterprise as Senator Barry Goldwater has at times been led, when discussing the so-called "gold flow," to suggest that restrictions on transactions in foreign exchange may be necessary as a "cure."</seg>
<seg id="747">This "cure" would be vastly worse than the disease.</seg>
<seg id="748">There is seldom anything truly new under the sun in economic policy, where the allegedly new generally turns out to be the discard of a prior century in flimsy disguise.</seg>
<seg id="749">Unless I am mistaken, however, full-fledged exchange controls and so-called "inconvertibility of currencies" are an exception and their origin reveals their authoritarian promise.</seg>
<seg id="750">To the best of my knowledge they were invented by Hjalmar Schacht in the early years of the Nazi regime.</seg>
<seg id="751">On many occasions in the past, of course, currencies have been described as inconvertible.</seg>
<seg id="752">But what the word then meant was that the government of the day was unwilling or unable to convert paper currency into gold or silver, or whatever the monetary commodity was, at the legally stipulated rate.</seg>
<seg id="753">It seldom meant that a country prohibited its citizens or residents from trading pieces of paper promising to pay specified sums in the monetary unit of that country for corresponding pieces of paper expressed in the monetary unit of another countryˇŞor for that matter for coin or bullion.</seg>
<seg id="754">During the Civil War in the United States and for a decade and a half thereafter, for example, U.S. currency was inconvertible in the sense that the holder of a greenback could not turn it in to the Treasury and get a fixed amount of gold for it.</seg>
<seg id="755">But throughout the period he was free to buy gold at the market price or to buy and sell British pounds for U.S. greenbacks at any price mutually agreeable to the two parties.</seg>
<seg id="756">In the United States, the dollar has been inconvertible in the older sense ever since 1933.</seg>
<seg id="757">It has been illegal for American citizens to hold gold or to buy and sell gold.</seg>
<seg id="758">The dollar has not been inconvertible in the newer sense.</seg>
<seg id="759">But unfortunately we seem to be adopting policies that are highly likely, sooner or later, to drive us in that direction.</seg>
<seg id="760">The Role of Gold in the U.S. Monetary SystemOnly a cultural lag leads us still to think of gold as the central element in our monetary system.</seg>
<seg id="761">A more accurate description of the role of gold in U.S. policy is that it is primarily a commodity whose price is supported, like wheat or other agricultural products.</seg>
<seg id="762">Our price-support program for gold differs in three important respects from our price-support program for wheat:</seg>
<seg id="763">first, we pay the support price to foreign as well as domestic producers;</seg>
<seg id="764">second, we sell freely at the support price only to foreign purchasers and not to domestic;</seg>
<seg id="765">third, and this is the one important relic of the monetary role of gold, the Treasury is authorized to create money to pay for gold it buys ˇŞto print paper money as it wereˇŞso that expenditures for the purchase of gold do not appear in the budget and the sums required need not be explicitly appropriated by Congress;</seg>
<seg id="766">similarly, when the Treasury sells gold, the books show simply a reduction in gold certificates, and not a receipt that enters into the budget.</seg>
<seg id="767">When the price of gold was first set at its present level of $ 35 an ounce in 1934, this price was well above the free market price of gold.</seg>
<seg id="768">In consequence, gold flooded the United States, our gold stock tripled in six years, and we came to hold well over half the world's gold stock.</seg>
<seg id="769">We accumulated a "surplus"of gold for the same reason we accumulated a "surplus" of wheatˇŞbecause the government offered to pay a higher price than the market price.</seg>
<seg id="770">More recently, the situation has changed.</seg>
<seg id="771">While the legally fixed price of gold has remained $ 35, prices of other goods have doubled or tripled.</seg>
<seg id="772">Hence $ 35 is now less than what the free market price would be.</seg>
<seg id="773">As a result, we now face a "shortage" rather than a "surplus" for precisely the same reason that rent ceilings inevitably produce a "shortage" of housingˇŞbecause the government is trying to hold the price of gold below the market price.</seg>
<seg id="774">The legal price of gold would long since have been raisedˇŞas wheat prices have been raised from time to timeˇŞexcept for the accident that the major producers of gold, and hence the major beneficiaries from a rise in its price, are Soviet Russia and South Africa, the two countries with whom the United States has least political sympathy.</seg>
<seg id="775">Governmental control of the price of gold, no less than the control of any other price, is inconsistent with a free economy.</seg>
<seg id="776">Such a pseudo gold standard must be distinguished sharply from the use of gold as money under a real gold standard which is entirely consistent with a free economy though it may not be feasible.</seg>
<seg id="777">Even more than the price fixing itself, the associated measures taken in 1933 and 1934 by the Roosevelt administration when it raised the price of gold represented a fundamental departure from liberal principles and established precedents that have returned to plague the free world.</seg>
<seg id="778">I refer to the nationalization of the gold stock, the prohibition of private possession of gold for monetary purposes, and the abrogation of gold clauses in public and private contracts.</seg>
<seg id="779">In 1933 and early 1934, private holders of gold were required by law to turn over their gold to the federal government.</seg>
<seg id="780">They were compensated at a price equal to the prior legal price, which was at the time decidedly below the market price.</seg>
<seg id="781">To make this requirement effective, private ownership of gold within the U.S. was made illegal except for use in the arts.</seg>
<seg id="782">One can hardly imagine a measure more destructive of the princi1 A warning is in order that this is a subtle point that depends on what is held constant in estimating the free market price, particularly with respect to gold's monetary role.ples of private property on which a free enterprise society rests.</seg>
<seg id="783">There is no difference in principle between this nationalization of gold at an artificially low price and Fidel Castro's nationalization of land and factories at an artificially low price.</seg>
<seg id="784">On what grounds of principle can the U.S. object to the one after having itself engaged in the other?</seg>
<seg id="785">Yet so great is the blindness of some supporters of free enterprise with respect to anything touching gold that in 1960 Henry Alexander, head of the Morgan Guaranty Trust Company, successor to J. P. Morgan and Company, proposed that the prohibition against the private ownership of gold by U.S. citizens be extended to cover gold held abroad!</seg>
<seg id="786">And his proposal was adopted by President Eisenhower with hardly a protest from the banking community.</seg>
<seg id="787">Though rationalized in terms of "conserving" gold for monetary use, prohibition of private ownership of gold was not enacted for any such monetary purpose, whether itself good or bad.</seg>
<seg id="788">The nationalization of gold was enacted to enable the government to reap the whole of the "paper" profit from the rise in the price of goldˇŞor perhaps, to prevent private individuals from benefiting.</seg>
<seg id="789">The abrogation of the gold clauses had a similar purpose.</seg>
<seg id="790">And this too was a measure destructive of the basic principles of free enterprise.</seg>
<seg id="791">Contracts entered into in good faith and with full knowledge on the part of both parties to them were declared invalid for the benefit of one of the parties!</seg>
<seg id="792">Current Payments and Capital FlightIn discussing international monetary relations on a more general level, it is necessary to distinguish two rather different problems:</seg>
<seg id="793">the balance of payments, and the danger of a run on gold.</seg>
<seg id="794">The difference between the problems can be illustrated most simply by considering the analogy of an ordinary commercial bank.</seg>
<seg id="795">The bank must so arrange its affairs that it takes in as service charges, interest on loans, and so on a large enough sum to enable it to pay its expensesˇŞwages and salaries, interest on borrowed funds, cost of supplies, returns to stockholders, and so on.</seg>
<seg id="796">It must strive, that is, for a healthy  income account.</seg>
<seg id="797">But a bank which is in good shape on its income account may nonetheless experience serious trouble if for any reason its depositors should lose confidence in it and suddenly demand their deposits en masse.</seg>
<seg id="798">Many a sound bank was forced to close its doors because of such a run on it during the liquidity crises described in the preceding chapter.</seg>
<seg id="799">These two problems are not of course unrelated.</seg>
<seg id="800">One important reason why a bank's depositors may lose confidence in it is because the bank is experiencing losses on income account.</seg>
<seg id="801">Yet the two problems are also very different.</seg>
<seg id="802">For one thing, problems on income account are generally slow to arise and considerable time is available to solve them.</seg>
<seg id="803">They seldom come as sudden surprises.</seg>
<seg id="804">A run, on the other hand, may arise suddenly and unpredictably out of thin air.</seg>
<seg id="805">The situation of the U.S. is precisely parallel.</seg>
<seg id="806">Residents of the United States and the U.S. government itself are seeking to buy foreign currencies with dollars in order to purchase goods and services in other countries, to invest in foreign enterprises, to pay interest on debts, to repay loans, or to give gifts to others, whether private or public.</seg>
<seg id="807">At the same time, foreigners are seeking to acquire dollars with foreign currencies for corresponding purposes.</seg>
<seg id="808">After the event, the number of dollars spent for foreign currency will precisely equal the number of dollars purchased with foreign currencyˇŞjust as the number of pairs of shoes sold is precisely equal to the number bought.</seg>
<seg id="809">Arithmetic is arithmetic and one man's purchase is another man's sale.</seg>
<seg id="810">But there is nothing to assure that, at any given price of foreign currency in terms of dollars, the number of dollars that some want to spend will equal the number others want to buyˇŞjust as there is nothing to assure that at any given price of shoes the number of pairs of shoes people want to buy is exactly equal to the number of pairs other people want to sell.</seg>
<seg id="811">The ex post equality reflects some mechanism that eliminates any ex ante discrepancy.</seg>
<seg id="812">The problem of achieving an appropriate mechanism for this purpose is the counterpart of the bank's problem on income account.</seg>
<seg id="813">In addition, the United States has a problem like the bank's of avoiding a run.</seg>
<seg id="814">The U.S. is committed to sell gold to foreign central banks and governments at $ 35 an ounce.</seg>
<seg id="815">Foreign central banks, governments, and residents hold large funds in the United States in the form of deposit accounts or U.S. securities that can be readily sold for dollars.</seg>
<seg id="816">At any time, the holders of these balances can start a run on the U.S. Treasury by trying to convert their dollar balances into gold.</seg>
<seg id="817">This is precisely what happened in the fall of 1960, and what is very likely to happen again at some unpredictable date in the future (perhaps before this is printed).</seg>
<seg id="818">The two problems are related in two ways.</seg>
<seg id="819">In the first place, as for a bank, income account difficulties are a major source of loss of confidence in the ability of the U.S. to honor its promise to sell gold at $ 35 an ounce.</seg>
<seg id="820">The fact that the U.S. has in effect been having to borrow abroad in order to achieve balance on current account is a major reason why holders of dollars are interested in converting them into gold or other currencies.</seg>
<seg id="821">In the second place, the fixed price of gold is the device we have adopted for pegging another set of pricesˇŞthe price of the dollar in terms of foreign currenciesˇŞand flows of gold are the device we have adopted for resolving ex ante discrepancies in the balance of payments.</seg>
<seg id="822">Alternative Mechanisms for Achieving Balance in Foreign Payments</seg>
<seg id="823">We can get more light on both of these relations by considering what alternative mechanisms are available for achieving balance in paymentsˇŞthe first and in many ways the more fundamental of the two problems.</seg>
<seg id="824">Suppose that the U.S. is roughly in balance in its international payments and that something comes along which alters the situation by, let us say, reducing the number of dollars that foreigners want to buy compared to the number that U.S. residents want to sell;</seg>
<seg id="825">or, looking at it from the other side, increasing the amount of foreign currency that holders of dollars want to buy compared to the amount that holders of foreign currency want to sell for dollars.</seg>
<seg id="826">That is, something threatens to produce a "deficit" in U.S. payments.</seg>
<seg id="827">This might result from increased efficiency in production abroad or decreased efficiency at home, increased foreign aid expenditures by the U.S. or reduced ones by other countries, or a million and one other changes of the kind that are always occurring.</seg>
<seg id="828">There are four, and only four ways, in which a country can adjust to such a disturbance and some combination of these ways must be used.</seg>
<seg id="829">1.U.S. reserves of foreign currencies can be drawn down or foreign reserves of U.S. currency built up.</seg>
<seg id="830">In practice, this means that the U.S. government can let its stock of gold go down, since gold is exchangeable for foreign currencies, or it can borrow foreign currencies and make them available for dollars at official exchange rates;</seg>
<seg id="831">or foreign governments can accumulate dollars by selling U.S. residents foreign currencies at official rates.</seg>
<seg id="832">Reliance on reserves is obviously at best a temporary expedient.</seg>
<seg id="833">Indeed, it is precisely the extensive use by the U.S. of this expedient that accounts for the great concern with the balance of payments.</seg>
<seg id="834">2.Domestic prices within the U.S. can be forced down relative to foreign prices.</seg>
<seg id="835">This is the main adjustment mechanism under a full-fledged gold standard.</seg>
<seg id="836">An initial deficit would produce an outflow of gold (mechanism 1, above);</seg>
<seg id="837">the outflow of gold would produce a decline in the stock of money;</seg>
<seg id="838">the decline in the stock of money would produce a fall in prices and incomes at home.</seg>
<seg id="839">At the same time, the reverse effects would occur abroad:</seg>
<seg id="840">the inflow of gold would expand the stock of money and thereby raise prices and income.</seg>
<seg id="841">Lowered U.S. prices and increased foreign prices would make U.S. goods more attractive to foreigners and thereby raise the number of dollars they wanted to buy;</seg>
<seg id="842">at the same time, the price changes would make foreign goods less attractive to U.S. residents and thereby lower the number of dollars they wanted to sell.</seg>
<seg id="843">Both effects would operate to reduce the deficit and restore balance without the necessity for further gold flows.</seg>
<seg id="844">Under the modern managed standard, these effects are not automatic.</seg>
<seg id="845">Gold flows may still occur as the first step, but they will not affect the stock of money in either the country that loses, or the country that gains gold, unless the monetary authorities in the separate countries decide that they should.</seg>
<seg id="846">In every country today, the central bank or the Treasury has the power to offset the influence of gold flows, or to change the stock of money without gold flows.</seg>
<seg id="847">Hence this mechanism will be used only if the authorities in the country experiencing the deficit are willing to produce a deflation, thereby creating unemployment, in order to resolve its payments problem, or the authorities in the country experiencing the surplus are willing to produce an inflation.</seg>
<seg id="848">3.Exactly the same effects can be achieved by a change in exchange rates as by a change in domestic prices.</seg>
<seg id="849">For example, suppose that under mechanism 2 the price of a particular car in the United States fell by 10 per cent from $ 2,800 to $ 2,520.</seg>
<seg id="850">If the price of the pound is throughout $ 2.80, this means that the price in Britain (neglecting freight and other charges) would fall from ˇę1, 000 to ˇę900.</seg>
<seg id="851">Exactly the same decline in the British price will occur without any change in the United States price if the price of a pound rises from $ 2.80 to $ 3.11.</seg>
<seg id="852">Formerly, the Englishman had to spend ˇę1,000 to get $ 2,800.</seg>
<seg id="853">Now he can get $ 2,800 for only ˇę900.</seg>
<seg id="854">He would not know the difference between this reduction in cost and the corresponding reduction through a fall in the U.S. price without a change in exchange rate.</seg>
<seg id="855">In practice, there are several ways in which the change in exchange rates can occur.</seg>
<seg id="856">With the kinds of pegged exchange rates many countries now have, it can occur through devaluation or appreciation, which is to say, a governmental declaration that it is changing the price at which it proposes to peg its currency.</seg>
<seg id="857">Alternatively, the exchange rate docs not need to be pegged at all.</seg>
<seg id="858">It can be a market rate changing from day to day, as was the case with the Canadian dollar from 1950 to 1962.</seg>
<seg id="859">If a market rate, it can be a truly free market rate determined primarily by private transactions as the Canadian rate apparently was from 1952 to 1961, or it can be manipulated by government speculation as was the situation in Britain from 1931 to 1939, and in Canada from 1950 to 1952 and again from 1961 to 1962.</seg>
<seg id="860">Of these various techniques, only the freely floating exchange rate is fully automatic and free from governmental control.</seg>
<seg id="861">4.The adjustments produced by mechanisms 2 and 3 consist of changes in flows of commodities and services induced by changes either in internal prices or exchange rates.</seg>
<seg id="862">Instead, direct governmental controls or interferences with trade could be used to reduce attempted U.S. expenditures of dollars and expand U.S. receipts.</seg>
<seg id="863">Tariffs could be raised to choke off imports, subsidies could be given to stimulate exports, import quotas could be imposed on a variety of goods, capital investment abroad by U.S. citizens or firms could be controlled, and so on and on up to the whole paraphernalia of exchange controls.</seg>
<seg id="864">In this category must be included not only controls over private activities but also changes in governmental programs for balance of payments purposes.</seg>
<seg id="865">Recipients of foreign aid may be required to spend the aid in the U.S.;</seg>
<seg id="866">the military may procure goods in the United States at greater expense instead of abroad in order to save "dollars"ˇŞin the self-contradictory terminology usedˇŞ and so on in bewildering array.</seg>
<seg id="867">The important thing to note is that one or another of these four ways will and must be used.</seg>
<seg id="868">Double entry books must balance.</seg>
<seg id="869">Payments must equal receipts.</seg>
<seg id="870">The only question is how.</seg>
<seg id="871">Our announced national policy has been and continues to be that we shall do none of these things.</seg>
<seg id="872">In a speech in December 1961, to the National Association of Manufacturers, President Kennedy stated "This administration, therefore, during its term of officeˇŞand I repeat this and make it as a flat statementˇŞhas no intention of imposing exchange controls, devaluing the dollar, raising trade barriers or choking off our economic recovery."</seg>
<seg id="873">As a matter of logic, this leaves only two possibilities:</seg>
<seg id="874">getting other countries to take the relevant measures, which is hardly a recourse that we can be sure of, or drawing down reserves, which the President and other officials repeatedly stated must not be permitted to continue.</seg>
<seg id="875">Yet Time magazine reports that the President's "promise drew a burst of applause" from the assembled businessmen.</seg>
<seg id="876">So far as our announced policy is concerned, we are in the position of a man living beyond his income who insists that he cannot possibly earn more, or spend less, or borrow, or finance the excess out of his assets!</seg>
<seg id="877">Because we have been unwilling to adopt any one coherent policy, we and our trading partnersˇŞ who make the same ostrich-like pronouncements as we doˇŞhave perforce been led to resort to all four mechanisms.</seg>
<seg id="878">In the early postwar years, U.S. reserves rose;</seg>
<seg id="879">more recently they have been declining.</seg>
<seg id="880">We welcomed inflation more readily than we otherwise would have when reserves were rising, and we have been more deflationary since 1958 than we would otherwise have been because of the drain of gold.</seg>
<seg id="881">Though we have not changed our official price of gold, our trading partners have changed theirs, and thereby the exchange rate between their currency and the dollar, and U.S. pressure has not been absent in producing these adjustments.</seg>
<seg id="882">Finally, our trading partners used direct controls extensively and, since we instead of they have been faced with deficits, we too have resorted to a wide range of direct interferences with payments, from reducing the amount of foreign goods that tourists can bring in free of dutyˇŞa trivial yet highly symptomatic stepˇŞto requiring foreign aid expenditures to be spent in the U.S., to keeping families from joining servicemen overseas, to more stringent import quotas on oil.</seg>
<seg id="883">We have been led also to engage in the demeaning step of asking foreign governments to take special measures to strengthen the U.S. balance of payments.</seg>
<seg id="884">Of the four mechanisms, the use of direct controls is clearly the worst from almost any point of view and certainly the most destructive of a free society.</seg>
<seg id="885">Yet in lieu of any clear policy, we have been led increasingly to rely on such controls in one form or another.</seg>
<seg id="886">We preach publicly the virtues of free trade;</seg>
<seg id="887">yet we have been forced by the inexorable pressure of the balance of payments to move in the opposite direction and there is great danger that we shall move still farther.</seg>
<seg id="888">We can pass all the laws imaginable to reduce tariffs;</seg>
<seg id="889">the Administration may negotiate any number of tariff reductions;</seg>
<seg id="890">yet unless we adopt an alternative mechanism for resolving balance of payments deficits, we shall be led to substitute one set of trade impediments for anotherˇŞindeed, to substitute a worse set for a better.</seg>
<seg id="891">While tariffs are bad, quotas and other direct interferences are even worse.</seg>
<seg id="892">A tariff, like a market price, is impersonal and does not involve direct interference by government in business affairs; a quota is likely to involve allocation and other administrative interferences, besides giving administrators valuable plums to pass out to private interests.</seg>
<seg id="893">Perhaps worse than either tariffs or quotas are extra-legal arrangements, such as the ''voluntary" agreement by Japan to restrict textile exports.</seg>
<seg id="894">Floating Exchange Rates as the Free Market Solution</seg>
<seg id="895">There are only two mechanisms that are consistent with a free market and free trade.</seg>
<seg id="896">One is a fully automatic international gold standard.</seg>
<seg id="897">This, as we saw in the preceding chapter, is neither feasible nor desirable.</seg>
<seg id="898">In any event, we cannot adopt it by ourselves.</seg>
<seg id="899">The other is a system of freely floating exchange rates determined in the market by private transactions without governmental intervention.</seg>
<seg id="900">This is the proper free market counterpart to the monetary rule advocated in the preceding chapter.</seg>
<seg id="901">If we do not adopt it, we shall inevitably fail to expand the area of free trade and shall sooner or later be induced to impose widespread direct controls over trade.</seg>
<seg id="902">In this area, as in others, conditions can and do change unexpectedly.</seg>
<seg id="903">It may well be that we shall muddle through the difficulties that are facing us as this is written (April, 1962) and indeed that we may find ourselves in a surplus rather than deficit position, accumulating reserves rather than losing them.</seg>
<seg id="904">If so, this will only mean that other countries will be faced with the necessity of imposing controls.</seg>
<seg id="905">When, in 1950, I wrote an article proposing a system of floating exchange rates, it was in the context of European payments difficulties accompanying the then alleged "dollar shortage."</seg>
<seg id="906">Such a turnabout is always possible.</seg>
<seg id="907">Indeed, it is the very difficulty of predicting when and how such changes occur that is the basic argument for a free market.</seg>
<seg id="908">Our problem is not to "solve" a balance of payments problem.</seg>
<seg id="909">It is to solve the balance of payments problem by adopting a mechanism that will enable free market forces to provide a prompt, effective, and automatic response to changes in conditions affecting international trade.</seg>
<seg id="910">Though freely floating exchange rates seem so clearly to be the appropriate free-market mechanism, they are strongly supported only by a fairly small number of liberals, mostly professional economists, and are opposed by many liberals who reject governmental intervention and governmental price fixing in almost every other area.</seg>
<seg id="911">Why is this so?</seg>
<seg id="912">One reason is simply the tyranny of the status quo.</seg>
<seg id="913">A second reason is the confusion between a real gold standard and a pseudo gold standard.</seg>
<seg id="914">Under a real gold standard, the prices of different national currencies in terms of one another would be very nearly rigid since the different currencies would simply be different names for different amounts of gold.</seg>
<seg id="915">It is easy to make the mistake of supposing that we can get the substance of the real gold standard by the mere adoption of the form of a nominal obeisance to goldˇŞthe adoption of a pseudo gold standard under which the prices of different national currencies in terms of one another are rigid only because they are pegged prices in rigged markets.</seg>
<seg id="916">A third reason is the inevitable tendency for everyone to be in favor of a free market for everyone else, while regarding himself as deserving of special treatment.</seg>
<seg id="917">This particularly affects bankers in respect of exchange rates.</seg>
<seg id="918">They like to have a guaranteed price.</seg>
<seg id="919">Moreover, they are not familiar with the market devices that would arise to cope with fluctuations in exchange rates.</seg>
<seg id="920">The firms that would specialize in speculation and arbitrage in a free market for exchange do not exist.</seg>
<seg id="921">This is one way the tyranny of the status quo is enforced.</seg>
<seg id="922">In Canada, for example, some bankers, after a decade of a free rate which gave them a different status quo, were in the forefront of those favoring its continuation and objecting to either a pegged rate or government manipulation of the rate.</seg>
<seg id="923">More important than any of these reasons, I believe, is a mistaken interpretation of experience with floating rates, arising out of a statistical fallacy that can be seen easily in a standard example.</seg>
<seg id="924">Arizona is clearly the worst place in the U.S. for a person with tuberculosis to go because the death rate from tuberculosis is higher in Arizona than in any other state.</seg>
<seg id="925">The fallacy is in this case obvious.</seg>
<seg id="926">It is less obvious in connection with exchange rates.</seg>
<seg id="927">When countries have gotten into severe financial difficulties through internal monetary mismanagement or for any other reason, they have had ultimately to resort to flexible exchange rates.</seg>
<seg id="928">No amount of exchange control or direct restrictions on trade enabled them to peg an exchange rate that was far out of line with economic realities.</seg>
<seg id="929">In consequence, it is unquestionably true that floating exchange rates have frequently been associated with financial and economic instabilityˇŞas, for example, in hyperinflations, or severe but not hyperinflations such as have occurred in many South American countries.</seg>
<seg id="930">It is easy to conclude, as many have, that floating exchange rates produce such instability.</seg>
<seg id="931">Being in favor of floating exchange rates does not mean being in favor of unstable exchange rates.</seg>
<seg id="932">When we support a free price system at home, this does not imply that we favor a system in which prices fluctuate wildly up and down.</seg>
<seg id="933">What we want is a system in which prices are free to fluctuate but in which the forces determining them are sufficiently stable so that in fact prices move within moderate ranges.</seg>
<seg id="934">This is equally true of a system of floating exchange rates.</seg>
<seg id="935">The ultimate objective is a world in which exchange rates, while free to vary, are, in fact, highly stable because basic economic policies and conditions are stable.</seg>
<seg id="936">Instability of exchange rates is a symptom of instability in the underlying economic structure.</seg>
<seg id="937">Elimination of this symptom by administrative freezing of exchange rates cures none of the underlying difficulties and only makes adjustments to them more painful.</seg>
<seg id="938">The Policy Measures Necessary for a Free Market in Gold and Foreign Exchange</seg>
<seg id="939">It may help bring out in concrete terms the implications of this discussion if I specify in detail the measures that I believe the U.S. should take to promote a truly free market in both gold and foreign exchange.</seg>
<seg id="940">1.The U.S. should announce that it no longer commits itself to buy or sell gold at any fixed price.</seg>
<seg id="941">2.Present laws making it illegal for individuals to own gold or to buy and sell gold should be repealed, so that there are no restrictions on the price at which gold can be bought or sold in terms of any other commodity or financial instrument, including national currencies.</seg>
<seg id="942">3.The present law specifying that the Reserve System must hold gold certificates equal to 25 per cent of its liabilities should be repealed.</seg>
<seg id="943">4.A major problem in getting rid completely of the gold price-support program, as of the wheat price-support program, is the transitional one of what to do with accumulated government stocks.</seg>
<seg id="944">In both cases, my own view is that the government should immediately restore a free market by instituting steps 1 and 2, and should ultimately dispose of all of its stocks.</seg>
<seg id="945">However, it would probably be desirable for the government to dispose of its stocks only gradually.</seg>
<seg id="946">For wheat, five years has always seemed to me a long enough period, so I have favored the government committing itself to dispose of one-fifth of its stocks in each of five years.</seg>
<seg id="947">This period seems reasonably satisfactory for gold as well.</seg>
<seg id="948">Hence, I propose that the government auction off its gold stocks on the free market over a five-year period.</seg>
<seg id="949">With a free gold market, individuals may well find warehouse certificates for gold more useful than actual gold.</seg>
<seg id="950">But if so, private enterprise can certainly provide the service of storing the gold and issuing certificates.</seg>
<seg id="951">Why should gold storage and the issuance of warehouse certificates be a nationalized industry?</seg>
<seg id="952">5. The U.S. should announce also that it will not proclaim any official exchange rates between the dollar and other currencies and in addition that it will not engage in any speculative or other activities aimed at influencing exchange rates.</seg>
<seg id="953">These would then be determined in free markets.</seg>
<seg id="954">6.These measures would conflict with our formal obligation as a member of the International Monetary Fund to specify an official parity for the dollar.</seg>
<seg id="955">However, the Fund found it possible to reconcile Canada's failure to specify a parity with its Articles and to give its approval to a floating rate for Canada.</seg>
<seg id="956">There is no reason why it cannot do the same for the U.S.</seg>
<seg id="957">7.Other nations might choose to peg their currencies to the dollar.</seg>
<seg id="958">That is their business and there is no reason for us to object so long as we undertake no obligations to buy or sell their currency at a fixed price.</seg>
<seg id="959">They will be able to succeed in pegging their currency to ours only by one or more of the measures listed earlierˇŞdrawing on or accumulating reserves, co-ordinating their internal policy with U.S. policy, tightening or loosening direct controls on trade.</seg>
<seg id="960">Eliminating U.S. Restrictions on TradeA system such as that just outlined would solve the balance of payments problem once and for all.</seg>
<seg id="961">No deficit could possibly arise to require high government officials to plead with foreign countries and central banks for assistance, or to require an American President to behave like a harried country banker trying to restore confidence in his bank, or to force an administration preaching free trade to impose import restrictions, or to sacrifice important national and personal interests to the trivial question of the name of the currency in which payments are made.</seg>
<seg id="962">Payments would always balance because a priceˇŞthe foreign exchange rateˇŞwould be free to produce a balance.</seg>
<seg id="963">No one could sell dollars unless he could find someone to buy them and conversely.</seg>
<seg id="964">A system of floating exchange rates would therefore enable us to proceed effectively and directly toward complete free trade in goods and servicesˇŞbarring only such deliberate interference as may be justified on strictly political and military grounds;</seg>
<seg id="965">for example, banning the sale of strategic goods to communist countries.</seg>
<seg id="966">So long as we are firmly committed to the strait jacket of fixed exchange rates, we cannot move definitively to free trade.</seg>
<seg id="967">The possibility of tariffs or direct controls must be retained as an escape valve in case of necessity.</seg>
<seg id="968">A system of floating exchange rates has the side advantage that it makes almost transparently obvious the fallacy in the most popular argument against free trade, the argument that "low" wages elsewhere make tariffs somehow necessary to protect "high" wages here.</seg>
<seg id="969">Is 100 yen an hour to a Japanese worker high or low compared with $ 4 an hour to an American worker?</seg>
<seg id="970">That all depends on the exchange rate.</seg>
<seg id="971">What determines the exchange rate?</seg>
<seg id="972">The necessity of making payments balance;</seg>
<seg id="973">i.e., of making the amount we can sell to the Japanese roughly equal to the amount they can sell to us.</seg>
<seg id="974">Suppose for simplicity that Japan and the U.S. are the only two countries involved in trade and that at some exchange rate, say 1,000 yen to the dollar, Japanese could produce every single item capable of entering into foreign trade more cheaply than the U.S.</seg>
<seg id="975">At that exchange rate the Japanese could sell much to us, we, nothing to them.</seg>
<seg id="976">Suppose we pay them in paper dollars.</seg>
<seg id="977">What would the Japanese exporters do with the dollars?</seg>
<seg id="978">They cannot eat them, wear them, or live in them.</seg>
<seg id="979">If they were willing simply to hold them, then the printing industryˇŞprinting the dollar billsˇŞwould be a magnificent export industry.</seg>
<seg id="980">Its output would enable us all to have the good things of life provided nearly free by the Japanese.</seg>
<seg id="981">But, of course, Japanese exporters would not want to hold the dollars.</seg>
<seg id="982">They would want to sell them for yen.</seg>
<seg id="983">By assumption, there is nothing they can buy for a dollar that they cannot buy for less than the 1,000 yen that a dollar will by assumption exchange for.</seg>
<seg id="984">This is equally true for other Japanese.</seg>
<seg id="985">Why then would any holder of yen give up 1,000 yen for a dollar that will buy less in goods than the 1,000 yen will?</seg>
<seg id="986">No one would.</seg>
<seg id="987">In order for the Japanese exporter to exchange his dollars for yen, he would have to offer to take fewer yenˇŞthe price of the dollar in terms of the yen would have to be less than 1, 000, or of the yen in terms of the dollar more than I mill.</seg>
<seg id="988">But at 500 yen to the dollar Japanese goods are twice as expensive to Americans as before;</seg>
