<seg id="989">American goods half as expensive to the Japanese.</seg>
<seg id="990">The Japanese will no longer be able to undersell American producers on all items.</seg>
<seg id="991">Where will the price of the yen in terms of dollars settle?</seg>
<seg id="992">At whatever level is necessary to assure that all exporters who desire to do so can sell the dollars they get for the goods they export to America to importers who use them to buy goods in America.</seg>
<seg id="993">To speak loosely, at whatever level is necessary to assure that the value of U.S. exports (in dollars) is equal to the value of U.S. imports (again in dollars).</seg>
<seg id="994">Loosely, because a precise statement would have to take into account capital transactions, gifts, and so on.</seg>
<seg id="995">But these do not alter the central principle.</seg>
<seg id="996">It will be noted that this discussion says nothing about the level of living of the Japanese worker or the American worker.</seg>
<seg id="997">These are irrelevant.</seg>
<seg id="998">If the Japanese worker has a lower standard of living than the American, it is because he is less productive on the average than the American, given the training he has, the amount of capital and land and so on that he has to work with.</seg>
<seg id="999">If the American worker is, let us say, on the average four times as productive as the Japanese worker, it is wasteful to use him to produce any goods in the production of which he is less than four times as productive.</seg>
<seg id="1000">It is better to produce those goods at which he is more productive and trade them for the goods at which he is less productive.</seg>
<seg id="1001">Tariffs do not assist the Japanese worker to raise his standard of living or protect the high standard of the American worker.</seg>
<seg id="1002">On the contrary, they lower the Japanese standard and keep the American standard from being as high as it could be.</seg>
<seg id="1003">Given that we should move to free trade, how should we do so?</seg>
<seg id="1004">The method that we have tried to adopt is reciprocal negotiation of tariff reductions with other countries.</seg>
<seg id="1005">This seems to me a wrong procedure.</seg>
<seg id="1006">In the first place, it ensures a slow pace.</seg>
<seg id="1007">He moves fastest who moves alone.</seg>
<seg id="1008">In the second place, it fosters an erroneous view of the basic problem.</seg>
<seg id="1009">It makes it appear as if tariffs help the country imposing them but hurt other countries, as if when we reduce a tariff we give up something good and should get something in return in the form of a reduction in the tariffs imposed by other countries.</seg>
<seg id="1010">In truth, the situation is quite different.</seg>
<seg id="1011">Our tariffs hurt us as well as other countries.</seg>
<seg id="1012">We would be benefited by dispensing with our tariffs even if other countries did not.</seg>
<seg id="1013">We would of course be benefited even more if they reduced theirs but our benefiting does not require that they reduce theirs.</seg>
<seg id="1014">Self interests coincide and do not conflict.</seg>
<seg id="1015">I believe that it would be far better for us to move to free trade unilaterally, as Britain did in the nineteenth century when it repealed the corn laws.</seg>
<seg id="1016">We, as they did, would experience an enormous accession of political and economic power.</seg>
<seg id="1017">We are a great nation and it ill behooves us to require reciprocal benefits from Luxembourg before we reduce a tariff on Luxembourg products, or to throw thousands of Chinese refugees suddenly out of work by imposing import quotas on textiles from Hong Kong.</seg>
<seg id="1018">Let us live up to our destiny and set the pace not be reluctant followers.</seg>
<seg id="1019">I have spoken in terms of tariffs for simplicity but, as already noted, non-tariff restrictions may now be more serious</seg>
<seg id="1020">There are conceivable exceptions to these statements but, so far as I can see, they are theoretical curiosities, not relevant practical possibilities.impediments to trade than tariffs.</seg>
<seg id="1021">We should remove both.</seg>
<seg id="1022">A prompt yet gradual program would be to legislate that all import quotas or other quantitative restrictions, whether imposed by us or "voluntarily" accepted by other countries, be raised 20 per cent a year until they are so high that they become irrelevant and can be abandoned, and that all tariffs be reduced by one-tenth of the present level in each of the next ten years.</seg>
<seg id="1023">There are few measures we could take that would do more to promote the cause of freedom at home and abroad.</seg>
<seg id="1024">Instead of making grants to foreign governments in the name of economic aidˇŞand thereby promoting socialismˇŞwhile at the same time imposing restrictions on the products they succeed in producingˇŞand thereby hindering free enterpriseˇŞwe could assume a consistent and principled stance.</seg>
<seg id="1025">We could say to the rest of the world:</seg>
<seg id="1026">We believe in freedom and intend to practice it.</seg>
<seg id="1027">No one can force you to be free.</seg>
<seg id="1028">That is your business.</seg>
<seg id="1029">But we can offer you full cooperation on equal terms to all.</seg>
<seg id="1030">Our market is open to you.</seg>
<seg id="1031">Sell here what you can and wish to.</seg>
<seg id="1032">Use the proceeds to buy what you wish.</seg>
<seg id="1033">In this way co-operation among individuals can be world wide yet free.</seg>
<seg id="1034">Chapter VˇŞ Fiscal Policy</seg>
<seg id="1035">Ever since the new deal, a primary excuse for the expansion of governmental activity at the federal level has been the supposed necessity for government spending to eliminate unemployment.</seg>
<seg id="1036">The excuse has gone through several stages.</seg>
<seg id="1037">At first, government spending was needed to "prime the pump."</seg>
<seg id="1038">Temporary expenditures would set the economy going and the government could then step out of the picture.</seg>
<seg id="1039">When the initial expenditures failed to eliminate unemployment and were followed by a sharp economic contraction in 1937¨C38, the theory of "secular stagnation" developed to justify a permanently high level of government spending.</seg>
<seg id="1040">The economy had become mature, it was argued.</seg>
<seg id="1041">Opportunities for investment had been largely exploited and no substantial new opportunities were likely to arise.</seg>
<seg id="1042">Yet individuals would still want to save.</seg>
<seg id="1043">Hence, it was essential for government to spend and run a perpetual deficit.</seg>
<seg id="1044">The securities issued to finance the deficit would provide individuals with a way to accumulate savings while the government expenditures provided employment.</seg>
<seg id="1045">This view has been thoroughly discredited by theoretical analysis and even more by actual experience, including the emergence of wholly new lines for private investment not dreamed of by the secular stagnationists.</seg>
<seg id="1046">Yet it has left its heritage.</seg>
<seg id="1047">The idea may be accepted by none, but the government programs undertaken in its name, like some of those intended to prime the pump, are still with us and indeed account for ever-growing government expenditures.</seg>
<seg id="1048">More recently, the emphasis has been on government expenditures neither to prime the pump nor to hold in check the specter of secular stagnation but as a balance wheel.</seg>
<seg id="1049">When private expenditures decline for any reason, it is said, governmental expenditures should rise to keep total expenditures stable;</seg>
<seg id="1050">conversely, when private expenditures rise, governmental expenditures should decline.</seg>
<seg id="1051">Unfortunately, the balance wheel is unbalanced.</seg>
<seg id="1052">Each recession, however minor, sends a shudder through politically sensitive legislators and administrators with their ever present fear that perhaps it is the harbinger of another 1929¨C33.</seg>
<seg id="1053">They hasten to enact federal spending programs of one kind or another.</seg>
<seg id="1054">Many of the programs do not in fact come into effect until after the recession has passed.</seg>
<seg id="1055">Hence, insofar as they do affect total expenditures, on which I shall have more to say later, they tend to exacerbate the succeeding expansion rather than to mitigate the recession.</seg>
<seg id="1056">The haste with which spending programs are approved is not matched by an equal haste to repeal them or to eliminate others when the recession is passed and expansion is under way.</seg>
<seg id="1057">On the contrary, it is then argued that a "healthy" expansion must not be "jeopardized" by cuts in governmental expenditures.</seg>
<seg id="1058">The chief harm done by the balance-wheel theory is therefore not that it has failed to offset recessions, which it has, and not that it has introduced an inflationary bias into governmental policy, which it has done too, but that it has continuously fostered an expansion in the range of governmental activities at the federal level and prevented a reduction in the burden of federal taxes.</seg>
<seg id="1059">In view of the emphasis on using the federal budget as a balance wheel, it is ironic that the most unstable component of national income in the postwar period is federal expenditure, and the instability has not at all been in a direction to offset movements of other expenditure components.</seg>
<seg id="1060">Far from being a balance wheel offsetting other forces making for fluctuations, the federal budget has if anything been itself a major source of disturbance and instability.</seg>
<seg id="1061">Because its expenditures are now so large a part of the total for the economy as a whole, the federal government cannot avoid having significant effects on the economy.</seg>
<seg id="1062">The first requisite is therefore that the government mend its own fences, that it adopt procedures that will lead to reasonable stability in its own flow of expenditures.</seg>
<seg id="1063">If it would do that, it would make a clear contribution to reducing the adjustments required in the rest of the economy.</seg>
<seg id="1064">Until it does that, it is farcical for government officials to adopt the self-righteous tones of the schoolmaster keeping unruly pupils in line.</seg>
<seg id="1065">Of course, their doing so is not surprising.</seg>
<seg id="1066">Passing the buck and blaming others for one's own deficiencies are not vices of which governmental officials have a monopoly.</seg>
<seg id="1067">Even if one were to accept the view that the federal budget should be and can be used as a balance wheelˇŞa view I shall consider in more detail belowˇŞthere is no necessity to use the expenditure side of the budget for this purpose.</seg>
<seg id="1068">The tax side is equally available.</seg>
<seg id="1069">A decline in national income automatically reduces the tax revenue of the federal government in greater proportion and thus shifts the budget in the direction of a deficit, and conversely during a boom.</seg>
<seg id="1070">If it is desired to do more, taxes can be lowered during recessions and raised during expansions.</seg>
<seg id="1071">Of course, politics might well enforce an asymmetry here too, making the declines politically more palatable than the rises.</seg>
<seg id="1072">If the balance-wheel theory has in practice been applied on the expenditure side, it has been because of the existence of other forces making for increased governmental expenditures;</seg>
<seg id="1073">in particular, the widespread acceptance by intellectuals of the belief that government should play a larger role in economic and private affairs;</seg>
<seg id="1074">the triumph, that is, of the philosophy of the welfare state.</seg>
<seg id="1075">This philosophy has found a useful ally in the balance-wheel theory;</seg>
<seg id="1076">it has enabled governmental intervention to proceed at a faster pace than would otherwise have been possible.</seg>
<seg id="1077">How different matters might now be if the balance-wheel theory had been applied on the tax side instead of the expenditure side.</seg>
<seg id="1078">Suppose each recession had seen a cut in taxes and suppose the political unpopularity of raising taxes in the succeeding expansion had led to resistance to newly proposed governmental expenditure programs and to curtailment of existing ones.</seg>
<seg id="1079">We might now be in a position where federal expenditures would be absorbing a good deal less of a national income that would be larger because of the reduction in the depressing and inhibiting effects of taxes.</seg>
<seg id="1080">I hasten to add that this dream is not intended to indicate support for the balance-wheel theory.</seg>
<seg id="1081">In practice, even if the effects would be in the direction expected under the balance wheel theory, they would be delayed in time and spread.</seg>
<seg id="1082">To make them an effective offset to other forces making for fluctuations, we would have to be able to forecast those fluctuations a long time in advance.</seg>
<seg id="1083">In fiscal policy as in monetary policy, all political considerations aside, we simply do not know enough to be able to use deliberate changes in taxation or expenditures as a sensitive stabilizing mechanism.</seg>
<seg id="1084">In the process of trying to do so, we almost surely make matters worse.</seg>
<seg id="1085">We make matters worse not by being consistently perverseˇŞthat would be easily cured by simply doing the opposite of what seemed at first the thing to do.</seg>
<seg id="1086">We make matters worse by introducing a largely random disturbance that is simply added to other disturbances.</seg>
<seg id="1087">That is what we seem in fact to have done in the pastˇŞin addition, of course to the major mistakes that have been seriously perverse.</seg>
<seg id="1088">What I have written elsewhere in respect of monetary policy is equally applicable to fiscal policy:</seg>
<seg id="1089">''What we need is not a skillful monetary driver of the economic vehicle continuously turning the steering wheel to adjust to the unexpected irregularities of the route, but some means of keeping the monetary passenger who is in the back seat as ballast from occasionally leaning over and giving the steering wheel a jerk that threatens to send the car off the road."</seg>
<seg id="1090">For fiscal policy, the appropriate counterpart to the monetary rule would be to plan expenditure programs entirely in terms of what the community wants to do through government rather than privately, and without any regard to problems of year-to-year economic stability;</seg>
<seg id="1091">to plan tax rates so as to provide sufficient revenues to cover planned expenditures on the average of one year with another, again without regard to year-to-year changes in economic stability; and to avoid erratic changes in either governmental expenditures or taxes.</seg>
<seg id="1092">Of course, some changes may be unavoidable.</seg>
<seg id="1093">A sudden change in the international situation may dictate large increases in military expenditures or permit welcome decreases.</seg>
<seg id="1094">Such changes account for some erratic shifts in federal expenditures in the postwar period.</seg>
<seg id="1095">But they by no means account for all.</seg>
<seg id="1096">Before leaving the subject of fiscal policy, I should like to discuss the view, now so widely held, that an increase in governmental expenditures relative to tax-receipts is necessarily expansionary and a decrease contractionary.</seg>
<seg id="1097">This view, which is at the heart of the belief that fiscal policy can serve as a balance wheel, is by now almost taken for granted by businessmen, professional economists, and laymen alike.</seg>
<seg id="1098">Yet it cannot be demonstrated to be true by logical considerations alone, has never been documented by empirical evidence, and is in fact inconsistent with the relevant empirical evidence of which I know.</seg>
<seg id="1099">The belief has its origin in a crude Keynesian analysis.</seg>
<seg id="1100">Suppose governmental expenditures are raised by $ 100 and taxes are kept unchanged.</seg>
<seg id="1101">Then, goes the simple analysis, on the first round, the people who receive the extra hundred dollars will have that much more income.</seg>
<seg id="1102">They will save some of it, say one-third, and spend the remaining two-thirds.</seg>
<seg id="1103">But this means that on the second round, someone else receives an extra $ 66 2/3 of income.</seg>
<seg id="1104">He in turn will save some and spend some, and so on and on in infinite sequence.</seg>
<seg id="1105">If at every stage1 A Program for Monetary Stability, one-third is saved and two-thirds spent, then the extra $ 100 of government expenditures will ultimately, on this analysis, add $ 300 to income.</seg>
<seg id="1106">This is the simple Keynesian multiplier analysis with a multiplier of three.</seg>
<seg id="1107">Of course, if there is one injection, the effects will die off, the initial jump in income of $ 100 being succeeded by a gradual decline back to the earlier level.</seg>
<seg id="1108">But if government expenditures are kept $ 100 higher per unit of time, say $ 100 a year higher, then, on this analysis, income will remain higher by $ 300 a year.</seg>
<seg id="1109">This simple analysis is extremely appealing.</seg>
<seg id="1110">But the appeal is spurious and arises from neglecting other relevant effects of the change in question.</seg>
<seg id="1111">When these are taken into account, the final result is much more dubious:</seg>
<seg id="1112">it may be anything from no change in income at all, in which case private expenditures will go down by the $ 100 by which government expenditures go up, to the full increase specified.</seg>
<seg id="1113">And even if money income increases, prices may rise, so real income will increase less or not at all.</seg>
<seg id="1114">Let us examine some of the possible slips' twixt cup and lip.</seg>
<seg id="1115">In the first place, nothing is said in the simple account about what the government spends the $ 100 on.</seg>
<seg id="1116">Suppose, for example, it spends it on something that individuals were otherwise obtaining for themselves.</seg>
<seg id="1117">They were, for example, spending $ 100 on paying fees to a park which paid the cost of attendants to keep it clean.</seg>
<seg id="1118">Suppose the government now pays these costs and permits people to enter the park "free."</seg>
<seg id="1119">The attendants still receive the same income, but the people who paid the fees have $ 100 available.</seg>
<seg id="1120">The government spending does not, even in the initial stage, add $ 100 to anyone's income.</seg>
<seg id="1121">What it does is to leave some people with $ 100 available to use for purposes other than the park, and presumably purposes they value less highly.</seg>
<seg id="1122">They can be expected to spend less out of their total income for consumer goods than formerly, since they are receiving the park services free.</seg>
<seg id="1123">How much less, it is not easy to say.</seg>
<seg id="1124">Even if we accept, as in the simple analysis, that people save one-third of additional income, it does not follow that when they get one set of consumer goods "free," two-thirds of the released money will be spent on other consumer goods.</seg>
<seg id="1125">One extreme possibility, of course, is that they will continue to buy the same collection of other consumer goods as they did before and add the released $ 100 to their savings.</seg>
<seg id="1126">In this case even in the simple Keynesian analysis, the effect of the government expenditures is completely offset:</seg>
<seg id="1127">government expenditures go up by $ 100, private down by $ 100.</seg>
<seg id="1128">Or, to take another example, the $ 100 may be spent to build a road that a private enterprise would otherwise have built or the availability of which may make repairs to the company's trucks unnecessary.</seg>
<seg id="1129">The firm then has funds released, but presumably will not spend them all on what are less attractive investments.</seg>
<seg id="1130">In these cases, government expenditures simply divert private expenditures and only the net excess of government expenditures is even available at the outset for the multiplier to work on.</seg>
<seg id="1131">From this point of view, it is paradoxical that the way to assure no diversion is to have the government spend the money for something utterly uselessˇŞthis is the limited intellectual content to the "filling-holes" type of make-work.</seg>
<seg id="1132">But of course this itself shows that there is something wrong with the analysis.</seg>
<seg id="1133">In the second place, nothing is said in the simple account about where the government gets the $ 100 to spend.</seg>
<seg id="1134">So far as the analysis goes, the results are the same whether the government prints extra money or borrows from the public.</seg>
<seg id="1135">But surely which it does will make a difference.</seg>
<seg id="1136">To separate fiscal from monetary policy, let us suppose the government borrows the $ 100 so that the stock of money is the same as it would have been in the absence of the government expenditure.</seg>
<seg id="1137">This is the proper assumption because the stock of money can be increased without extra government expenditure, if that is desired, simply by printing the money and buying outstanding government bonds with it.</seg>
<seg id="1138">But we must now ask what the effect of borrowing is.</seg>
<seg id="1139">To analyze this problem, let us assume that diversion does not occur, so in the first instance there is no direct offset to the $ 100 in the form of a compensating drop in private expenditures.</seg>
<seg id="1140">Note that the government's borrowing to spend does not alter the amount of money in private hands.</seg>
<seg id="1141">The government borrows $ 100 with its right hand from some individuals and hands the money with its left hand to those individuals to whom its expenditures go.</seg>
<seg id="1142">Different people hold the money but the total amount of money held is unchanged.</seg>
<seg id="1143">The simple Keynesian analysis implicitly assumes that borrowing the money does not have any effects on other spending.</seg>
<seg id="1144">There are two extreme circumstances under which this can occur.</seg>
<seg id="1145">First, suppose people are utterly indifferent to whether they hold bonds or money, so that bonds to get the $ 100 can be sold without having to offer a higher return to the buyer than such bonds were yielding before.</seg>
<seg id="1146">(Of course, $ 100 is so small an amount that it would in practice have a negligible effect on the required rate of return, but the issue is one of principle whose practical effect can be seen by letting the $ 100 stand for $ 100 million or $ 100 ten-million.)</seg>
<seg id="1147">In Keynesian jargon, there is a "liquidity trap" so people buy the bonds with "idle money."</seg>
<seg id="1148">If this is not the case, and clearly it cannot be indefinitely, then the government can sell the bonds only by offering a higher rate of return on it.</seg>
<seg id="1149">A higher rate will then have to be paid also by other borrowers.</seg>
<seg id="1150">This higher rate will in general discourage private spending on the part of would-be borrowers.</seg>
<seg id="1151">Here comes the second extreme circumstance under which the simple Keynesian analysis will hold:</seg>
<seg id="1152">if potential borrowers are so stubborn about spending that no rise in interest rates however steep will cut down their expenditures, or, in Keynesian jargon, if the marginal efficiency schedule of investment is perfectly inelastic with respect to the interest rate.</seg>
<seg id="1153">I know of no established economist, no matter how much of a Keynesian he may regard himself as being, who would regard either of these extreme assumptions as holding currently, or as being capable of holding over any considerable range of borrowing or rise in interest rates, or as having held except under rather special circumstances in the past.</seg>
<seg id="1154">Yet many an economist, let alone noneconomist, whether regarding himself as Keynesian or not, accepts as valid the belief that a rise in governmental expenditures relative to tax receipts, even when financed by borrowing, is necessarily expansionist, though as we have seen, this belief implicitly requires one of these extreme circumstances to hold.</seg>
<seg id="1155">If neither assumption holds, the rise in government expenditures will be offset by a decline in private expenditures on the part either of those who lend funds to the government, or of those who would otherwise have borrowed the funds.</seg>
<seg id="1156">How much of the rise in expenditures will be offset?</seg>
<seg id="1157">This depends on the holders of money.</seg>
<seg id="1158">The extreme assumption, implicit in a rigid quantity theory of money, is that the amount of money people want to hold depends, on the average, only on their income and not on the rate of return that they can get on bonds and similar securities.</seg>
<seg id="1159">In this case, since the total stock of money is the same before and after, the total money income will also have to be the same in order to make people just satisfied to hold that money stock.</seg>
<seg id="1160">This means that interest rates will have to rise enough to choke off an amount of private spending exactly equal to the increased public expenditure.</seg>
<seg id="1161">In this extreme case, there is no sense at all in which the government expenditures are expansionary.</seg>
<seg id="1162">Not even money income goes up, let alone real income.</seg>
<seg id="1163">All that happens is that government expenditures go up and private expenditures down.</seg>
<seg id="1164">I warn the reader that this is a highly simplified analysis.</seg>
<seg id="1165">A full analysis would require a lengthy textbook.</seg>
<seg id="1166">But even this simplified analysis is enough to demonstrate that any result is possible between a $ 300 rise in income and a zero rise.</seg>
<seg id="1167">The more stubborn consumers are with respect to how much they will spend on consumption out of a given income, and the more stubborn purchasers of capital goods are with respect to how much they will spend on such goods regardless of cost, the nearer the result will be to the Keynesian extreme of a $ 300 rise.</seg>
<seg id="1168">On the other side, the more stubborn money holders are with respect to the ratio they wish to maintain between their cash balances and their income, the closer the result will be to the rigid quantity theory extreme of no change in income.</seg>
<seg id="1169">In which of these respects the public is more stubborn is an empirical question to be judged from the factual evidence, not something that can be determined by reason alone.</seg>
<seg id="1170">Before the Great Depression of the 1930's, the bulk of economists would unquestionably have concluded that the result would be nearer to no rise in income than to a $ 300 rise.</seg>
<seg id="1171">Since then, the bulk of economists would unquestionably conclude the opposite.</seg>
<seg id="1172">More recently, there has been a movement back toward the earlier position.</seg>
<seg id="1173">Sad to say, none of these shifts can be said to be based on satisfactory evidence.</seg>
<seg id="1174">They have been based rather on intuitive judgments from crude experience.</seg>
<seg id="1175">In co-operation with some of my students, I have done some fairly extensive empirical work, for the U.S. and other countries, to get some more satisfactory evidence.</seg>
<seg id="1176">The results are striking.</seg>
<seg id="1177">They strongly suggest that the actual outcome will be closer to the quantity theory extreme than to the Keynesian.</seg>
<seg id="1178">The judgement that seems justified on the basis of this evidence is that the assumed $ 100 increase in government expenditures can on the average be expected to add just about $ 100 to income, sometimes less, sometimes more.</seg>
<seg id="1179">This means that a rise in government expenditures relative to income is not expansionary in any relevant sense.</seg>
<seg id="1180">It may add to money income but all of this addition is absorbed by government expenditures.</seg>
<seg id="1181">Private expenditures are unchanged.</seg>
<seg id="1182">Since prices are likely to rise in the process, or fall less than they otherwise would, the effect is to leave private expenditures smaller in real terms.</seg>
<seg id="1183">Converse propositions hold for a decline in government expenditures.</seg>
<seg id="1184">These conclusions cannot of course be regarded as final.</seg>
<seg id="1185">They are based on the broadest and most comprehensive body of evidence I know about, but that body of evidence still leaves much to be desired.One thing is however clear.</seg>
<seg id="1186">Whether the views so widely accepted about the effects of fiscal policy be right or wrong, they are contradicted by at least one extensive body of evidence.</seg>
<seg id="1187">I know of no other coherent or organized body of evidence justifying them.</seg>
<seg id="1188">They are part of economic mythology, not the demonstrated conclusions of economic analysis or quantitative studies.</seg>
<seg id="1189">Yet they have wielded immense influence in securing widespread public backing for far-reaching governmental interference in economic life.</seg>
<seg id="1190">Chapter VIˇŞ The Role of Government in Education</seg>
<seg id="1191">Formal schooling is today paid for and almost entirely administered by government bodies or nonprofit institutions.</seg>
<seg id="1192">This situation has developed gradually and is now taken so much for granted that little explicit attention is any longer directed to the reasons for the special treatment of schooling even in countries that are predominantly free enterprise in organization and philosophy.</seg>
<seg id="1193">The result has been an indiscriminate extension of governmental responsibility.</seg>
<seg id="1194">In terms of the principles developed in chapter ii, governmental intervention into education can be rationalized on two grounds.</seg>
<seg id="1195">The first is the existence of substantial "neighborhood effects," i.e., circumstances under which the action of one individual imposes significant costs on other individuals for which it is not feasible to make him compensate them, or yields significant gains to other individuals for which it is not feasible to make them compensate himˇŞcircumstances that make voluntary exchange impossible.</seg>
<seg id="1196">The second is the paternalistic concern for children and other irresponsible individuals.</seg>
<seg id="1197">Neighborhood effects and paternalism have very different implications for (1) general education for citizenship, and (2) specialized vocational education.</seg>
<seg id="1198">The grounds for governmental intervention are widely different in these two areas and justify very different types of action.</seg>
<seg id="1199">One further preliminary remark:</seg>
<seg id="1200">it is important to distinguish between "schooling" and "education."</seg>
<seg id="1201">Not all schooling is education nor all education, schooling.</seg>
<seg id="1202">The proper subject of concern is education.</seg>
<seg id="1203">The activities of government are mostly limited to schooling.</seg>
<seg id="1204">General Education for Citizenship</seg>
<seg id="1205">A stable and democratic society is impossible without a minimum degree of literacy and knowledge on the part of most citizens and without widespread acceptance of some common set of values.</seg>
<seg id="1206">Education can contribute to both.</seg>
<seg id="1207">In consequence, the gain from the education of a child accrues not only to the child or to his parents but also to other members of the society.</seg>
<seg id="1208">The education of my child contributes to your welfare by promoting a stable and democratic society.</seg>
<seg id="1209">It is not feasible to identify the particular individuals (or families) benefited and so to charge for the services rendered.</seg>
<seg id="1210">There is therefore a significant "neighborhood effect.</seg>
<seg id="1211">"What kind of governmental action is justified by this particular neighborhood effect?</seg>
<seg id="1212">The most obvious is to require that each child receive a minimum amount of schooling of a specified kind.</seg>
<seg id="1213">Such a requirement could be imposed upon the parents without further government action, just as owners of buildings, and frequently of automobiles, are required to adhere to specified standards to protect the safety of others.</seg>
<seg id="1214">There is, however, a difference between the two cases.</seg>
<seg id="1215">Individuals who cannot pay the costs of meeting the standards required for buildings or automobiles can generally divest themselves of the property by selling it.</seg>
<seg id="1216">The requirement can thus generally be enforced without government subsidy.</seg>
<seg id="1217">The separation of a child from a parent who cannot pay for the minimum required schooling is clearly inconsistent with our reliance on the family as the basic social unit and our belief in the freedom of the individual.</seg>
<seg id="1218">Moreover, it would be very likely to detract from his education for citizenship in a free society.</seg>
<seg id="1219">If the financial burden imposed by such a schooling requirement could readily be met by the great bulk of the families in a community, it might still be both feasible and desirable to require the parents to meet the cost directly.</seg>
<seg id="1220">Extreme cases could be handled by special subsidy provisions for needy families.</seg>
<seg id="1221">There are many areas in the United States today where these conditions are satisfied.</seg>
<seg id="1222">In these areas, it would be highly desirable to impose the costs directly on the parents.</seg>
<seg id="1223">This would eliminate the governmental machinery now required to collect tax funds from all residents during the whole of their lives and then pay it back mostly to the same people during the period when their children are in school.</seg>
<seg id="1224">It would reduce the likelihood that governments would also administer schools, a matter discussed further below.</seg>
<seg id="1225">It would increase the likelihood that the subsidy component of school expenditures would decline as the need for such subsidies declined with increasing general levels of income.</seg>
<seg id="1226">If, as now, the government pays for all or most schooling, a rise in income simply leads to a still larger circular flow of funds through the tax mechanism, and an expansion in the role of the government.</seg>
<seg id="1227">Finally, but by no means least, imposing the costs on the parents would tend to equalize the social and private costs of having children and so promote a better distribution of families by size.</seg>
<seg id="1228">Differences among families in resources and in number of children, plus the imposition of a standard of schooling involving very sizable costs, make such a policy hardly feasible in many parts of the United States.</seg>
<seg id="1229">Both in such areas, and in It is by no means so fantastic as may appear that such a step would noticeably affect the size of families.</seg>
<seg id="1230">It has paid, not only for the minimum amount of schooling required of all, but also for additional schooling at higher levels available to youngsters but not required of them.</seg>
<seg id="1231">One argument for both steps is the ''neighborhood effects" discussed above.</seg>
<seg id="1232">The costs are paid because this is the only feasible means of enforcing the required minimum.</seg>
<seg id="1233">Additional schooling is financed because other people benefit from the schooling of those of greater ability and interest, since this is a way of providing better social and political leadership.</seg>
<seg id="1234">The gain from these measures must be balanced against the costs, and there can be much honest difference of judgment about how extensive a subsidy is justified.</seg>
<seg id="1235">Most of us, however, would probably conclude that the gains are sufficiently important to justify some government subsidy.</seg>
<seg id="1236">These grounds justify government subsidy of only certain kinds of schooling.</seg>
<seg id="1237">To anticipate, they do not justify subsidizing purely vocational training which increases the economic productivity of the student but does not train him for either citizenship or leadership.</seg>
<seg id="1238">It is extremely difficult to draw a sharp line between the two types of schooling.</seg>
<seg id="1239">Most general schooling adds to the economic value of the studentˇŞindeed it is only in modern times and in a few countries that literacy has ceased to have a marketable value.</seg>
<seg id="1240">And much vocational training broadens the student's outlook.</seg>
<seg id="1241">Yet the distinction is meaningful.</seg>
<seg id="1242">Subsidizing the training of veterinarians, beauticians, dentists, and a host of other specialists, as is widely done in the United States in governmentally supported educational institutions, cannot be justified on the same grounds as subsidizing elementary schools or, at a higher level, liberal arts colleges.</seg>
<seg id="1243">Whether it can be justified on quite different grounds will be discussed later in this chapter.</seg>
<seg id="1244">The qualitative argument from "neighborhood effects" does not, of course, determine the specific kinds of schooling that should be subsidized or by how much they should be subsidized.</seg>
<seg id="1245">The social gain presumably is greatest for the lowest levels of schooling, where there is the nearest approach to unanimity about content, and declines continuously as the level of schooling rises.</seg>
<seg id="1246">Even this statement cannot be taken completely for granted.</seg>
<seg id="1247">Many governments subsidized universities long before they subsidized lower schools.</seg>
<seg id="1248">What forms of education have the greatest social advantage and how much of the community's limited resources should be spent on them must be decided by the judgment of the community expressed through its accepted political channels.</seg>
<seg id="1249">The aim of this analysis is not to decide these questions for the community but rather to clarify the issues involved in making a choice, in particular whether it is appropriate to make the choice on a communal rather than individual basis.</seg>
<seg id="1250">As we have seen, both the imposition of a minimum required level of schooling and the financing of this schooling by the state can be justified by the "neighborhood effects" of schooling.</seg>
<seg id="1251">A third step, namely the actual administration of educational institutions by the government, the "nationalization," as it were, of the bulk of the "education industry" is much more difficult to justify on these, or, so far as I can see, any other, grounds.</seg>
<seg id="1252">The desirability of such nationalization has seldom been faced explicitly.</seg>
<seg id="1253">Governments have, in the main, financed schooling by paying directly the costs of running educational institutions.</seg>
<seg id="1254">Thus this step seemed required by the decision to subsidize schooling.</seg>
<seg id="1255">Yet the two steps could readily be separated.</seg>
<seg id="1256">Governments could require a minimum level of schooling financed by giving parents vouchers redeemable for a specified maximum sum per child per year if spent on "approved" educational services.</seg>
<seg id="1257">Parents would then be free to spend this sum and any additional sum they themselves provided on purchasing educational services from an "approved'' institution of their own choice.</seg>
<seg id="1258">The educational services could be rendered by private enterprises operated for profit, or by non-profit institutions.</seg>
<seg id="1259">The role of the government would be limited to insuring that the schools met certain minimum standards, such as the inclusion of a minimum common content in their programs, much as it now inspects restaurants to insure that they maintain minimum sanitary standards.</seg>
<seg id="1260">An excellent example of a program of this sort is the United States educational program for veterans after World War II.</seg>
<seg id="1261">Each veteran who qualified was given a maximum sum per year that could be spent at any institution of his choice, provided it met certain minimum standards.</seg>
<seg id="1262">A more limited example is the provision in Britain whereby local authorities pay the fees of some students attending non-state schools.</seg>
<seg id="1263">Another is the arrangement in France whereby the state pays part of the costs for students attending non-state schools.</seg>
<seg id="1264">One argument for nationalizing schools resting on a "neighborhood effect" is that it might otherwise be impossible to provide the common core of values deemed requisite for social stability.</seg>
<seg id="1265">The imposition of minimum standards on privately conducted schools, as suggested above, might not be enough to achieve this result.</seg>
<seg id="1266">The issue can be illustrated concretely in terms of schools run by different religious groups.</seg>
<seg id="1267">Such schools, it can be argued, will instil sets of values that are inconsistent with one another and with those instilled in non-sectarian schools;</seg>
<seg id="1268">in this way, they convert education into a divisive rather than a unifying force.</seg>
<seg id="1269">Carried to its extreme, this argument would call not only for governmentally administered schools, but also for compulsory attendance at such schools.</seg>
<seg id="1270">Existing arrangements in the United States and most other Western countries are a halfway house.</seg>
<seg id="1271">Governmentally administered schools are available but not compulsory.</seg>
<seg id="1272">However, the link between the financing of schooling and its administration places other schools at a disadvantage:</seg>
<seg id="1273">they get the benefit of little or none of the governmental funds spent on schoolingˇŞa situation that has been the source of much political dispute, particularly in France and at present in the United States.</seg>
<seg id="1274">The elimination of this disadvantage might, it is feared, greatly strengthen the parochial schools and so render the problem of achieving a common core of values even more difficult.</seg>
<seg id="1275">Persuasive as this argument is, it is by no means clear that it is valid or that denationalizing schooling would have the effects suggested.</seg>
<seg id="1276">On grounds of principle, it conflicts with the preservation of freedom itself.</seg>
<seg id="1277">Drawing a line between providing for the common social values required for a stable society, on the one hand, and indoctrination inhibiting freedom of thought and belief, on the other is another of those vague boundaries that is easier to mention than to define.</seg>
<seg id="1278">In terms of effects, denationalizing schooling would widen the range of choice available to parents.</seg>
<seg id="1279">If, as at present, parents can send their children to public schools without special payment, very few can or will send them to other schools unless they too are subsidized.</seg>
<seg id="1280">Parochial schools are at a disadvantage in not getting any of the public funds devoted to schooling, but they have the compensating advantage of being run by institutions that are willing to subsidize them and can raise funds to do so.</seg>
<seg id="1281">There are few other sources of subsidies for private schools.</seg>
<seg id="1282">If present public expenditures on schooling were made available to parents regardless of where they send their children, a wide variety of schools would spring up to meet the demand.</seg>
<seg id="1283">Parents could express their views about schools directly by withdrawing their children from one school and sending them to another, to a much greater extent than is now possible.</seg>
<seg id="1284">In general, they can now take this step only at considerable costˇŞby sending their children to a private school or by changing their residence.</seg>
<seg id="1285">For the rest, they can express their views only through cumbrous political channels.</seg>
<seg id="1286">Perhaps a somewhat greater degree of freedom to choose schools could be made available in a governmentally administered system, but it would be difficult to carry this freedom very far in view of the obligation to provide every child with a place.</seg>
<seg id="1287">Here, as in other fields, competitive enterprise is likely to be far more efficient in meeting consumer demand than either nationalized enterprises or enterprises run to serve other purposes.</seg>
<seg id="1288">The final result may therefore be that parochial schools would decline rather than grow in importance.</seg>
<seg id="1289">A related factor working in the same direction is the understandable reluctance of parents who send their children to parochial schools to increase taxes to finance higher public school expenditures.</seg>
<seg id="1290">As a result, those areas where parochial schools are important have great difficulty raising funds for public schools.</seg>
<seg id="1291">Insofar as quality is related to expenditure, as to some extent it undoubtedly is, public schools tend to be of lower quality in such areas and hence parochial schools are relatively more attractive.</seg>
<seg id="1292">Another special case of the argument that governmentally conducted schools are necessary for education to be a unifying force is that private schools would tend to exacerbate class distinctions.</seg>
<seg id="1293">Given greater freedom about where to send their children, parents of a kind would flock together and so prevent a healthy intermingling of children from decidedly different backgrounds.</seg>
<seg id="1294">Whether or not this argument is valid in principle, it is not at all clear that the stated results would follow.</seg>
<seg id="1295">Under present arrangements, stratification of residential areas effectively restricts the intermingling of children from decidedly different backgrounds.</seg>
<seg id="1296">In addition, parents are not now prevented from sending their children to private schools.</seg>
<seg id="1297">Only a highly limited class can or does do so, parochial schools aside, thus producing further stratification.</seg>
<seg id="1298">Indeed, this argument seems to me to point in almost the diametrically opposite directionˇŞtoward the denationalizing of schools.</seg>
<seg id="1299">Ask yourself in what respect the inhabitant of a low income neighborhood, let alone of a Negro neighborhood in a large city, is most disadvantaged.</seg>
<seg id="1300">If he attaches enough importance to, say, a new automobile, he can, by dint of saving, accumulate enough money to buy the same car as a resident of a high-income suburb.</seg>
<seg id="1301">To do so, he need not move to that suburb.</seg>
<seg id="1302">On the contrary, he can get the money partly by economizing on his living quarters.</seg>
<seg id="1303">And this goes equally for clothes, or furniture, or books, or what not.</seg>
<seg id="1304">But let a poor family in a slum have a gifted child and let it set such high value on his or her schooling that it is willing to scrimp and save for the purpose.</seg>
<seg id="1305">Unless it can get special treatment, or scholarship assistance, at one of the very few private schools, the family is in a very difficult position.</seg>
<seg id="1306">The "good" public schools are in the high income neighborhoods.</seg>
<seg id="1307">The family might be willing to spend something in addition to what it pays in taxes to get better schooling for its child.</seg>
<seg id="1308">But it can hardly afford simultaneously to move to the expensive neighborhood.</seg>
<seg id="1309">Our views in these respects are, I believe, still dominated by the small town which had but one school for the poor and rich residents alike.</seg>
<seg id="1310">Under such circumstances, public schools may well have equalized opportunities.</seg>
<seg id="1311">With the growth of urban and suburban areas, the situation has changed drastically.</seg>
<seg id="1312">Our present school system, far from equalizing opportunity, very likely does the opposite.</seg>
<seg id="1313">It makes it all the harder for the exceptional fewˇŞand it is they who are the hope of the futureˇŞto rise above the poverty of their initial state.</seg>
<seg id="1314">Another argument for nationalizing schooling is "technical monopoly."</seg>
<seg id="1315">In small communities and rural areas, the number of children may be too small to justify more than one school of reasonable size, so that competition cannot be relied on to protect the interests of parents and children.</seg>
<seg id="1316">As in other cases of technical monopoly, the alternatives are unrestricted private monopoly, state controlled private monopoly, and public operationˇŞa choice among evils.</seg>
<seg id="1317">This argument, though clearly valid and significant, has been greatly weakened in recent decades by improvements in transportation and increasing concentration of the population in urban communities.</seg>
<seg id="1318">The arrangement that perhaps comes closest to being justified by these considerationsˇŞat least for primary and secondary educationˇŞis a combination of public and private schools.</seg>
<seg id="1319">Parents who choose to send their children to private schools would be paid a sum equal to the estimated cost of educating a child in a public school, provided that at least this sum was spent on education in an approved school.</seg>
<seg id="1320">This arrangement would meet the valid features of the "technical monopoly" argument.</seg>
<seg id="1321">It would meet the just complaints of parents that if they send their children to private nonsubsidized schools they are required to pay twice for educationˇŞonce in the form of general taxes and once directly.</seg>
<seg id="1322">It would permit competition to develop.</seg>
<seg id="1323">The development and improvement of all schools would thus be stimulated.</seg>
<seg id="1324">The injection of competition would do much to promote a healthy variety of schools.</seg>
<seg id="1325">It would do much, also, to introduce flexibility into school systems.</seg>
<seg id="1326">Not least of its benefits would be to make the salaries of school teachers responsive to market forces.</seg>
<seg id="1327">It would thereby give public authorities an independent standard against which to judge salary scales and promote a more rapid adjustment to changes in conditions of demand and supply.</seg>
<seg id="1328">It is widely urged that the great need in schooling is more money to build more facilities and to pay higher salaries to teachers in order to attract better teachers.</seg>
<seg id="1329">This seems a false diagnosis.</seg>
<seg id="1330">The amount of money spent on schooling has been rising at an extraordinarily high rate, far faster than our total income.</seg>
<seg id="1331">Teachers' salaries have been rising far faster than returns in comparable occupations.</seg>
<seg id="1332">The problem is not primarily that we are spending too little moneyˇŞthough we may beˇŞbut that we are getting so little per dollar spent.</seg>
<seg id="1333">Perhaps the amounts of money spent on magnificent structures and luxurious grounds at many schools are properly classified as expenditures on schooling.</seg>
<seg id="1334">It is hard to accept them equally as expenditures on education.</seg>
<seg id="1335">And this is equally clear with respect to courses in basket weaving, social dancing, and the numerous other special subjects that do such credit to the ingenuity of educators.</seg>
<seg id="1336">I hasten to add that there can be no conceivable objection to parents' spending their own money on such frills if they wish.</seg>
<seg id="1337">That is their business.</seg>
<seg id="1338">The objection is to using money raised by taxation imposed on parents and non-parents alike for such purposes.</seg>
<seg id="1339">Wherein are the "neighborhood effects" that justify such use of tax money?</seg>
<seg id="1340">A major reason for this kind of use of public money is the present system of combining the administration of schools with their financing.</seg>
<seg id="1341">The parent who would prefer to see money used for better teachers and texts rather than coaches and corridors has no way of expressing this preference except by persuading a majority to change the mixture for all.</seg>
<seg id="1342">This is a special case of the general principle that a market permits each to satisfy his own tasteˇŞeffective proportional representation;</seg>
<seg id="1343">whereas the political process imposes conformity.</seg>
<seg id="1344">In addition, the parent who would like to spend some extra money on his child's education is greatly limited.</seg>
<seg id="1345">He cannot add something to the amount now being spent to school his child and transfer his child to a correspondingly more costly school.</seg>
<seg id="1346">If he does transfer his child, he must pay the whole cost and not simply the additional cost.</seg>
<seg id="1347">He can only spend extra money easily on extra-curricular activitiesˇŞdancing lessons, music lessons, etc.</seg>
<seg id="1348">Since the private outlets for spending more money on schooling are so blocked, the pressure to spend more on the education of children manifests itself in ever higher public expenditures on items ever more tenuously related to the basic justification for governmental intervention into schooling.</seg>
<seg id="1349">As this analysis implies, the adoption of the suggested arrangements might well mean smaller governmental expenditures on schooling, yet higher total expenditures.</seg>
<seg id="1350">It would enable parents to buy what they want more efficiently and thereby lead them to spend more than they now do directly and indirectly through taxation.</seg>
<seg id="1351">It would prevent parents from being frustrated in spending more money on schooling by both the present need for conformity in how the money is spent and by the understandable reluctance on the part of persons not currently having children in school, and especially those who will not in the future have them in school, to impose higher taxes on themselves for purposes often far removed from education as they understand the term.</seg>
<seg id="1352">With respect to teachers' salaries, the major problem is not that they are too low on the averageˇŞ they may well be too high on the averageˇŞbut that they are too uniform and rigid.</seg>
<seg id="1353">Poor teachers are grossly overpaid and good teachers grossly underpaid.</seg>
<seg id="1354">Salary schedules tend to be uniform and determined far more by seniority, degrees received, and teaching certificates acquired than by merit.</seg>
<seg id="1355">This, too, is largely a result of the present system of governmental administration of schools and becomes more serious as the unit over which governmental control is exercised becomes larger.</seg>
<seg id="1356">Indeed, this very fact is a major reason why professional educational organizations so strongly favor broadening the unitˇŞfrom the local school district to the state, from the state to the federal government.</seg>
<seg id="1357">In any bureaucratic, essentially civil-service organization, standard salary scales are almost inevitable;</seg>
<seg id="1358">it is next to impossible to simulate competition capable of providing wide differences in salaries according to merit.</seg>
<seg id="1359">The educators, which means the teachers themselves, come to exercise primary control.</seg>
<seg id="1360">The parent or local community comes to exercise little control.</seg>
<seg id="1361">In any area, whether it be carpentry or plumbing or teaching, the majority of workers favor standard salary scales and oppose merit differentials, for the obvious reason that the2 A striking example of the same effect in another field is the British National Health Service.</seg>
<seg id="1362">This is a special case of the general tendency for people to seek to collude to fix prices, whether through unions or industrial monopolies.</seg>
<seg id="1363">But collusive agreements will generally be destroyed by competition unless the government enforces them, or at least renders them considerable support.</seg>
<seg id="1364">If one were to seek deliberately to devise a system of recruiting and paying teachers calculated to repel the imaginative and daring and self-confident and to attract the dull and mediocre and uninspiring, he could hardly do better than imitate the system of requiring teaching certificates and enforcing standard salary structures that has developed in the larger city and state-wide systems.</seg>
<seg id="1365">It is perhaps surprising that the level of ability in elementary and secondary school teaching is as high as it is under these circumstances.</seg>
<seg id="1366">The alternative system would resolve these problems and permit competition to be effective in rewarding merit and attracting ability to teaching.</seg>
<seg id="1367">Why has governmental intervention in schooling in the United States developed along the lines it has?</seg>
<seg id="1368">I do not have the detailed knowledge of educational history that would be required to answer this question definitively.</seg>
<seg id="1369">A few conjectures may nonetheless be useful to suggest the kinds of considerations that may alter the appropriate social policy.</seg>
<seg id="1370">I am by no means sure that the arrangements I now propose would in fact have been desirable a century ago.</seg>
<seg id="1371">Before the extensive growth in transportation, the "technical monopoly" argument was much stronger.</seg>
<seg id="1372">Equally important, the major problem in the United States in the nineteenth and early twentieth century was not to promote diversity but to create the core of common values essential to a stable society.</seg>
<seg id="1373">Great streams of immigrants were flooding the United States from all over the world, speaking different languages and observing diverse customs.</seg>
<seg id="1374">The "melting pot" had to introduce some measure of conformity and loyalty to common values.</seg>
<seg id="1375">The public school had an important function in this task, not least by imposing English as a common language.</seg>
<seg id="1376">Under the alternative voucher scheme, the minimum standards imposed on schools to qualify for approval could have included the use of English.</seg>
<seg id="1377">But it might well have been more difficult to insure that this requirement was imposed and satisfied in a private school system.</seg>
<seg id="1378">I do not mean to conclude that the public school system was definitely preferable to the alternative, but only that a far stronger case could have been made for it then than now.</seg>
<seg id="1379">Our problem today is not to enforce conformity;</seg>
<seg id="1380">it is rather that we are threatened with an excess of conformity.</seg>
<seg id="1381">Our problem is to foster diversity, and the alternative would do this far more effectively than a nationalized school system.</seg>
<seg id="1382">Another factor that may have been important a century ago was the combination of the general disrepute of cash grants to individuals ("handouts"), with the absence of an efficient administrative machinery to handle the distribution of vouchers and check their use.</seg>
<seg id="1383">Such machinery is a phenomenon of modern times that has come to full flower with the enormous extension of personal taxation and of social security programs.</seg>
<seg id="1384">In its absence, the administration of schools may have been regarded as the only possible way to finance education.</seg>
<seg id="1385">As some of the examples cited above (England and France) suggest, some features of the proposed arrangements are present in existing educational systems.</seg>
<seg id="1386">And there has been strong and, I believe, increasing pressure for arrangements of this kind in most Western countries.</seg>
<seg id="1387">This is perhaps partly explained by modern developments in governmental administrative machinery that facilitate such arrangements.</seg>
<seg id="1388">Although many administrative problems would arise in changing over from the present to the proposed system and in its administration, these seem neither insoluble nor unique.</seg>
<seg id="1389">As in the denationalization of other activities, existing premises and equipment could be sold to private enterprises that wanted to enter the field.</seg>
<seg id="1390">Thus, there would be no waste of capital in the transition.</seg>
<seg id="1391">Since governmental units, at least in some areas, would continue to administer schools, the transition would be gradual and easy.</seg>
<seg id="1392">The local administration of schooling in the United States and some other countries would similarly facilitate the transition, since it would encourage experimentation on a small scale.</seg>
<seg id="1393">Difficulties would doubtless arise in determining eligibility for grants from a particular governmental unit, but this is identical with the existing problem of determining which unit is obligated to provide schooling facilities for a particular child.</seg>
<seg id="1394">Differences in size of grants would make one area more attractive than another just as differences in the quality of schooling now have the same effect.</seg>
<seg id="1395">The only additional complication is a possibly greater opportunity for abuse because of the greater freedom to decide where to educate children.</seg>
<seg id="1396">Supposed difficulty of administration is a standard defense of the status quo against any proposed change;</seg>
<seg id="1397">in this particular case, it is an even weaker defense than usual because existing arrangements must master not only the major problems raised by the proposed arrangements but also the additional problems raised by the administration of schools as a governmental function.</seg>
<seg id="1398">Schooling at College and University Level</seg>
<seg id="1399">The preceding discussion is concerned mostly with primary and secondary schooling.</seg>
<seg id="1400">For higher schooling, the case for nationalization on grounds either of neighborhood effects or of technical monopoly is even weaker.</seg>
<seg id="1401">For the lowest levels of schooling, there is considerable agreement, approximating unanimity, on the appropriate content of an educational program for citizens of a democracyˇŞthe three R's cover most of the ground.</seg>
<seg id="1402">At successively higher levels, there is less and less agreement.</seg>
<seg id="1403">Surely, well below the level of the American college, there is insufficient agreement to justify imposing the views of a majority, much less a plurality, on all.</seg>
<seg id="1404">The lack of agreement may, indeed, extend so far as to cast doubts on the appropriateness even of subsidizing schooling at this level;</seg>
<seg id="1405">it surely goes far enough to undermine any case for nationalization on the grounds of providing a common core of values.</seg>
<seg id="1406">There can hardly be any question of "technical monopoly" at this level, in view of the distances that individuals can and do go to attend institutions of higher learning.</seg>
<seg id="1407">Governmental institutions play a smaller role in the United States in higher schooling than at primary and secondary levels.</seg>
<seg id="1408">Yet they grew greatly in importance, certainly until the 1920's, and now account for more than half of the students attending colleges and universities.</seg>
<seg id="1409">One of the main reasons for their growth was their relative cheapness;</seg>
<seg id="1410">most state and municipal colleges and universities charge much lower tuition fees than private universities can afford to charge.</seg>
<seg id="1411">Private universities have in consequence had serious financial problems, and have quite properly complained of ''unfair" competition.</seg>
<seg id="1412">They have wanted to maintain their independence from government, yet at the same time have felt driven by financial pressure to seek government aid.</seg>
<seg id="1413">The preceding analysis suggests the lines along which a satisfactory solution can be sought.</seg>
<seg id="1414">Public expenditures on higher schooling can be justified as a means of training youngsters for citizenship and for community leadershipˇŞthough I hasten to add that the large fraction of current expenditure that goes for strictly vocational training cannot be justified in this way, or indeed, as we shall see, in any other.</seg>
<seg id="1415">Restricting the subsidy to schooling obtained at a state-administered institution cannot be justified on any grounds.</seg>
<seg id="1416">Any subsidy should be granted to individuals to be spent at institutions of their own choosing, provided only that the schooling is of a kind that it is desired to subsidize.</seg>
<seg id="1417">Any government schools that are retained should charge fees covering educational costs and so compete on an equal level with non-government-supported schools.</seg>
<seg id="1418">The resulting system would follow in its broad outlines the arrangements adopted in the United States after World War II for financing the education of veterans, except that the funds would presumably come from the states rather than the federal government.</seg>
<seg id="1419">The adoption of such arrangements would make for more effective competition among various types of schools and for a more efficient utilization of their resources.</seg>
<seg id="1420">It would eliminate the pressure for direct government assistance to private colleges and universities and thus preserve their full independence and diversity at the same time as it enabled them to grow</seg>
<seg id="1421">It might also have the ancillary advantage of causing scrutiny of the purposes for which subsidies are granted.</seg>
<seg id="1422">The subsidization of institutions rather than of people has led to an indiscriminate subsidization of all activities appropriate for such institutions, rather than of the activities appropriate for the state to subsidize.</seg>
<seg id="1423">Even cursory examination suggests that while the two classes of activities overlap, they are far from identical.</seg>
<seg id="1424">The equity argument for the alternative arrangement is particularly clear at college and university levels because of the existence of a large number and variety of private schools.</seg>
<seg id="1425">The state of Ohio, for example, says to its citizens:</seg>
<seg id="1426">"If you have a youngster who wants to go to college, we shall automatically grant him or her a sizable four-year scholarship, provided that he or she can satisfy rather minimal education requirements, and provided further that he or she is smart enough to choose to go to the University of Ohio.</seg>
<seg id="1427">If your youngster wants to go, or you want him or her to go, to Oberlin College, or Western Reserve University, let alone to Yale, Harvard, Northwestern, Beloit, or the University of Chicago, not a penny for him."</seg>
<seg id="1428">How can such a program be justified?</seg>
<seg id="1429">Would it not be far more equitable, and promote a higher standard of scholarship, to devote such money as the state of Ohio wished to spend on higher education to scholarships tenable at any college or university and to require the University of Ohio to compete on equal terms with other colleges and universities?</seg>
<seg id="1430">Vocational and Professional Schooling</seg>
<seg id="1431">Vocational and professional schooling has no neighborhood effects of the kind attributed above to general education.</seg>
<seg id="1432">It is a form of investment in human capital precisely analogous to investment in machinery, buildings, or other forms of non-human capital.</seg>
<seg id="1433">Its function is to raise the economic productivity.</seg>
<seg id="1434">If it does so, the individual is rewarded in a free enterprise society by receiving a higher return for his services than he would otherwise be able to command.</seg>
<seg id="1435">This difference in return is the economic incentive to invest capital whether in the form of a machine or a human being.</seg>
<seg id="1436">In both cases, extra returns must be balanced against the costs of acquiring them.</seg>
<seg id="1437">For vocational schooling, the major costs are the income foregone during the period of training, interest lost by postponing the beginning of the earning period, and special expenses of acquiring the training such as tuition fees and expenditures on books and equipment.</seg>
<seg id="1438">For physical capital, the major costs are the expense of constructing the capital equipment and the interest foregone during construction.</seg>
<seg id="1439">In both cases, an individual presumably regards the investment as desirable if the extra returns, as he views them, exceed the extra costs, as he views them.</seg>
<seg id="1440">In both cases, if the individual undertakes the investment and if the state neither subsidizes the investment nor taxes the return, the individual (or his parents, sponsor, or benefactor) in general bears all the extra costs and receives all the extra returns:</seg>
<seg id="1441">there are no obvious unborne costs or unappropriable returns that tend to make private incentives diverge systematically from those that are socially appropriate.</seg>
<seg id="1442">If capital were as readily available for investment in human beings as for investment in physical assets, whether through the market or through direct investment by the individuals concerned, or their parents or benefactors, the rate of return on capital would tend to be roughly equal in the two fields.</seg>
<seg id="1443">If it were higher on non-human capital, parents would have an incentive to buy such capital for their children instead of investing a corresponding sum in vocational training, and conversely.</seg>
<seg id="1444">In fact, however, there is considerable empirical evidence</seg>
<seg id="1445">The increased return may be only partly in a monetary form;</seg>
<seg id="1446">it may also consist of non-pecuniary advantages attached to the occupation for which the vocational training fits the individual.</seg>
<seg id="1447">Similarly, the occupation may have non-pecuniary disadvantages, which would have to be reckoned among the costs of the investment.</seg>
<seg id="1448">Investment in human beings cannot be financed on the same terms or with th e same ease as investment in physical capital.</seg>
<seg id="1449">It is easy to see why.</seg>
<seg id="1450">If a fixed money loan is made to finance investment in physical capital, the lender can get some security for his loan in the form of a mortgage or residual claim to the physical asset itself, and he can count on realizing at least part of his investment in case of default by selling the physical asset.</seg>
<seg id="1451">If he makes a comparable loan to increase the earning power of a human being, he clearly cannot get any comparable security.</seg>
<seg id="1452">In a non-slave state, the individual embodying the investment cannot be bought and sold.</seg>
<seg id="1453">Even if he could, the security would not be comparable.</seg>
<seg id="1454">The productivity of the physical capital does not in general depend on the co-operativeness of the original borrower.</seg>
<seg id="1455">The productivity of the human capital quite obviously does.</seg>
<seg id="1456">A loan to finance the training of an individual who has no security to offer other than his future earnings is therefore a much less attractive proposition than a loan to finance the erection of a building:</seg>
<seg id="1457">the security is less, and the cost of subsequent collection of interest and principal is very much greater.</seg>
<seg id="1458">A further complication is introduced by the inappropriateness of fixed money loans to finance investment in training.</seg>
<seg id="1459">Such an investment necessarily involves much risk.</seg>
<seg id="1460">The average expected return may be high, but there is wide variation about the average.</seg>
<seg id="1461">Death or physical incapacity is one obvious source of variation but this is probably much less important than differences in ability, energy, and good fortune.</seg>
<seg id="1462">Consequently if fixed money loans were made, and were secured only by expected future earnings, a considerable fraction would never be repaid.</seg>
<seg id="1463">In order to make such loans attractive to lenders, the nominal interest rate charged on all loans would have to be sufficiently high to compensate for the capital losses on the defaulted loans.</seg>
<seg id="1464">The high nominal interest rate would both conflict with usury laws and make the loans unattractive to borrowers</seg>
<seg id="1465">The device adopted to meet the corresponding problem for other risky investments is equity investment plus limited liability on the part of the shareholders.</seg>
<seg id="1466">The counterpart for education would be to "buy" a share in an individual's earning prospects;</seg>
<seg id="1467">to advance him the funds needed to finance his training on condition that he agree to pay the lender a specified fraction of his future earnings.</seg>
<seg id="1468">In this way, a lender would get back more than his initial investment from relatively successful individuals, which would compensate for the failure to recoup his original investment from the unsuccessful.</seg>
<seg id="1469">There seems no legal obstacle to private contracts of this kind, even though they are economically equivalent to the purchase of a share in an individual's earning capacity and thus to partial slavery.</seg>
<seg id="1470">One reason why such contracts have not become common, despite their potential profitability to both lender and borrower, is presumably the high costs of administering them, given the freedom of individuals to move from one place to another, the need for getting accurate income statements, and the long period over which the contracts would run.</seg>
<seg id="1471">These costs would presumably be particularly high for investment on a small scale with a wide geographical spread of the individuals financed.</seg>
<seg id="1472">Such costs may well be the primary reason that this type of investment has never developed under private auspices.</seg>
<seg id="1473">The main development has been stimulated by United Student Aid Funds, a non-profit institution which underwrites loans made by individual banks.been played by the cumulative effect of the novelty of the idea, the reluctance to think of investment in human beings as strictly comparable to investment in physical assets, the resultant likelihood of irrational public condemnation of such contracts, even if voluntarily entered into, and legal and conventional limitations on the kind of investments that may be made by the financial intermediaries that would be best suited to engage in such investments, namely, life insurance companies.</seg>
<seg id="1474">The potential gains, particularly to early entrants, are so great that it would be worth incurring extremely heavy administrative costs.</seg>
<seg id="1475">Whatever the reason, an imperfection of the market has led to underinvestment in human capital.</seg>
<seg id="1476">Government intervention might therefore be rationalized on grounds both of "technical monopoly," insofar as the obstacle to the development of such investment has been administrative costs, and of improving the operation of the market, insofar as it has been simply market frictions and rigidities.</seg>
<seg id="1477">If government does intervene, how should it do so?</seg>
<seg id="1478">One obvious form of intervention, and the only form that has so far been taken, is outright government subsidy of vocational or professional schooling financed out of general revenues.</seg>
<seg id="1479">This form seems clearly inappropriate.</seg>
<seg id="1480">Investment should be carried to the point at which the extra return repays the investment and yields the market rate of interest on it.</seg>
<seg id="1481">If the investment is in a human being, the extra return takes the form of a higher payment for the individual's services than he could otherwise command.</seg>
<seg id="1482">In a private market economy, the individual would get this return as his personal income.</seg>
<seg id="1483">If the investment were subsidized, he would have borne none of the costs.</seg>
<seg id="1484">In consequence, if subsidies were given to all who wished</seg>
<seg id="1485">All sorts of other common services might be rendered by the XYZ company to "its" physicians, lawyers, dentists, and so on.to get the training, and could meet minimum quality standards, there would tend to be overinvestment in human beings, since individuals would have an incentive to get the training so long as it yielded any extra return over private costs, even if the return were insufficient to repay the capital invested, let alone yield any interest on it.</seg>
<seg id="1486">To avoid such overinvestment, government would have to restrict the subsidies.</seg>
<seg id="1487">Even apart from the difficulty of calculating the "correct" amount of investment, this would involve rationing in some essentially arbitrary way the limited amount of investment among more claimants than could be financed.</seg>
<seg id="1488">Those fortunate enough to get their training subsidized would receive all the returns from the investment whereas the costs would be borne by the taxpayers in generalˇŞan entirely arbitrary and almost surely perverse redistribution of income.</seg>
<seg id="1489">The desideratum is not to redistribute income but to make capital available at comparable terms for human and physical investment.</seg>
<seg id="1490">Individuals should bear the costs of investment in themselves and receive the rewards.</seg>
<seg id="1491">They should not be prevented by market imperfections from making the investment when they are willing to bear the costs.</seg>
<seg id="1492">One way to achieve this result is for government to engage in equity investment in human beings.</seg>
<seg id="1493">A governmental body could offer to finance or help finance the training of any individual who could meet minimum quality standards.</seg>
