<seg id="1494">It would make available a limited sum per year for a specified number of years, provided the funds were spent on securing training at a recognized institution.</seg>
<seg id="1495">The individual in return would agree to pay to the government in each future year a specified percentage of his earnings in excess of a specified sum for each $ 1,000 that he received from the government.</seg>
<seg id="1496">This payment could easily be combined with payment of income tax and so involve a minimum of additional administrative expense.</seg>
<seg id="1497">The base sum should be set equal to estimated average earnings without the specialized training;</seg>
<seg id="1498">the fraction of earnings paid should be calculated so as to make the whole project self-financing.</seg>
<seg id="1499">In this way, the individuals who received the training would in effect bear the whole cost.</seg>
<seg id="1500">The amount invested could then be determined by individual choice.</seg>
<seg id="1501">Provided this was the only way in which government financed vocational or professional training, and provided the calculated earnings reflected all relevant returns and costs, the free choice of individuals would tend to produce the optimum amount of investment.</seg>
<seg id="1502">The second proviso is unfortunately not likely to be fully satisfied because of the impossibility of including non-pecuniary returns mentioned above.</seg>
<seg id="1503">In practice, therefore, investment under the plan would still be somewhat too small and would not be distributed in the optimum manner.</seg>
<seg id="1504">For several reasons, it would be preferable for private financial institutions and non-profit institutions such as foundations and universities to develop this plan.</seg>
<seg id="1505">Because of the difficulties involved in estimating the base earnings and the fraction of earnings in excess of the base to be paid to the government, there is great danger that the scheme would turn into a political football.</seg>
<seg id="1506">Information on existing earnings in various occupations would provide only a rough approximation to the values that would render the project self-financing.</seg>
<seg id="1507">In addition, the base earnings and the fraction should vary from individual to individual in accordance with any differences in expected earning capacity that can be predicted in advance, just as life insurance premiums vary among groups that have different life expectancy.</seg>
<seg id="1508">Insofar as administrative expense is the obstacle to the development of such a plan on a private basis, the appropriate unit of government to make funds available is the federal government rather than smaller units.</seg>
<seg id="1509">Any one state would have the same costs as an insurance company, say, in keeping track of the people whom it had financed.</seg>
<seg id="1510">These would be minimized though not completely eliminated for the federal government.</seg>
<seg id="1511">An individual who migrated to another country, for example, might still be legally or morally obligated to pay the agreed-on share of his earnings, yet it might be difficult and expensive to enforce the obligation.</seg>
<seg id="1512">Highly successful people might therefore have an incentive to migrate.</seg>
<seg id="1513">For a fuller discussion of the role of non-pecuniary advantages and disadvantages in determining earnings in different pursuits, problem arises, of course, under the income tax, and to a very much greater extent.</seg>
<seg id="1514">This and other administrative problems of conducting the scheme on a federal level, while doubtless troublesome in detail, do not seem serious.</seg>
<seg id="1515">The serious problem is the political one already mentioned:</seg>
<seg id="1516">how to prevent the scheme from becoming a political football and in the process being converted from a self-financing project to a means of subsidizing vocational education.</seg>
<seg id="1517">But if the danger is real, so is the opportunity.</seg>
<seg id="1518">Existing imperfections in the capital market tend to restrict the more expensive vocational and professional training to individuals whose parents or benefactors can finance the training required.</seg>
<seg id="1519">They make such individuals a "non-competing" group sheltered from competition by the unavailability of the necessary capital to many able individuals.</seg>
<seg id="1520">The result is to perpetuate inequalities in wealth and status.</seg>
<seg id="1521">The development of arrangements such as those outlined above would make capital more widely available and would thereby do much to make equality of opportunity a reality, to diminish inequalities of income and wealth, and to promote the full use of our human resources.</seg>
<seg id="1522">And it would do so not by impeding competition, destroying incentive, and dealing with symptoms, as would result from the outright redistribution of income, but by strengthening competition, making incentives effective, and eliminating the causes of inequality.</seg>
<seg id="1523">Chapter VIIˇŞ Capitalism and Discrimination</seg>
<seg id="1524">It is a striking historical fact that the development of capitalism has been accompanied by a major reduction in the extent to which particular religious, racial, or social groups have operated under special handicaps in respect of their economic activities;</seg>
<seg id="1525">have, as the saying goes, been discriminated against.</seg>
<seg id="1526">The substitution of contract arrangements for status arrangements was the first step toward the freeing of the serfs in the Middle Ages.</seg>
<seg id="1527">The preservation of Jews through the Middle Ages was possible because of the existence of a market sector in which they could operate and maintain themselves despite official persecution.</seg>
<seg id="1528">Puritans and Quakers were able to migrate to the New World because they could accumulate the funds to do so in the market despite disabilities imposed on them in other aspects of their life.</seg>
<seg id="1529">The Southern states after the Civil War took many measures to impose legal restrictions on Negroes.</seg>
<seg id="1530">One measure which was never taken on any scale was the establishment of barriers to the ownership of either real or personal property.</seg>
<seg id="1531">The failure to impose such barriers clearly did not reflect any special concern to avoid restrictions on Negroes.</seg>
<seg id="1532">It reflected rather, a basic belief in private property which was so strong that it overrode the desire to discriminate against Negroes.</seg>
<seg id="1533">The maintenance of the general rules of private property and of capitalism have been a major source of opportunity for Negroes and have permitted them to make greater progress than they otherwise could have made.</seg>
<seg id="1534">To take a more general example, the preserves of discrimination in any society are the areas that are most monopolistic in character, whereas discrimination against groups of particular color or religion is least in those areas where there is the greatest freedom of competition.</seg>
<seg id="1535">As pointed out in chapter i, one of the paradoxes of experience is that, in spite of this historical evidence, it is precisely the minority groups that have frequently furnished the most vocal and most numerous advocates of fundamental alterations in a capitalist society.</seg>
<seg id="1536">They have tended to attribute to capitalism the residual restrictions they experience rather than to recognize that the free market has been the major factor enabling these restrictions to be as small as they are.</seg>
<seg id="1537">We have already seen how a free market separates economic efficiency from irrelevant characteristics.</seg>
<seg id="1538">As noted in chapter i, the purchaser of bread does not know whether it was made from wheat grown by a white man or a Negro, by a Christian or a Jew.</seg>
<seg id="1539">In consequence, the producer of wheat is in a position to use resources as effectively as he can, regardless of what the attitudes of the community may be toward the color, the religion, or other characteristics of the people he hires.</seg>
<seg id="1540">Furthermore, and perhaps more important, there is an economic incentive in a free market to separate economic efficiency from other characteristics of the individual.</seg>
<seg id="1541">A businessman or an entrepreneur who expresses preferences in his business activities that are not related to productive efficiency is at a disadvantage compared to other individuals who do not.</seg>
<seg id="1542">Such an individual is in effect imposing higher costs on himself than are other individuals who do not have such preferences.</seg>
<seg id="1543">Hence, in a free market they will tend to drive him out.</seg>
<seg id="1544">This same phenomenon is of much wider scope.</seg>
<seg id="1545">It is often taken for granted that the person who discriminates against others because of their race, religion, color, or whatever, incurs no costs by doing so but simply imposes costs on others.</seg>
<seg id="1546">This view is on a par with the very similar fallacy that a country does not hurt itself by imposing tariffs on the products of other countries.</seg>
<seg id="1547">Both are equally wrong.</seg>
<seg id="1548">The man who objects to buying from or working alongside a Negro, for example, thereby limits his range of choice.</seg>
<seg id="1549">He will generally have to pay a higher price for what he buys or receive a lower return for his work.</seg>
<seg id="1550">Or, put the other way, those of us who regard color of skin or religion as irrelevant can buy some things more cheaply as a result.</seg>
<seg id="1551">As these comments perhaps suggest, there are real problems in defining and interpreting discrimination.</seg>
<seg id="1552">The man who exercises discrimination pays a price for doing so.</seg>
<seg id="1553">He is, as it were, ''buying" what he regards as a "product."</seg>
<seg id="1554">It is hard to see that discrimination can have any meaning other than a "taste" of others that one does not share.</seg>
<seg id="1555">We do not regard it as "discrimination"ˇŞor at least not in the same invidious senseˇŞif an individual is willing to pay a higher price to listen to one singer than to another, although we do if he is willing to pay a higher price to have services rendered to him by a person of one color than by a person of another.</seg>
<seg id="1556">The difference between the two cases is that in the one case we share the taste, and in the other case we do not.</seg>
<seg id="1557">Is there any difference in principle between the taste that leads a householder to prefer an attractive servant to an ugly one and the taste that leads another to prefer a Negro to a white or a white to a Negro, except that we sympathize and agree with the one taste and may not with the other?</seg>
<seg id="1558">I do not mean to say that all tastes are equally good.</seg>
<seg id="1559">On the contrary, I believe strongly that the color of a man's skin or the religion of his parents is, by itself, no reason to treat him differently;</seg>
<seg id="1560">that a man should be judged by what he is and what he does and not by these external characteristics.</seg>
<seg id="1561">I deplore what seem to me the prejudice and narrowness of outlook of those whose tastes differ from mine in this respect and I think the less of them for it.</seg>
<seg id="1562">But in a society based on free discussion, the appropriate recourse is for me to seek to persuade them that their tastes are bad and that they should change their views and their behavior, not to use coercive power to enforce my tastes and my attitudes on others.</seg>
<seg id="1563">Fair Employment Practices Legislation</seg>
<seg id="1564">Fair employment practice commissions that have the task of preventing "discrimination" in employment by reason of race, color, or religion have been established in a number of states.</seg>
<seg id="1565">Such legislation clearly involves interference with the freedom of individuals to enter into voluntary contracts with one another.</seg>
<seg id="1566">It subjects any such contract to approval or disapproval by the state.</seg>
<seg id="1567">Thus it is directly an interference with freedom of the kind that we would object to in most other contexts.</seg>
<seg id="1568">Moreover, as is true with most other interferences with freedom, the individuals subjected to the law may well not be those whose actions even the proponents of the law wish to control.</seg>
<seg id="1569">For example, consider a situation in which there are grocery stores serving a neighborhood inhabited by people who have a strong aversion to being waited on by Negro clerks.</seg>
<seg id="1570">Suppose one of the grocery stores has a vacancy for a clerk and the first applicant qualified in other respects happens to be a Negro.</seg>
<seg id="1571">Let us suppose that as a result of the law the store is required to hire him.</seg>
<seg id="1572">The effect of this action will be to reduce the business done by this store and to impose losses on the owner.</seg>
<seg id="1573">If the preference of the community is strong enough, it may even cause the store to close.</seg>
<seg id="1574">When the owner of the store hires white clerks in preference to Negroes in the absence of the law, he may not be expressing any preference or prejudice or taste of his own.</seg>
<seg id="1575">He may simply be transmitting the tastes of the community.</seg>
<seg id="1576">He is, as it were, producing the services for the consumers that the consumers are willing to pay for.</seg>
<seg id="1577">Nonetheless, he is harmed, and indeed may be the only one harmed appreciably, by a law which prohibits him from engaging in this activity, that is, prohibits him from pandering to the tastes of the community for having a white rather than a Negro clerk.</seg>
<seg id="1578">The consumers, whose preferences the law is intended to curb, will be affected substantially only to the extent that the number of stores is limited and hence they must pay higher prices because one store has gone out of business.</seg>
<seg id="1579">This analysis can be generalized.</seg>
<seg id="1580">In a very large fraction of cases, employers are transmitting the preference of either their customers or their other employees when they adopt employment policies that treat factors irrelevant to technical physical productivity as relevant to employment.</seg>
<seg id="1581">Indeed, employers typically have an incentive, as noted earlier, to try to find ways of getting around the preferences of their consumers or of their employees if such preferences impose higher costs upon them.</seg>
<seg id="1582">The proponents of FEPC argue that interference with the freedom of individuals to enter into contracts with one another with respect to employment is justified because the individual who refuses to hire a Negro instead of a white, when both are equally qualified in terms of physical productive capacity, is harming others, namely, the particular color or religious group whose employment opportunity is limited in the process.</seg>
<seg id="1583">This argument involves a serious confusion between two very different kinds of harm.</seg>
<seg id="1584">One kind is the positive harm that one individual does another by physical force, or by forcing him to enter into a contract without his consent.</seg>
<seg id="1585">An obvious example is the man who hits another over the head with a blackjack.</seg>
<seg id="1586">A less obvious example is stream pollution discussed in chapter ii.</seg>
<seg id="1587">The second kind is the negative harm that occurs when two individuals are unable to find mutually acceptable contracts, as when I am unwilling to buy something that someone wants to sell me and therefore make him worse off than he would be if I bought the item.</seg>
<seg id="1588">If the community at large has a preference for blues singers rather than for opera singers, they are certainly increasing the economic well-being of the first relative to the second.</seg>
<seg id="1589">If a potential blues singer can find employment and a potential opera singer cannot, this simply means that the blues singer is rendering services which the community regards as worth paying for whereas the potential opera singer is not.</seg>
<seg id="1590">The potential opera singer is "harmed" by the community's taste.</seg>
<seg id="1591">He would be better off and the blues singer "harmed" if the tastes were the reverse.</seg>
<seg id="1592">Clearly, this kind of harm does not involve any involuntary exchange or an imposition of costs or granting of benefits to third parties.</seg>
<seg id="1593">There is a strong case for using government to prevent one person from imposing positive harm, which is to say, to prevent coercion.</seg>
<seg id="1594">There is no case whatsoever for using government to avoid the negative kind of ''harm."</seg>
<seg id="1595">On the contrary, such government intervention reduces freedom and limits voluntary co-operation.</seg>
<seg id="1596">FEPC legislation involves the acceptance of a principle that proponents would find abhorrent in almost every other application.</seg>
<seg id="1597">If it is appropriate for the state to say that individuals may not discriminate in employment because of color or race or religion, then it is equally appropriate for the state, provided a majority can be found to vote that way, to say that individuals must discriminate in employment on the basis of color, race or religion.</seg>
<seg id="1598">The Hitler Nuremberg laws and the laws in the Southern states imposing special disabilities upon Negroes are both examples of laws similar in principle to FEPC.</seg>
<seg id="1599">Opponents of such laws who are in favor of FEPC cannot argue that there is anything wrong with them in principle, that they involve a kind of state action that ought not to be permitted.</seg>
<seg id="1600">They can only argue that the particular criteria used are irrelevant.</seg>
<seg id="1601">They can only seek to persuade other men that they should use other criteria instead of these.</seg>
<seg id="1602">If one takes a broad sweep of history and looks at the kind of things that the majority will be persuaded of if each individual case is to be decided on its merits rather than as part of a general principle, there can be little doubt that the effect of a widespread acceptance of the appropriateness of government action in this area would be extremely undesirable, even from the point of view of those who at the moment favor FEPC.</seg>
<seg id="1603">If, at the moment, the proponents of FEPC are in a position to make their views effective, it is only because of a constitutional and federal situation in which a regional majority in one part of the country may be in a position to impose its views on a majority in another part of the country.</seg>
<seg id="1604">As a general rule, any minority that counts on specific majority action to defend its interests is shortsighted in the extreme.</seg>
<seg id="1605">Acceptance of a general self-denying ordinance applying to a class of cases may inhibit specific majorities from exploiting specific minorities.</seg>
<seg id="1606">In the absence of such a selfdenying ordinance, majorities can surely be counted on to use their power to give effect to their preferences, or if you will, prejudices, not to protect minorities from the prejudices of majorities.</seg>
<seg id="1607">To put the matter in another and perhaps more striking way, consider an individual who believes that the present pattern of tastes is undesirable and who believes that Negroes have less opportunity than he would like to see them have.</seg>
<seg id="1608">Suppose he puts his beliefs into practice by always choosing the Negro applicant for a job whenever there are a number of applicants more or less equally qualified in other respects.</seg>
<seg id="1609">Under present circumstances should he be prevented from doing so?</seg>
<seg id="1610">Clearly the logic of the FEPC is that he should be.</seg>
<seg id="1611">The counterpart to fair employment in the area where these principles have perhaps been worked out more than any other, namely, the area of speech, is "fair speech" rather than free speech.</seg>
<seg id="1612">In this respect the position of the American Civil Liberties Union seems utterly contradictory.</seg>
<seg id="1613">It favors both free speech and fair employment laws.</seg>
<seg id="1614">One way to state the justification for free speech is that we do not believe that it is desirable that momentary majorities decide what at any moment shall be regarded as appropriate speech.</seg>
<seg id="1615">We want a free market in ideas, so that ideas get a chance to win majority or near-unanimous acceptance, even if initially held only by a few.</seg>
<seg id="1616">Precisely the same considerations apply to employment or more generally to the market for goods and services.</seg>
<seg id="1617">Is it any more desirable that momentary majorities decide what characteristics are relevant to employment than what speech is appropriate?</seg>
<seg id="1618">Indeed, can a free market in ideas long be maintained if a free market in goods and services is destroyed?</seg>
<seg id="1619">The ACLU will fight to the death to protect the right of a racist to preach on a street corner the doctrine of racial segregation.</seg>
<seg id="1620">But it will favor putting him in jail if he acts on his principles by refusing to hire a Negro for a particular job.</seg>
<seg id="1621">As already stressed, the appropriate recourse of those of us who believe that a particular criterion such as color is irrelevant is to persuade our fellows to be of like mind, not to use the coercive power of the state to force them to act in accordance with our principles.</seg>
<seg id="1622">Of all groups, the ACLU should be the first both to recognize and proclaim that this is so.</seg>
<seg id="1623">Right-to-Work Laws</seg>
<seg id="1624">Some states have passed so-called "right-to-work" laws.</seg>
<seg id="1625">These are laws which make it illegal to require membership in a union as a condition of employment.</seg>
<seg id="1626">The principles involved in right-to-work laws are identical with those involved in FEPC.</seg>
<seg id="1627">Both interfere with the freedom of the employment contract, in the one case by specifying that a particular color or religion cannot be made a condition of employment;</seg>
<seg id="1628">in the other, that membership in a union cannot be.</seg>
<seg id="1629">Despite the identity of principle, there is almost 100 per cent divergence of views with respect to the two laws.</seg>
<seg id="1630">Almost all who favor FEPC oppose right to work;</seg>
<seg id="1631">almost all who favor right to work oppose FEPC.</seg>
<seg id="1632">As a liberal, I am opposed to both, as I am equally to laws outlawing the so-called "yellow-dog" contract (a contract making non-membership in a union a condition of employment).</seg>
<seg id="1633">Given competition among employers and employees, there seems no reason why employers should not be free to offer any terms they want to their employees.</seg>
<seg id="1634">In some cases employers find that employees prefer to have part of their remuneration take the form of amenities such as baseball fields or play facilities or better rest facilities rather than cash.</seg>
<seg id="1635">Employers then find that it is more profitable to offer these facilities as part of their employment contract rather than to offer higher cash wages.</seg>
<seg id="1636">Similarly, employers may offer pension plans, or require participation in pension plans, and the like.</seg>
<seg id="1637">None of this involves any interference with the freedom of individuals to find employment.</seg>
<seg id="1638">It simply reflects an attempt by employers to make the characteristics of the job suitable and attractive to employees.</seg>
<seg id="1639">So long as there are many employers, all employees who have particular kinds of wants will be able to satisfy them by finding employment with corresponding employers.</seg>
<seg id="1640">Under competitive conditions the same thing would be true with respect to the closed shop.</seg>
<seg id="1641">If in fact some employees would prefer to work in firms that have a closed shop and others in firms that have an open shop, there would develop different forms of employment contracts, some having the one provision, others the other provision.</seg>
<seg id="1642">As a practical matter, of course, there are some important differences between FEPC and right to work.</seg>
<seg id="1643">The differences are the presence of monopoly in the form of union organizations on the employee side and the presence of federal legislation in respect of labor unions.</seg>
<seg id="1644">It is doubtful that in a competitive labor market, it would in fact ever be profitable for employers to offer a closed shop as a condition of employment.</seg>
<seg id="1645">Whereas unions may frequently be found without any strong monopoly power on the side of labor, a closed shop almost never is.</seg>
<seg id="1646">It is almost always a symbol of monopoly power.</seg>
<seg id="1647">The coincidence of a closed shop and labor monopoly is not an argument for a right-to-work law.</seg>
<seg id="1648">It is an argument for action to eliminate monopoly power regardless of the particular forms and manifestations which it takes.</seg>
<seg id="1649">It is an argument for more effective and widespread antitrust action in the labor field.</seg>
<seg id="1650">Another special feature that is important in practice is the conflict between federal and state law and the existence at the moment of a federal law which applies to all the states and which leaves a loophole for the individual state only through the passage of a right-to-work law.</seg>
<seg id="1651">The optimum solution would be to have the federal law revised.</seg>
<seg id="1652">The difficulty is that no individual state is in a position to bring this about and yet people within an individual state might wish to have a change in the legislation governing union organization within their state.</seg>
<seg id="1653">The right-to-work law may be the only effective way in which this can be done and therefore the lesser of evils.</seg>
<seg id="1654">Partly,I suppose, because I am inclined to believe that a right-to-work law will not in and of itself have any great effect on the monopoly power of the unions, I do not accept this justification for it.</seg>
<seg id="1655">The practical arguments seem to me much too weak to outweigh the objection of principle.</seg>
<seg id="1656">Segregation in Schooling</seg>
<seg id="1657">Segregation in schooling raises a particular problem not covered by the previous comments for one reason only.</seg>
<seg id="1658">The reason is that schooling is, under present circumstances, primarily operated and administered by government.</seg>
<seg id="1659">This means that government must make an explicit decision.</seg>
<seg id="1660">It must either enforce segregation or enforce integration.</seg>
<seg id="1661">Both seem to me bad solutions.</seg>
<seg id="1662">Those of us who believe that color of skin is an irrelevant characteristic and that it is desirable for all to recognize this, yet who also believe in individual freedom, are therefore faced with a dilemma.</seg>
<seg id="1663">If one must choose between the evils of enforced segregation or enforced integration, I myself would find it impossible not to choose integration.</seg>
<seg id="1664">The preceding chapter, written initially without any regard at all to the problem of segregation or integration, gives the appropriate solution that permits the avoidance of both evilsˇŞa nice illustration of how arrangements designed to enhance freedom in general cope with problems of freedom in particular.</seg>
<seg id="1665">The appropriate solution is to eliminate government operation of the schools and permit parents to choose the kind of school they want their children to attend.</seg>
<seg id="1666">In addition, of course, we should all of us, insofar as we possibly can, try by behavior and speech to foster the growth of attitudes and opinions that would lead mixed schools to become the rule and segregated schools the rare exception.</seg>
<seg id="1667">If a proposal like that of the preceding chapter were adopted, it would permit a variety of schools to develop, some all white, some all Negro, some mixed.</seg>
<seg id="1668">It would permit the transition from one collection of schools to anotherˇŞhopefully to mixed schoolsˇŞto be gradual as community attitudes changed.</seg>
<seg id="1669">It would avoid the harsh political conflict that has been doing so much to raise social tensions and disrupt the community.</seg>
<seg id="1670">It would in this special area, as the market does in general, permit co-operation without conformity.</seg>
<seg id="1671">The state of Virginia has adopted a plan having many features in common with that outlined in the preceding chapter.</seg>
<seg id="1672">Though adopted for the purpose of avoiding compulsory integration, I predict that the ultimate effects of the law will be very differentˇŞafter all, the difference between result and intention is one of the primary justifications of a free society;</seg>
<seg id="1673">it is desirable to let men follow the bent of their own interests because there is no way of predicting where they will come out.</seg>
<seg id="1674">Indeed, even in the early stages there have been surprises.</seg>
<seg id="1675">I have been told that one of the first requests for a voucher to finance a change of school was by a parent transferring a child from a segregated to an integrated school.</seg>
<seg id="1676">The transfer was requested not for this purpose but simply because the integrated school happened to be the better school educationally.</seg>
<seg id="1677">Looking further ahead, if the voucher system is not abolished, Virginia will provide an experiment to test the conclusions of the preceding chapter.</seg>
<seg id="1678">If those conclusions are right, we should see a flowering of the schools available in Virginia, with an increase in their diversity, a substantial if not spectacular rise in the quality of the leading schools, and a later rise in the quality of the rest under the impetus of the leaders.</seg>
<seg id="1679">On the other side of the picture, we should not be so na?ve as to suppose that deep-seated values and beliefs can be uprooted in short measure by law.</seg>
<seg id="1680">I live in Chicago.</seg>
<seg id="1681">Chicago has no law compelling segregation.</seg>
<seg id="1682">Its laws require integration.</seg>
<seg id="1683">Yet in fact the public schools of Chicago are probably as thoroughly segregated as the schools of most Southern cities.</seg>
<seg id="1684">There is almost no doubt at all that if the Virginia system were introduced in Chicago, the result would be an appreciable decrease in segregation, and a great widening in the opportunities available to the ablest and most ambitious Negro youth.</seg>
<seg id="1685">Chapter VIIIˇŞ Monopoly and the Social Responsibility of Business and Labor</seg>
<seg id="1686">Competition has two very different meanings.</seg>
<seg id="1687">In ordinary discourse, competition means personal rivalry, with one individual seeking to outdo his known competitor.</seg>
<seg id="1688">In the economic world, competition means almost the opposite.</seg>
<seg id="1689">There is no personal rivalry in the competitive market place.</seg>
<seg id="1690">There is no personal higgling.</seg>
<seg id="1691">The wheat farmer in a free market does not feel himself in personal rivalry with, or threatened by, his neighbor, who is, in fact, his competitor.</seg>
<seg id="1692">The essence of a competitive market is its impersonal character.</seg>
<seg id="1693">No one participant can determine the terms on which other participants shall have access to goods or jobs.</seg>
<seg id="1694">All take prices as given by the market and no individual can by himself have more than a negligible influence on price though all participants together determine price by the combined effect of their separate actions.</seg>
<seg id="1695">Monopoly exists when a specific individual or enterprise has sufficient control over a particular product or service to determine significantly the terms on which other individuals shall have access to it.</seg>
<seg id="1696">In some ways, monopoly comes closer to the ordinary concept of competition since it does involve personal rivalry.</seg>
<seg id="1697">Monopoly raises two classes of problems for a free society.</seg>
<seg id="1698">First, the existence of monopoly means a limitation on voluntary exchange through a reduction in the alternatives available to individuals.</seg>
<seg id="1699">Second, the existence of monopoly raises the issue of the "social responsibility," as it has come to be called, of the monopolist.</seg>
<seg id="1700">The participant in a competitive market has no appreciable power to alter terms of exchange;</seg>
<seg id="1701">he is hardly visible as a separate entity;</seg>
<seg id="1702">hence it is hard to argue that he has any "social responsibility" except that which is shared by all citizens to obey the law of the land and to live according to his lights.</seg>
<seg id="1703">The monopolist is visible and has power.</seg>
<seg id="1704">It is easy to argue that he should discharge his power not solely to further his own interests but to further socially desirable ends.</seg>
<seg id="1705">Yet the widespread application of such a doctrine would destroy a free society.</seg>
<seg id="1706">Of course, competition is an ideal type, like a Euclidean line or point.</seg>
<seg id="1707">No one has ever seen a Euclidean lineˇŞwhich has zero width and depthˇŞyet we all find it useful to regard many a Euclidean volumeˇŞsuch as a surveyor's stringˇŞas a Euclidean line.</seg>
<seg id="1708">Similarly, there is no such thing as "pure" competition.</seg>
<seg id="1709">Every producer has some effect, however tiny, on the price of the product he produces.</seg>
<seg id="1710">The important issue for understanding and for policy is whether this effect is significant or can properly be neglected, as the surveyor can neglect the thickness of what he calls a "line."</seg>
<seg id="1711">The answer must, of course, depend on the problem.</seg>
<seg id="1712">But as I have studied economic activities in the United States, I have become increasingly impressed with how wide is the range of problems and industries for which it is appropriate to treat the economy as if it were competitive.</seg>
<seg id="1713">The issues raised by monopoly are technical and cover a field in which I have no special competence.</seg>
<seg id="1714">In consequence, this chapter is limited to a fairly cursory survey of some of the broader issues:</seg>
<seg id="1715">the extent of monopoly, sources of monopoly, appropriate government policy, and the social responsibility of business and labor.</seg>
<seg id="1716">The Extent of Monopoly</seg>
<seg id="1717">There are three important areas of monopoly requiring separate consideration:</seg>
<seg id="1718">monopoly in industry, monopoly in labor, and governmentally produced monopoly.</seg>
<seg id="1719">1.Monopoly in Industry</seg>
<seg id="1720">The most important fact about enterprise monopoly is its relative unimportance from the point of view of the economy as a whole.</seg>
<seg id="1721">There are some four million separate operating enterprises in the United States;</seg>
<seg id="1722">some four hundred thousand new ones are born each year;</seg>
<seg id="1723">a somewhat smaller number die each year.</seg>
<seg id="1724">Nearly one fifth of the working population is self-employed.</seg>
<seg id="1725">In almost any industry that one can mention, there are giants and pygmies side by side.</seg>
<seg id="1726">Beyond these general impressions, it is difficult to cite a satisfactory objective measure of the extent of monopoly and of competition.</seg>
<seg id="1727">The main reason is one already noted:</seg>
<seg id="1728">these concepts as used in economic theory are ideal constructs designed to analyze particular problems rather than to describe existing situations.</seg>
<seg id="1729">As a result, there can be no clear-cut determination of whether a particular enterprise or industry is to be regarded as monopolistic or as competitive.</seg>
<seg id="1730">The difficulty of assigning precise meanings to such terms leads to much misunderstanding.</seg>
<seg id="1731">The same word is used to refer to different things, depending on the background of experience in terms of which the state of competition is judged.</seg>
<seg id="1732">Perhaps the most striking example is the extent to which an American student will describe as monopolistic, arrangements that a European would regard as highly competitive.</seg>
<seg id="1733">As a result, Europeans interpreting American literature and discussion in terms of the meaning attached to the terms competition and monopoly in Europe tend to believe that there is a much greater degree of monopoly in the United States than in fact exists.</seg>
<seg id="1734">A number of studies, particularly by G. Warren Nutter and George J. Stigler, have tried to classify industries as monopolistic, workably competitive, and governmentally operated or supervised, and to trace changes over time in these categories.</seg>
<seg id="1735">They conclude that, as of 1939, roughly one quarter of the economy could be regarded as governmentally operated or supervised.</seg>
<seg id="1736">Of the three-quarters remaining, at most one-quarter and perhaps as little as 15 per cent can be regarded as monopolistic, at least three-quarters and perhaps as much as 85 per cent, as competitive.</seg>
<seg id="1737">The governmentally operated or supervised sector has of course grown greatly over the past half-century or so.</seg>
<seg id="1738">Within the private sector, on the other hand, there appears not to have been any tendency for the scope of monopoly to have increased and it may well have decreased.</seg>
<seg id="1739">There is, I suspect, a widespread impression that monopoly is both far more important than these estimates suggest and has been growing steadily over time.</seg>
<seg id="1740">One reason for this mistaken impression is the tendency to confuse absolute and relative size.</seg>
<seg id="1741">As the economy has grown, enterprises have become larger in absolute size.</seg>
<seg id="1742">This has been taken to mean also that they account for a larger fraction of the market, whereas the market may have grown even faster.</seg>
<seg id="1743">A second reason is that monopoly is more newsworthy and leads to more attention than competition.</seg>
<seg id="1744">If individuals were asked to list the major industries in the United States, almost all would include automobile production, few would include wholesale trade.</seg>
<seg id="1745">Yet wholesale trade is twice as important as automobile production.</seg>
<seg id="1746">Wholesale trade is highly competitive, hence draws little attention to itself.</seg>
<seg id="1747">Few people could name any leading enterprises in wholesale trade, though there are some that are very large in absolute size.</seg>
<seg id="1748">Automobile production, while in certain respects highly competitive, has many fewer firms and is certainly closer to monopoly.</seg>
<seg id="1749">Everyone can name the leading firms producing automobiles.</seg>
<seg id="1750">To cite one other striking example:</seg>
<seg id="1751">domestic service is a vastly more important industry than the telegraph and telephone industry.</seg>
<seg id="1752">A third reason is the general bias and tendency to overemphasize1G.</seg>
<seg id="1753">.the importance of the big versus the small, of which the preceding point is only a particular manifestation.</seg>
<seg id="1754">Finally, the main characteristic of our society is taken to be its industrial character.</seg>
<seg id="1755">This leads to overemphasis of the manufacturing sector of the economy, which accounts for only about one-quarter of output or employment.</seg>
<seg id="1756">And monopoly is far more prevalent in manufacturing than in other sectors of the economy.</seg>
<seg id="1757">The over-estimation of the importance of monopoly is accompanied, for much the same reasons, by an over-estimation of the importance of those technological changes that promote monopoly by comparison with those that extend competition.</seg>
<seg id="1758">For example, the spread of mass production has been greatly stressed.</seg>
<seg id="1759">The developments in transportation and communication that have promoted competition by reducing the importance of local regional markets and widening the scope within which competition could take place have been given much less attention.</seg>
<seg id="1760">The growing concentration of the automobile industry is a commonplace;</seg>
<seg id="1761">growth of the trucking industry which reduces dependence on large railroads passes with little notice;</seg>
<seg id="1762">so does the declining concentration in the steel industry.</seg>
<seg id="1763">2.Monopoly in Labor</seg>
<seg id="1764">There is a similar tendency to overestimate the importance of monopoly on the side of labor.</seg>
<seg id="1765">Labor unions include roughly a quarter of the working population and this greatly overestimates the importance of unions on the structure of wages.</seg>
<seg id="1766">Many unions are utterly ineffective.</seg>
<seg id="1767">Even the strong and powerful unions have only a limited effect on the wage structure.</seg>
<seg id="1768">It is even clearer for labor than for industry why there is a strong tendency to overestimate the importance of monopoly.</seg>
<seg id="1769">Given a labor union, any wage increase will come through the union, even though it may not be a consequence of the union organization.</seg>
<seg id="1770">The wages of domestic servants have risen very greatly in recent years.</seg>
<seg id="1771">Had there been a union of domestic servants, the increase would have come through the union and would have been attributed to it.</seg>
<seg id="1772">This is not to say that unions are unimportant.</seg>
<seg id="1773">Like enterprise monopoly, they play a significant and meaningful role making many wage rates different from what the market alone would establish.</seg>
<seg id="1774">It would be as much a mistake to underestimate as to overestimate their importance.</seg>
<seg id="1775">I once made a rough estimate that because of unions something like 10 to 15 per cent of the working population has had its wage rates raised by something like 10 to 15 per cent.</seg>
<seg id="1776">This means that something like 85 or 90 per cent of the working population has had its wage rates reduced by some 4 per cent.</seg>
<seg id="1777">Since I made these estimates, much more detailed studies have been done by others.</seg>
<seg id="1778">My impression is that they yield results of much the same order of magnitude.</seg>
<seg id="1779">If unions raise wage rates in a particular occupation or industry, they necessarily make the amount of employment available in that occupation or industry less than it otherwise would beˇŞjust as any higher price cuts down the amount purchased.</seg>
<seg id="1780">The effect is an increased number of persons seeking other jobs, which forces down wages in other occupations.</seg>
<seg id="1781">Since unions have generally been strongest among groups that would have been high-paid anyway, their effect has been to make high paid workers higher paid at the expense of lower-paid workers.</seg>
<seg id="1782">Unions have therefore not only harmed the public at large and workers as a whole by distorting the use of labor;</seg>
<seg id="1783">they have also made the incomes of the working class more unequal by reducing the opportunities available to the most disadvantaged workers.</seg>
<seg id="1784">In one respect, there is an important difference between labor and enterprise monopoly.</seg>
<seg id="1785">While there seems not to have been any upward trend in the importance of enterprise monopoly over the past half-century, there certainly has been in the importance of labor monopoly.</seg>
<seg id="1786">Labor unions grew notably in importance during World War I, declined during the 'twenties and early 'thirties, then took an enormous leap forward during the New Deal period.</seg>
<seg id="1787">They consolidated their gains during and after World War II.</seg>
<seg id="1788">More recently, they have been just holding their own or even declining.</seg>
<seg id="1789">The decline does not reflect a decline within particular industries or occupations but rather a declining importance of those industries or occupations in which unions are strong relative to those in which unions are weak.</seg>
<seg id="1790">The distinction I have been drawing between labor monopoly and enterprise monopoly is in one respect too sharp.</seg>
<seg id="1791">To some extent, labor unions have served as a means of enforcing monopoly in the sale of a product.</seg>
<seg id="1792">The clearest example is in coal.</seg>
<seg id="1793">The Guffey Coal Act was an attempt to provide legal support for a price-fixing cartel of coal-mine operators.</seg>
<seg id="1794">When, in the mid-thirties, this Act was declared unconstitutional, John L. Lewis and the United Mine Workers stepped into the breach.</seg>
<seg id="1795">By calling strikes or work stoppages whenever the amount of coal above the ground got so large as to threaten to force down prices, Lewis controlled output and thereby prices with the unspoken co-operation of the industry.</seg>
<seg id="1796">The gains from this cartel management were divided between the coal mine operators and the miners.</seg>
<seg id="1797">The gain to the miners was in the form of higher wage rates, which of course meant fewer miners employed.</seg>
<seg id="1798">Hence only those miners who retained employment shared the cartel gains and even they took a large part of the gain in the form of greater leisure.</seg>
<seg id="1799">The possibility of the unions playing this role derives from their exemption from the Sherman Antitrust Act.</seg>
<seg id="1800">Many other unions have taken advantage of this exemption and are better interpreted as enterprises selling the services of cartellizing an industry than as labor organizations.</seg>
<seg id="1801">The Teamster's Union is perhaps the most notable.</seg>
<seg id="1802">3.Government and Government-Supported Monopoly</seg>
<seg id="1803">In the United States, direct government monopoly in the production of goods for sale is not very extensive.</seg>
<seg id="1804">The post office, electric power production, as by TVA and other publicly owned power stations;</seg>
<seg id="1805">the provision of highway services, sold indirectly through the gasoline tax or directly by tolls, and municipal water and similar plants are the main examples.</seg>
<seg id="1806">In addition, with so large a defense, space, and research budget as we now have, the federal government has become essentially the only purchaser of the products of many enterprises and whole industries.</seg>
<seg id="1807">This raises very serious problems for the preservation of a free society, but not of a kind that are best considered under the heading of "monopoly."</seg>
<seg id="1808">The use of government to establish, support and enforce cartel and monopoly arrangements among private producers has grown much more rapidly than direct government mo nopoly and is currently far more important.</seg>
<seg id="1809">The Interstate Commerce Commission is an early example, and it has extended its scope from railroads to trucking and other means of transport.</seg>
<seg id="1810">The agricultural program is undoubtedly the most notorious.</seg>
<seg id="1811">It is essentially a governmentally enforced cartel.</seg>
<seg id="1812">Other examples are the Federal Communications Commission, with its control over radio and television;</seg>
<seg id="1813">the Federal Power Commission, with its control over oil and gas moving in interstate trade;</seg>
<seg id="1814">the Civil Aeronautics Board, with its control over airlines; and the enforcement by the Federal Reserve Board of maximum interest rates that banks may pay on time deposits, and the legal prohibition of the payment of interest on demand deposits.</seg>
<seg id="1815">These examples are on a federal level.</seg>
<seg id="1816">In addition, there has been a great proliferation of similar developments on a state and local level.</seg>
<seg id="1817">The Texas Railroad Commission, which so far as I know, has nothing to do with railroads, enforces output restrictions on oil wells, by limiting the number of days when wells may produce.</seg>
<seg id="1818">It does so in the name of conservation but in fact for the purpose of controlling prices.</seg>
<seg id="1819">More recently, it has been strongly assisted by federal import quotas on oil.</seg>
<seg id="1820">Keeping oil wells idle most of the time to keep up prices seems to me featherbedding of precisely the same kind as paying coal firemen on diesel locomotives for being idle.</seg>
<seg id="1821">Yet some representatives of business who are loudest in their condemnation of labor featherbedding as a violation of free enterpriseˇŞnotably the oil industry itselfˇŞare deafeningly silent about featherbedding in oil.</seg>
<seg id="1822">Licensure provisions, discussed in the next chapter, are another example of governmentally created and supported monopoly on a state level.</seg>
<seg id="1823">Restrictions on the number of taxicabs that can be operated exemplify similar restriction on a local level.</seg>
<seg id="1824">In New York, a medallion signifying the right to operate an independent cab now sells for something like $ 20,000 to $ 25,000;</seg>
<seg id="1825">in Philadelphia, for $ 15,000.</seg>
<seg id="1826">Another example on a local level is the enactment of building codes, ostensibly designed for public safety, but in fact generally under the control of local building trade unions or associations of private contractors.</seg>
<seg id="1827">Such restrictions are numerous and apply to a considerable variety of activities on both city and state levels.</seg>
<seg id="1828">All constitute arbitrary limitations on the ability of individuals to enter into voluntary exchanges with one another.</seg>
<seg id="1829">They simultaneously restrict freedom and promote the waste of resources.</seg>
<seg id="1830">A kind of governmentally created monopoly very different in principle from those so far considered is the grant of patents to inventors and copyrights to authors.</seg>
<seg id="1831">These are different, because they can equally be regarded as defining property rights.</seg>
<seg id="1832">In a literal sense, if I have a property right to a particular piece of land, I can be said to have a monopoly with respect to that piece of land defined and enforced by the government.</seg>
<seg id="1833">With respect to inventions and publications, the problem is whether it is desirable to establish an analogous property right.</seg>
<seg id="1834">This problem is part of the general need to use government to establish what shall and what shall not be regarded as property.</seg>
<seg id="1835">In both patents and copyrights, there is clearly a strong prima facie case for establishing property rights.</seg>
<seg id="1836">Unless this is done, the inventor will find it difficult or impossible to collect a payment for the contribution his invention makes to output.</seg>
<seg id="1837">He will, that is, confer benefits on others for which he cannot be compensated.</seg>
<seg id="1838">Hence he will have no incentive to devote the time and effort required to produce the invention.</seg>
<seg id="1839">Similar considerations apply to the writer.</seg>
<seg id="1840">At the same time, there are costs involved.</seg>
<seg id="1841">For one thing, there are many "inventions" that are not patentable.</seg>
<seg id="1842">The "inventor" of the supermarket, for example, conferred great benefits on his fellow men for which he could not charge them.</seg>
<seg id="1843">Insofar as the same kind of ability is required for the one kind of invention as for the other, the existence of patents tends to divert activity to patentable inventions.</seg>
<seg id="1844">For another, trivial patents, or patents that would be of dubious legality if contested in court, are often used as a device for maintaining private collusive arrangements that would otherwise be more difficult or impossible to maintain.</seg>
<seg id="1845">These are very superficial comments on a difficult and important problem.</seg>
<seg id="1846">Their aim is not to suggest any specific answer but only to show why patents and copyrights are in a different class from the other governmentally supported monopolies and to illustrate the problem of social policy that they raise.</seg>
<seg id="1847">One thing is clear.</seg>
<seg id="1848">The specific conditions attached to patents and copyrightsˇŞfor example, the grant of patent protection for seventeen years rather than some other periodˇŞare not a matter of principle.</seg>
<seg id="1849">They are matters of expediency to be determined by practical considerations.</seg>
<seg id="1850">I am myself inclined to believe that a much shorter period of patent protection would be preferable.</seg>
<seg id="1851">But this is a casual judgment on a subject on which there has been much detailed study and on which much more is needed.</seg>
<seg id="1852">Hence, it is deserving of little confidence.</seg>
<seg id="1853">The Sources of Monopoly</seg>
<seg id="1854">There are three major sources of monopoly:</seg>
<seg id="1855">"technical" considerations, direct and indirect governmental assistance, and private collusion.</seg>
<seg id="1856">1.Technical Considerations</seg>
<seg id="1857">As pointed out in chapter ii, monopoly arises to some extent because technical considerations make it more efficient or economical to have a single enterprise rather than many.</seg>
<seg id="1858">The most obvious example is a telephone system, water system, and the like in an individual community.</seg>
<seg id="1859">There is unfortunately no good solution for technical monopoly.</seg>
<seg id="1860">There is only a choice among three evils:</seg>
<seg id="1861">private unregulated monopoly, private monopoly regulated by the state, and government operation.</seg>
<seg id="1862">It seems impossible to state as a general proposition that one of these evils is uniformly preferable to another.</seg>
<seg id="1863">As stated in chapter ii, the great disadvantage of either governmental regulation or governmental operation of monopoly is that it is exceedingly difficult to reverse.</seg>
<seg id="1864">In consequence, I am inclined to urge that the least of the evils is private unregulated monopoly wherever this is tolerable.</seg>
<seg id="1865">Dynamic changes are highly likely to undermine it and there is at least some chance that these will be allowed to have their effect.</seg>
<seg id="1866">And even in the short run, there is generally a wider range of substitutes than there seems to be at first blush, so private enterprises are fairly narrowly limited in the extent to which it is profitable to keep prices above cost.</seg>
<seg id="1867">Moreover, as we have seen, the regulatory agencies often tend themselves to fall under the control of the producers and so prices may not be any lower with regulation than without regulation.</seg>
<seg id="1868">Fortunately, the areas in which technical considerations make monopoly a likely or a probable outcome are fairly limited.</seg>
<seg id="1869">They would offer no serious threat to the preservation of a free economy if it were not for the tendency of regulation, introduced on this ground, to spread to situations in which it is not so justified.</seg>
<seg id="1870">2.Direct and Indirect Government Assistance</seg>
<seg id="1871">Probably the most important source of monopoly power has been government assistance, direct and indirect.</seg>
<seg id="1872">Numerous examples of reasonably direct government assistance have been cited above.</seg>
<seg id="1873">The indirect assistance to monopoly consists of measures taken for other purposes which have as a largely unintended effect the imposition of limitations on potential competitors of existing firms.</seg>
<seg id="1874">Perhaps the three clearest examples are tariffs, tax legislation, and law enforcement and legislation with respect to labor disputes.</seg>
<seg id="1875">Tariffs have of course been imposed largely to "protect" domestic industries, which means to impose handicaps on potential competitors.</seg>
<seg id="1876">They always interfere with the freedom of individuals to engage in voluntary exchange.</seg>
<seg id="1877">After all, the liberal takes the individual, not the nation or citizen of a particular nation, as his unit.</seg>
<seg id="1878">Hence he regards it just as much a violation of freedom if citizens of the United States and Switzerland are prevented from consummating an exchange that would be mutually advantageous as if two citizens of the United States are prevented from doing so.</seg>
<seg id="1879">Tariffs need not produce monopoly.</seg>
<seg id="1880">If the market for the protected industry is sufficiently large and technical conditions permit many firms, there can be effective competition domestically in the protected industry, as in the United States in textiles.</seg>
<seg id="1881">Clearly, however, tariffs do foster monopoly.</seg>
<seg id="1882">It is far easier for a few firms than for many to collude to fix prices, and it is generally easier for enterprises in the same country to collude than for enterprises in different countries.</seg>
<seg id="1883">Britain was protected by free trade from widespread monopoly during the nineteenth and early twentieth centuries, despite the relatively small size of her domestic market and the large scale of many firms.</seg>
<seg id="1884">Monopoly has become a much more serious problem in Britain since free trade was abandoned, first after World War I and then more extensively in the early 1930's.</seg>
<seg id="1885">The effects of tax legislation have been even more indirect yet not less important.</seg>
<seg id="1886">A major element has been the linkage of the corporate and individual income tax combined with the special treatment of capital gains under the individual income tax.</seg>
<seg id="1887">Let us suppose a corporation earns an income of $ 1 million over and above corporate taxes.</seg>
<seg id="1888">If it pays the whole million dollars to its stockholders as dividends, they must include it as part of their taxable income.</seg>
<seg id="1889">Suppose they would, on the average, have to pay 50 per cent of this additional income as income tax.</seg>
<seg id="1890">They would then have available only $ 500, 000 to spend on consumption or to save and invest.</seg>
<seg id="1891">If instead the corporation pays no cash dividends to its stockholders, it has the whole million dollars to invest internally.</seg>
<seg id="1892">Such reinvestment will tend to raise the capital value of its stock. stockholders who would have saved the funds if distributed can simply hold the stock and postpone all taxes until they sell the stock.</seg>
<seg id="1893">They, as well as others who sell at an earlier date to realize income for consumption, will pay tax at capital gains rates, which are lower than rates on regular income.</seg>
<seg id="1894">This tax structure encourages retention of corporate earnings.</seg>
<seg id="1895">Even if the return that can be earned internally is appreciably less than the return that the stockholder himself could earn by investing the funds externally, it may pay to invest internally because of the tax saving.</seg>
<seg id="1896">This leads to a waste of capital, to its use for less productive rather than more productive purposes.</seg>
<seg id="1897">It has been a major reason for the post-World-War-II tendency toward horizontal diversification as firms have sought outlets for their earnings.</seg>
<seg id="1898">It is also a great source of strength for established corporations relative to new enterprises.</seg>
<seg id="1899">The established corporations can be less productive than new enterprises, yet their stockholders have an incentive to invest in them rather than to have the income paid out so that they can invest it in new enterprises through the capital market.</seg>
<seg id="1900">A major source of labor monopoly has been government assistance.</seg>
<seg id="1901">Licensure provisions, building codes, and the like, discussed above have been one source.</seg>
<seg id="1902">Legislation granting special immunities to labor unions, such as exemption from the anti-trust laws, restrictions on union responsibility, the right to appear before special tribunals, and so on, are a second source.</seg>
<seg id="1903">Perhaps of equal or greater importance than either is a general climate of opinion and law enforcement applying different standards to actions taken in the course of a labor dispute than to the same actions under other circumstances.</seg>
<seg id="1904">If men turn cars over, or destroy property, out of sheer wickedness or in the course of exacting private vengeance, not a hand will be lifted to protect them from the legal consequences.</seg>
<seg id="1905">If they commit the same acts in the course of labor dispute, they may well get off scot free.</seg>
<seg id="1906">Union actions involving actual or potential physical violence or coercion could hardly take place if it were not for the unspoken acquiescence of the authorities.</seg>
<seg id="1907">3.Private Collusion</seg>
<seg id="1908">The final source of monopoly is private collusion.</seg>
<seg id="1909">As Adam Smith says, "People of the same trade seldom meet together, even for merriment and diversion, but the conversation ends in a conspiracy against the public, or in some contrivance to raise prices."</seg>
<seg id="1910">Such collusion or private cartel arrangements are therefore constantly arising.</seg>
<seg id="1911">However, they are generally unstable and of brief duration unless they can call government to their assistance.</seg>
<seg id="1912">The establishment of the cartel, by raising prices, makes it more profitable for outsiders to enter the industry.</seg>
<seg id="1913">Moreover, since the higher price can be established only by the participants' restricting their output below the level that they would like to produce at the fixed price, there is an incentive for each one separately to undercut the price in order to expand output.</seg>
<seg id="1914">Each one, of course, hopes that the others will abide by the agreement.</seg>
<seg id="1915">It takes only one or at most a few "chiselers"ˇŞwho are indeed public benefactorsˇŞto break the cartel.</seg>
<seg id="1916">In the absence of government assistance in enforcing the cartel, they are almost sure to succeed fairly promptly.</seg>
<seg id="1917">The major role of our antitrust laws has been to inhibit such private collusion.</seg>
<seg id="1918">Their main contribution in this respect has been less through actual prosecutions than by their indirect effects.</seg>
<seg id="1919">They have ruled out the obvious collusive devicesˇŞsuch as the public get-together for this specific purposeˇŞand have therefore made collusion more expensive.</seg>
<seg id="1920">More important, they have reaffirmed common law doctrine that combinations in restraint of trade are unenforceable in the courts.</seg>
<seg id="1921">In various European countries, the courts will enforce an agreement entered into by a group of enterprises to sell only through a joint selling agency, committing the enterprises to pay specified penalties if they violate the agreement.</seg>
<seg id="1922">In the United States, such an agreement would not be enforceable in the courts.</seg>
<seg id="1923">This difference is one of the major reasons why cartels have been more stable and widespread in European countries than in the United States.</seg>
<seg id="1924">Appropriate Government Policy</seg>
<seg id="1925">The first and most urgent necessity in the area of government policy is the elimination of those measures which directly support monopoly, whether enterprise monopoly or labor monopoly, and an even-handed enforcement of the laws on enterprises and labor unions alike.</seg>
<seg id="1926">Both should be subjected to the anti-trust laws;</seg>
<seg id="1927">both should be treated alike with respect to laws about the destruction of property and about interference with private activities.</seg>
<seg id="1928">Beyond this, the most important and effective step toward the reduction of monopoly power would be an extensive reform of the tax laws.</seg>
<seg id="1929">The corporate tax should be abolished.</seg>
<seg id="1930">Whether this is done or not, corporations should be required to attribute to individual stockholders earnings which are not paid out as dividends.</seg>
<seg id="1931">That is, when the corporation sends out a dividend check, it should also send a statement saying, "In addition to this dividend ofˇŞˇŞˇŞcents per share, your corporation also earnedˇŞˇŞˇŞcents per share which was reinvested."</seg>
<seg id="1932">The individual stockholder should then be required to report the attributed but undistributed earnings on his tax return as well as the dividend.</seg>
<seg id="1933">Corporations would still be free to plough back as much as they wish, but they would have no incentive to do so except the proper incentive that they could earn more internally than the stockholder could earn externally.</seg>
<seg id="1934">Few measures would do more to invigorate capital markets, to stimulate enterprise, and to promote effective competition.</seg>
<seg id="1935">Of course, so long as the individual income tax is as highly graduated as it is now, there is strong pressure to find devices to evade its impact.</seg>
<seg id="1936">In this way as well as directly, the highly graduated income tax constitutes a serious impediment to the efficient use of our resources.</seg>
<seg id="1937">The appropriate solution is the drastic scaling down of the higher rates, combined with an elimination of the avoidance devices that have been incorporated in the law.</seg>
<seg id="1938">Social Responsibility of Business and Labor</seg>
<seg id="1939">The view has been gaining widespread acceptance that corporate officials and labor leaders have a "social responsibility" that goes beyond serving the interest of their stockholders or their members.</seg>
<seg id="1940">This view shows a fundamental misconception of the character and nature of a free economy.</seg>
<seg id="1941">In such an economy, there is one and only one social responsibility of businessˇŞto use its resources and engage in activities designed to increase its profits so long as it stays within the rules of the game, which is to say, engages in open and free competition, without deception or fraud.</seg>
<seg id="1942">Similarly, the "social responsibility" of labor leaders is to serve the interests of the members of their unions.</seg>
<seg id="1943">It is the responsibility of the rest of us to establish a framework of law such that an individual in pursuing his own interest is, to quote Adam Smith again, "led by an invisible hand to promote an end which was no part of his intention.</seg>
<seg id="1944">Nor is it always the worse for the society that it was no part of it.</seg>
<seg id="1945">By pursuing his own interest, he frequently promotes that of the society more effectually than when he really intends to promote it.</seg>
<seg id="1946">I have never known much good done by those who affected to trade for the public good."</seg>
<seg id="1947">Few trends could so thoroughly undermine the very foundations of our free society as the acceptance by corporate officials of a social responsibility other than to make as much money for their stockholders as possible.</seg>
<seg id="1948">This is a fundamentally subversive doctrine.</seg>
<seg id="1949">If businessmen do have a social responsibility other than making maximum profits for stockholders, how are they to know what it is?</seg>
<seg id="1950">Can self-selected private individuals decide what the social interest is?</seg>
<seg id="1951">Can they decide how great a burden I bid they are justified in placing on themselves or their stockholders to serve that social interest?</seg>
<seg id="1952">Is it tolerable that these public functions of taxation, expenditure, and control be exercised by the people who happen at the moment to be in charge of particular enterprises, chosen for those posts by strictly private groups?</seg>
<seg id="1953">If businessmen are civil servants rather than the employees of their stockholders then in a democracy they will, sooner or later, be chosen by the public techniques of election and appointment.</seg>
<seg id="1954">And long before this occurs, their decision-making power will have been taken away from them.</seg>
<seg id="1955">A dramatic illustration was the cancellation of a steel price increase by U.S. Steel in April 1962 through the medium of a public display of anger by President Kennedy and threats of reprisals on levels ranging from anti-trust suits to examination of the tax reports of steel executives.</seg>
<seg id="1956">This was a striking episode because of the public display of the vast powers concentrated in Washington.</seg>
<seg id="1957">We were all made aware of how much of the power needed for a police state was already available.</seg>
<seg id="1958">It illustrates the present point as well.</seg>
<seg id="1959">If the price of steel is a public decision, as the doctrine of social responsibility declares, then it cannot be permitted to be made privately.</seg>
<seg id="1960">The particular aspect of the doctrine which this example illustrates, and which has been most prominent recently, is an alleged social responsibility of business and labor to keep prices and wage rates down in order to avoid price inflation.</seg>
<seg id="1961">Suppose that at a time when there was upward pressure on pricesˇŞultimately of course reflecting an increase in the stock of money ˇŞevery businessman and labor leader were to accept this responsibility and suppose all could succeed in keeping any price from rising, so we had voluntary price and wage control without open inflation.</seg>
<seg id="1962">What would be the result?</seg>
<seg id="1963">Clearly product shortages, labor shortages, gray markets, black markets.</seg>
<seg id="1964">If prices are not allowed to ration goods and workers, there must be some other means to do so.</seg>
<seg id="1965">Can the alternative rationing schemes be private?</seg>
<seg id="1966">Perhaps for a time in a small and unimportant area.</seg>
<seg id="1967">But if the goods involved are many and important, there will necessarily be pressure, and probably irresistible pressure, for governmental rationing of goods, a governmental wage policy, and governmental measures for allocating and distributing labor.</seg>
<seg id="1968">Price controls, whether legal or voluntary, if effectively enforced would eventually lead to the destruction of the free-enterprise system and its replacement by a centrally controlled system.</seg>
<seg id="1969">And it would not even be effective in preventing inflation.</seg>
<seg id="1970">History offers ample evidence that what determines the average level of prices and wages is the amount of money in the economy and not the greediness of businessmen or of workers.</seg>
<seg id="1971">Governments ask for the self-restraint of business and labor because of their inability to manage their own affairsˇŞwhich includes the control of moneyˇŞ and the natural human tendency to pass the buck.</seg>
<seg id="1972">One topic in the area of social responsibility that I feel duty-bound to touch on, because it affects my own personal interests, has been the claim that business should contribute to the support of charitable activities and especially to universities.</seg>
<seg id="1973">Such giving by corporations is an inappropriate use of corporate funds in a free-enterprise society.</seg>
<seg id="1974">The corporation is an instrument of the stockholders who own it.</seg>
<seg id="1975">If the corporation makes a contribution, it prevents the individual stockholder from himself deciding how he should dispose of his funds.</seg>
<seg id="1976">With the corporation tax and the deductibility of contributions, stockholders may of course want the corporation to make a gift on their behalf, since this would enable them to make a larger gift.</seg>
<seg id="1977">The best solution would be the abolition of the corporate tax.</seg>
<seg id="1978">But so long as there is a corporate tax, there is no justification for permitting deductions for contributions to charitable and educational institutions.</seg>
<seg id="1979">Such contributions should be made by the individuals who are the ultimate owners of property in our society.</seg>
<seg id="1980">People who urge extension of the deductibility of this kind of corporate contribution in the name of free enterprise are fundamentally working against their own interest.</seg>
<seg id="1981">A major complaint made frequently against modern business is that it involves the separation of ownership and controlˇŞthat the corporation has become a social institution that is a law unto itself, with irresponsible executives who do not serve the interests of their stockholders.</seg>
<seg id="1982">This charge is not true.</seg>
<seg id="1983">But the direction in which policy is now moving, of permitting corporations to make contributions for charitable purposes and allowing deductions for income tax, is a step in the direction of creating a true divorce between ownership and control and of undermining the basic nature and character of our society.</seg>
<seg id="1984">It is a step away from an individualistic society and toward the corporate state.</seg>
<seg id="1985">Chapter IXˇŞ Occupational Licensure</seg>
<seg id="1986">The overthrow of the medieval guild system was an indispensable early step in the rise of freedom in the Western world.</seg>
<seg id="1987">It was a sign of the triumph of liberal ideas, and widely recognized as such, that by the mid-nineteenth century, in Britain, the United States, and to a lesser extent on the continent of Europe, men could pursue whatever trade or occupation they wished without the by your-leave of any governmental or quasi-governmental authority.</seg>
<seg id="1988">In more recent decades, there has been a retrogression, an increasing tendency for particular occupations to be restricted to individuals licensed to practice them by the state.</seg>
<seg id="1989">These restrictions on the freedom of individuals to use their resources as they wish are important in their own right.</seg>
<seg id="1990">In addition, they provide still a different class of problems to which we can apply the principles developed in the first two chapters.</seg>
<seg id="1991">I shall discuss first the general problem and then a particular example, restrictions on the practice of medicine.</seg>
<seg id="1992">The reason for choosing medicine is that it seems desirable to discuss the restrictions for which the strongest case can be madeˇŞthere is not much to be learned by knocking down straw men.</seg>
<seg id="1993">I suspect that most people, possibly even most liberals, believe that it is desirable to restrict the practice of medicine to people who are licensed by the state.</seg>
<seg id="1994">I agree that the case for licensure is stronger for medicine than for most other fields.</seg>
<seg id="1995">Yet the conclusions I shall reach are that liberal principles do not justify licensure even in medicine and that in practice the results of state licensure in medicine have been undesirable.</seg>
<seg id="1996">Ubiquity of Governmental Restrictions on Economic Activities</seg>
<seg id="1997">Men May Engage inLicensure is a special case of a much more general and exceedingly widespread phenomenon, namely, edicts that individuals may not engage in particular economic activities except under conditions laid down by a constituted authority of the state.</seg>
<seg id="1998">Medieval guilds were a particular example of an explicit system for specifying which individuals should be permitted to follow particular pursuits.</seg>
<seg id="1999">The Indian caste system is another example.</seg>
<seg id="2000">To a considerable extent in the caste system, to a lesser extent in the guilds, the restrictions were enforced by general social customs rather than explicitly by government.</seg>
<seg id="2001">A widespread notion about the caste system is that every person's occupation is completely determined by the caste into which he is born.</seg>
<seg id="2002">It is obvious to an economist that this is an impossible system, since it prescribes a rigid distribution of persons among occupations determined entirely by birthrates and not at all by conditions of demand.</seg>
<seg id="2003">Of course, this is not the way the system worked.</seg>
<seg id="2004">What was true, and to some measure still is, was that a limited number of occupations were reserved to members of certain castes, but not every member of those castes followed those occupations.</seg>
<seg id="2005">There were some general occupations, such as general agricultural work, which members of various castes might engage in.</seg>
<seg id="2006">These permitted an adjustment of the supply of people in different occupations to the demand for their services.</seg>
<seg id="2007">Currently, tariffs, fair-trade laws, import quotas, production quotas, trade union restrictions on employment and so on are examples of similar phenomena.</seg>
<seg id="2008">In all these cases, governmental authority determines the conditions under which particular individuals can engage in particular activities, which is to say, the terms on which some individuals are permitted to make arrangements with others.</seg>
<seg id="2009">The common feature of these examples, as well as of licensure, is that the legislation is enacted on behalf of a producer group.</seg>
<seg id="2010">For licensure, the producer group is generally a craft.</seg>
<seg id="2011">For the other examples, it may be a group producing a particular product which wants a tariff, a group of small retailers who would like to be protected from competition by the ''chiseling" chain stores, or a group of oil producers, of farmers, or of steel workers.</seg>
<seg id="2012">Occupational licensure is by now very widespread.</seg>
<seg id="2013">According to Walter Gellhorn, who has written the best brief survey I know, "By 1952 more than 80 separate occupations exclusive of 'owner businesses,' like restaurants and taxicab companies, had been licensed by state law;</seg>
