<seg id="2014">and in addition to the state laws there are municipal ordinances in abundance, not to mention the federal statutes that require the licensing of such diverse occupations as radio operators and stockyard commission agents.</seg>
<seg id="2015">As long ago as 1938 a single state, North Carolina, had extended its law to 60 occupations.</seg>
<seg id="2016">One may not be surprised to learn that pharmacists, accountants, and dentists have been reached by state law as have sanitarians and psychologists, assayers and architects, veterinarians and librarians.</seg>
<seg id="2017">But with what joy of discovery does one learn about the licensing of threshing machine operators and dealers in scrap tobacco?</seg>
<seg id="2018">What of egg graders and guide dog trainers, pest controllers and yacht salesmen, tree surgeons and well diggers, tile layers and potato growers?</seg>
<seg id="2019">And what of the hypertrichologists who are licensed in Connecticut, where they remove excessive and unsightly hair with the solemnity appropriate to their high sounding title?"</seg>
<seg id="2020">In the arguments that seek to persuade legislatures to enact such licensure provisions, the justification is always said to be the necessity of protecting the public interest.</seg>
<seg id="2021">However, the pressure on the legislature to license an occupation rarely comes from the members of the public who have been mulcted or in other ways abused by members of the occupation.</seg>
<seg id="2022">On the contrary, the pressure invariably comes from members of the occupation itself.</seg>
<seg id="2023">Of course, they are more aware than others of how much they exploit the customer and so perhaps they can lay claim to expert knowledge.</seg>
<seg id="2024">Similarly, the arrangements made for licensure almost invariably involve control by members of the occupation which is to be licensed.</seg>
<seg id="2025">Again, this is in some ways quite natural.</seg>
<seg id="2026">If the occupation of plumbing is to be restricted to those who have the requisite capacity and skills to provide good service for their customers, clearly only plumbers are capable of judging who should be licensed.</seg>
<seg id="2027">Consequently, the board or other body that grants licenses is almost invariably made up largely of plumbers or pharmacists or physicians or whatever may be the particular occupation licensed.</seg>
<seg id="2028">Gellhorn points out that "Seventy-five per cent of the occupational licensing boards at work in this country today are composed exclusively of licensed practitioners in the respective occupations.</seg>
<seg id="2029">These men and women, most of whom are only part-time officials, may have a direct economic interest in many of the decisions they make concerning admission requirements and the definition of standards to be observed by licensees.</seg>
<seg id="2030">More importantly, they are as a rule directly representative of organized groups within the occupations.</seg>
<seg id="2031">Ordinarily they are nominated by these groups as a step toward a gubernatorial or other appointment that is frequently a mere formality.</seg>
<seg id="2032">Often the formality is dispensed with entirely, appointment being made directly by the occupational associationˇŞas happens, for example, with the embalmers in North Carolina, the den1 Walter Gellhorn, Individual Freedom and Governmental Restraints</seg>
<seg id="2033">Licensure therefore frequently establishes essentially the medieval guild kind of regulation in which the state assigns power to the members of the profession.</seg>
<seg id="2034">In practice, the considerations taken into account in determining who shall get a license often involve matters that, so far as a layman can see, have no relation whatsoever to professional competence.</seg>
<seg id="2035">This is not surprising.</seg>
<seg id="2036">If a few individuals are going to decide whether other individuals may pursue an occupation, all sorts of irrelevant considerations are likely to enter.</seg>
<seg id="2037">Just what the irrelevant considerations will be, will depend on the personalities of the members of the licensing board and the mood of the time.</seg>
<seg id="2038">Gellhorn notes the extent to which a loyalty oath was required of various occupations when the fear of communist subversion was sweeping the country.</seg>
<seg id="2039">He writes, "A Texas statute of 1952 requires each applicant for a pharmacist's license to swear that 'he is not a member of the Communist Party or affiliated with such party, and that he does not believe in and is neither a member of nor supports any group or organization that believes in, furthers or teaches the overthrow of the United States Government by force or any illegal or unconstitutional methods.'</seg>
<seg id="2040">The relationship between this oath on the one hand and, on the other, the public health which is the interest purportedly protected by the licensing of pharmacists, is somewhat obscure.</seg>
<seg id="2041">No more apparent is the justification for requiring professional boxers and wrestlers in Indiana to swear that they are not subversive . . .</seg>
<seg id="2042">A junior high school teacher of music, having been forced to resign after being identified as a Communist, had difficulty becoming a piano tuner in the District of Columbia because, forsooth, he was 'under Communist discipline.'</seg>
<seg id="2043">Veterinarians in the state of Washington may not minister to an ailing cow or cat unless they have first signed a non-Communist oath."</seg>
<seg id="2044">Whatever one's attitude towards communism, any relationship between the requirements imposed and the qualities which the licensure is intended to assure is rather far-fetched.</seg>
<seg id="2045">The extent to which such requirements go is sometimes little short of ludicrous.</seg>
<seg id="2046">A few more quotations from Gellhorn may provide a touch of comic relief.</seg>
<seg id="2047">One of the most amusing sets of regulations is that laid down for barbers, a trade that is licensed in many places.</seg>
<seg id="2048">Here is an example from a law which was declared invalid by Maryland courts, though similar language can be found in statutes of other states which were declared legal.</seg>
<seg id="2049">"The court was depressed rather than impressed by a legislative command that neophyte barbers must receive formal instruction in the 'scientific fundamentals for barbering, hygiene, bacteriology, histology of the hair, skin, nails, muscles and nerves, structure of the head, face and neck, elementary chemistry relating to sterilization and antiseptics, disease of the skin, hair, glands and nails, haircutting, shaving and arranging, dressing, coloring, bleaching, and tinting of the hair'."</seg>
<seg id="2050">One more quotation on the barbers:</seg>
<seg id="2051">"Of eighteen representative states included in a study of barbering regulations in 1929, not one then commanded an aspirant to be a graduate of a 'barber college,' though apprenticeship was necessary in all.</seg>
<seg id="2052">Today, the states typically insist upon graduation from a barbering school that provides not less (and often much more) than one thousand hours of instruction in 'theoretical subjects' such as sterilization of instruments, and this must still be followed by apprenticeship."</seg>
<seg id="2053">I trust these quotations make it clear that the problem of licensing of occupations is something more than a trivial illustration of the problem of state intervention, that it is already in this country a serious infringement on the freedom of individuals to pursue activities of their own choice, and that it threatens to become a much more serious one with the continual pressure upon legislatures to extend it.</seg>
<seg id="2054">Before discussing the advantages and disadvantages of license</seg>
<seg id="2055">The declaration by a large number of different state legislatures that barbers must be approved by a committee of other barbers is hardly persuasive evidence that there is in fact a public interest in having such legislation.</seg>
<seg id="2056">Surely the explanation is different;</seg>
<seg id="2057">it is that a producer group tends to be more concentrated politically than a consumer group.</seg>
<seg id="2058">This is an obvious point often made and yet one whose importance cannot be overstressed.</seg>
<seg id="2059">Each of us is a producer and also a consumer.</seg>
<seg id="2060">However, we are much more specialized and devote a much larger fraction of our attention to our activity as a producer than as a consumer.</seg>
<seg id="2061">We consume literally thousands if not millions of items.</seg>
<seg id="2062">The result is that people in the same trade, like barbers or physicians, all have an intense interest in the specific problems of this trade and are willing to devote considerable energy to doing something about them.</seg>
<seg id="2063">On the other hand, those of us who use barbers at all, get barbered infrequently and spend only a minor fraction of our income in barber shops.</seg>
<seg id="2064">Our interest is casual.</seg>
<seg id="2065">Hardly any of us are willing to devote much time going to the legislature in order to testify against the iniquity of restricting the practice of barbering.</seg>
<seg id="2066">The same point holds for tariffs.</seg>
<seg id="2067">The groups that think they have a special interest in particular tariffs are concentrated groups to whom the issue makes a great deal of difference.</seg>
<seg id="2068">The public interest is widely dispersed.</seg>
<seg id="2069">In consequence, in the absence of any general arrangements to offset the pressure of special interests, producer groups will invariably have a much stronger influence on legislative action and the powers that be than will the diverse, widely spread consumer interest.</seg>
<seg id="2070">Indeed from this point of view, the puzzle is not why we have so many silly licensure laws, but why we don't have far more.</seg>
<seg id="2071">The puzzle is how we ever succeeded in getting the relative freedom from government controls over the productive activities of individuals that we have had and still have in this country, and that other countries have had as well.</seg>
<seg id="2072">The only way that I can see to offset special producer groups is to establish a general presumption against the state undertaking certain kinds of activities.</seg>
<seg id="2073">Only if there is a general recognition that governmental activities should be severely limited with respect to a class of cases, can the burden of proof be put strongly enough on those who would depart from this general presumption to give a reasonable hope of limiting the spread of special measures to further special interests.</seg>
<seg id="2074">This point is one we have adverted to time and again.</seg>
<seg id="2075">It is of a piece with the argument for the Bill of Rights and for a rule to govern monetary policy and fiscal policy.</seg>
<seg id="2076">Policy Issues Raised by Licensure</seg>
<seg id="2077">It is important to distinguish three different levels of control:</seg>
<seg id="2078">first, registration;</seg>
<seg id="2079">second, certification; third, licensing.</seg>
<seg id="2080">By registration, I mean an arrangement under which individuals are required to list their names in some official register if they engage in certain kinds of activities.</seg>
<seg id="2081">There is no provision for denying the right to engage in the activity to anyone who is willing to list his name.</seg>
<seg id="2082">He may be charged a fee, either as a registration fee or as a scheme of taxation.</seg>
<seg id="2083">The second level is certification.</seg>
<seg id="2084">The governmental agency may certify that an individual has certain skills but may not prevent, in any way, the practice of any occupation using these skills by people who do not have such a certificate.</seg>
<seg id="2085">One example is accountancy.</seg>
<seg id="2086">In most states, anybody can be an accountant, whether he is a certified public accountant or not, but only those people who have passed a particular test can put the title CPA after their names or can put a sign in their offices saying they are certified public accountants.</seg>
<seg id="2087">Certification is frequently only an intermediate stage.</seg>
<seg id="2088">In many states, there has been a tendency to restrict an increasing range of activities to certified public accountants.</seg>
<seg id="2089">With respect to such activities there is licensure, not certification.</seg>
<seg id="2090">In some states, "architect" is a title which can be used only by those who have passed a specified examination.</seg>
<seg id="2091">This is certification.</seg>
<seg id="2092">It does not prevent anyone else from going into the business of advising people for a fee how to build houses.</seg>
<seg id="2093">The third stage is licensing proper.</seg>
<seg id="2094">This is an arrangement under which one must obtain a license from a recognized authority in order to engage in the occupation.</seg>
<seg id="2095">The license is more than a formality.</seg>
<seg id="2096">It requires some demonstration of competence or the meeting of some tests ostensibly designed to insure competence, and anyone who does not have a license is not authorized to practice and is subject to a fine or a jail sentence if he does engage in practice.</seg>
<seg id="2097">The question I want to consider is this:</seg>
<seg id="2098">under what circumstances, if any, can we justify the one or the other of these steps?</seg>
<seg id="2099">There are three different grounds on which it seems to me registration can be justified consistently with liberal principles.</seg>
<seg id="2100">First, it may assist in the pursuit of other aims.</seg>
<seg id="2101">Let me illustrate.</seg>
<seg id="2102">The police are often concerned with acts of violence.</seg>
<seg id="2103">After the event, it is desirable to find out who had access to firearms.</seg>
<seg id="2104">Before the event, it is desirable to prevent firearms from getting into the hands of people who are likely to use them for criminal purposes.</seg>
<seg id="2105">It may assist in the pursuit of this aim to register stores selling firearms.</seg>
<seg id="2106">Of course, if I may revert to a point made several times in earlier chapters, it is never enough to say that there might be a justification along these lines, in order to conclude that there is justification.</seg>
<seg id="2107">It is necessary to set up a balance sheet of the advantages and disadvantages in the light of liberal principles.</seg>
<seg id="2108">All I am now saying is that this consideration might in some cases justify overriding the general presumption against requiring the registration of people.</seg>
<seg id="2109">Second, registration is sometimes a device to facilitate taxation and nothing more.</seg>
<seg id="2110">The questions at issue then become whether the particular tax is an appropriate method to raise revenue for financing government services regarded as necessary, and whether registration facilitates the collection of taxes.</seg>
<seg id="2111">It may do so either because a tax is imposed on the person who registers, or because the person who registers is used as a tax collector.</seg>
<seg id="2112">For example, in collecting a sales tax imposed on various items of consumption, it is necessary to have a register or list of all the places selling goods subject to the tax.</seg>
<seg id="2113">Third, and this is the one possible justification for registration which is close to our main interest, registration may be a means to protect consumers against fraud.</seg>
<seg id="2114">In general, liberal principles assign to the state the power to enforce contracts, and fraud involves the violation of a contract.</seg>
<seg id="2115">It is, of course, dubious that one should go very far to protect in advance against fraud because of the interference with voluntary contracts involved in doing so.</seg>
<seg id="2116">But I do not think that one can rule out on grounds of principle the possibility that there may be certain activities that are so likely to give rise to fraud as to render it desirable to have in advance a list of people known to be pursuing this activity.</seg>
<seg id="2117">Perhaps one example along these lines is the registration of taxicab drivers.</seg>
<seg id="2118">A taxicab driver picking up a person at night may be in a particularly good position to steal from him.</seg>
<seg id="2119">To inhibit such practices, it may be desirable to have a list of names of people who are engaged in the taxicab business, to give each a number, and to require that this number be put in the cab so that anyone molested need only remember the number of the cab.</seg>
<seg id="2120">This involves simply the use of the police power to protect individuals against violence on the part of other individuals and may be the most convenient method of doing so.</seg>
<seg id="2121">Certification is much more difficult to justify.</seg>
<seg id="2122">The reason is that this is something the private market generally can do for itself.</seg>
<seg id="2123">This problem is the same for products as for people's services.</seg>
<seg id="2124">There are private certification agencies in many areas that certify the competence of a person or the quality of a particular product.</seg>
<seg id="2125">The Good Housekeeping seal is a private certification arrangement.</seg>
<seg id="2126">For industrial products there are private testing laboratories that will certify to the quality of a particular product.</seg>
<seg id="2127">For consumer products, there are consumer testing agencies of which Consumer's Union and Consumer's Research are the best known in the United States.</seg>
<seg id="2128">Better Business Bureaus are voluntary organizations that certify the quality of particular dealers.</seg>
<seg id="2129">Technical schools, colleges, and universities certify the quality of their graduates.</seg>
<seg id="2130">One function of retailers and department stores is to certify the quality of the many items they sell.</seg>
<seg id="2131">The consumer develops confidence in the store, and the store in turn has an incentive to earn this confidence by investigating the quality of the items it sells.</seg>
<seg id="2132">One can however argue that in some cases, or perhaps even in many, voluntary certification will not be carried as far as individuals would be willing to pay for carrying it because of the difficulty of keeping the certification confidential.</seg>
<seg id="2133">The issue is essentially the one involved in patents and copyrights, namely, whether individuals are in a position to capture the value of the services that they render to others.</seg>
<seg id="2134">If I go into the business of certifying people, there may be no efficient way in which I can require you to pay for my certification.</seg>
<seg id="2135">If I sell my certification information to one person, how can I keep him from passing it on to others?</seg>
<seg id="2136">Consequently, it may not be possible to get effective voluntary exchange with respect to certification, even though this is a service that people would be willing to pay for if they had to.</seg>
<seg id="2137">One way to get around this problem, as we get around other kinds of neighborhood effects, is to have governmental certification.</seg>
<seg id="2138">Another possible justification for certification is on monopoly grounds.</seg>
<seg id="2139">There are some technical monopoly aspects to certification, since the cost of making a certification is largely independent of the number of people to whom the information is transmitted.</seg>
<seg id="2140">However, it is by no means clear that monopoly is inevitable.</seg>
<seg id="2141">Licensure seems to me still more difficult to justify.</seg>
<seg id="2142">It goes still farther in the direction of trenching upon the rights of individuals to enter into voluntary contracts.</seg>
<seg id="2143">Nonetheless, there are some justifications given for licensure that the liberal will have to recognize as within his own conception of appropriate government action, though, as always, the advantages have to be weighed against the disadvantages.</seg>
<seg id="2144">The main argument that is relevant to a liberal is the existence of neighborhood effects.</seg>
<seg id="2145">The simplest and most obvious example is the "incompetent" physician who produces an epidemic.</seg>
<seg id="2146">Insofar as he harms only his patient, that is simply a question of voluntary contract and exchange between the patient and his physician.</seg>
<seg id="2147">On this score, there is no ground for intervention.</seg>
<seg id="2148">However, it can be argued that if the physician treats his patient badly, he may unleash an epidemic that will cause harm to third parties who are not involved in the immediate transaction.</seg>
<seg id="2149">In such a case, it is conceivable that everybody, including even the potential patient and physician, would be willing to submit to the restriction of the practice of medicine to "competent" people in order to prevent such epidemics from occurring.</seg>
<seg id="2150">In practice, the major argument given for licensure by its proponents is not this one, which has some appeal to a liberal, but rather a strictly paternalistic argument that has little or no appeal.</seg>
<seg id="2151">Individuals, it is said, are incapable of choosing their own servants adequately, their own physician or plumber or barber.</seg>
<seg id="2152">In order for a man to choose a physician intelligently, he would have to be a physician himself.</seg>
<seg id="2153">Most of us, it is said, are therefore incompetent and we must be protected against our own ignorance.</seg>
<seg id="2154">This amounts to saying that we in our capacity as voters must protect ourselves in our capacity as consumers against our own ignorance, by seeing to it that people are not served by incompetent physicians or plumbers or barbers.</seg>
<seg id="2155">So far, I have been listing the arguments for registration, certification, and licensing.</seg>
<seg id="2156">In all three cases, it is clear that there are also strong social costs to be set against any of these advantages.</seg>
<seg id="2157">Some of these social costs have already been suggested and I shall illustrate them in more detail for medicine, but it may be worth recording them here in general form.</seg>
<seg id="2158">The most obvious social cost is that any one of these measures, whether it be registration, certification, or licensure, almost inevitably becomes a tool in the hands of a special producer group to obtain a monopoly position at the expense of the rest of the public.</seg>
<seg id="2159">There is no way to avoid this result.</seg>
<seg id="2160">One can devise one or another set of procedural controls designed to avert this outcome, but none is likely to overcome the problem that arises out of the greater concentration of producer than of consumer interest.</seg>
<seg id="2161">The people who are most concerned with any such arrangement, who will press most for its enforcement and be most concerned with its administration, will be the people in the particular occupation or trade involved.</seg>
<seg id="2162">They will inevitably press for the extension of registration to certification and of certification to licensure.</seg>
<seg id="2163">Once licensure is attained, the people who might develop an interest in undermining the regulations are kept from exerting their influence.</seg>
<seg id="2164">They don't get a license, must therefore go into other occupations, and will lose interest.</seg>
<seg id="2165">The result is invariably control over entry by members of the occupation itself and hence the establishment of a monopoly position.</seg>
<seg id="2166">Certification is much less harmful in this respect.</seg>
<seg id="2167">If the certified ''abuse" their special certificates;</seg>
<seg id="2168">if, in certifying newcomers, members of the trade impose unnecessarily stringent requirements and reduce the number of practitioners too much, the price differential between certified and noncertified will become sufficiently large to induce the public to use non-certified practitioners.</seg>
<seg id="2169">In technical terms, the elasticity of demand for the services of certified practitioners will be fairly large, and the limits within which they can exploit the rest of the public by taking advantage of their special position will be rather narrow.</seg>
<seg id="2170">In consequence, certification without licensure is a half-way house that maintains a good deal of protection against monopolization.</seg>
<seg id="2171">It also has its disadvantages, but it is worth noting that the usual arguments for licensure, and in particular the paternalistic arguments, are satisfied almost entirely by certification alone.</seg>
<seg id="2172">If the argument is that we are too ignorant to judge good practitioners, all that is needed is to make the relevant information available.</seg>
<seg id="2173">If, in full knowledge, we still want to go to someone who is not certified, that is our business;</seg>
<seg id="2174">we cannot complain that we did not have the information.</seg>
<seg id="2175">Since arguments for licensure made by people who are not members of the occupation can be satisfied so fully by certification, I personally find it difficult to see any case for which licensure rather than certification can be justified.Even registration has significant social costs.</seg>
<seg id="2176">It is an important first step in the direction of a system in which every individual has to carry an identity card, every individual has to inform authorities what he plans to do before he does it.</seg>
<seg id="2177">Moreover, as already noted, registration tends to be the first step toward certification and licensure.</seg>
<seg id="2178">Medical Licensure</seg>
<seg id="2179">The medical profession is one in which practice of the profession has for a long time been restricted to people with licenses.</seg>
<seg id="2180">Offhand, the question, "Ought we to let incompetent physicians practice?" seems to admit of only a negative answer.</seg>
<seg id="2181">But I want to urge that second thought may give pause.</seg>
<seg id="2182">In the first place, licensure is the key to the control that the medical profession can exercise over the number of physicians.</seg>
<seg id="2183">To understand why this is so requires some discussion of the structure of the medical profession.</seg>
<seg id="2184">The American Medical Association is perhaps the strongest trade union in the United States.</seg>
<seg id="2185">The essence of the power of a trade union is its power to restrict the number who may engage in a particular occupation.</seg>
<seg id="2186">This restriction may be exercised indirectly by being able to enforce a wage rate higher than would otherwise prevail.</seg>
<seg id="2187">If such a wage rate can be enforced, it will reduce the number of people who can get jobs and thus indirectly the number of people pursuing the occupation.</seg>
<seg id="2188">This technique of restriction has disadvantages.</seg>
<seg id="2189">There is always a dissatisfied fringe of people who are trying to get into the occupation.</seg>
<seg id="2190">A trade union is much better off if it can limit directly the number of people who enter the occupationˇŞwho ever try to get jobs in it.</seg>
<seg id="2191">The disgruntled and dissatisfied are excluded at the outset, and the union does not have to worry about them.</seg>
<seg id="2192">The American Medical Association is in this position.</seg>
<seg id="2193">It is a trade union that can limit the number of people who can enter.</seg>
<seg id="2194">How can it do this?</seg>
<seg id="2195">The essential control is at the stage of admission to medical school.</seg>
<seg id="2196">The Council on Medical Education and Hospitals of the American Medical Association approves medical schools.</seg>
<seg id="2197">In order for a medical school to get and stay on its list of approved schools it has to meet the standards of the Council.</seg>
<seg id="2198">The power of the Council has been demonstrated at various times when there has been pressure to reduce numbers.</seg>
<seg id="2199">For example, in the 1930's during the depression, the Council on Medical Education and Hospitals wrote a letter to the various medical schools saying the medical schools were admitting more students than could be given the proper kind of training.</seg>
<seg id="2200">In the next year or two, every school reduced the number it was admitting, giving very strong presumptive evidence that the recommendation had some effect.</seg>
<seg id="2201">Why does the Council's approval matter so much?</seg>
<seg id="2202">If it abuses its power, why don't unapproved medical schools arise?</seg>
<seg id="2203">The answer is that in almost every state in the United States, a person must be licensed to practice medicine, and to get the license, he must be a graduate of an approved school.</seg>
<seg id="2204">In almost every state, the list of approved schools is identical with the list of schools approved by the Council on Medical Education and Hospitals of the American Medical Association.</seg>
<seg id="2205">That is why the licensure provision is the key to the effective control of admission.</seg>
<seg id="2206">It has a dual effect.</seg>
<seg id="2207">On the one hand, the members of the licensure commission are always physicians and hence have some control at the step at which men apply for a license.</seg>
<seg id="2208">This control is more limited in effectiveness than control at the medical school level.</seg>
<seg id="2209">In almost all professions requiring licensure, people may try to get admitted more than once.</seg>
<seg id="2210">If a person tries long enough and in enough jurisdictions he is likely to get through sooner or later.</seg>
<seg id="2211">Since he has already spent the money and time to get his training, he has a strong incentive to keep trying.</seg>
<seg id="2212">Licensure provisions that come into operation only after a man is trained therefore affect entry largely by raising the costs of getting into the occupation, since it may take a longer time to get in and since there is always some uncertainty whether he will succeed.</seg>
<seg id="2213">But this rise in cost is nothing like so effective in limiting entry as is preventing a man from getting started on his career.</seg>
<seg id="2214">If he is eliminated at the stage of entering medical school, he never comes up as a candidate for examination;</seg>
<seg id="2215">he can never be troublesome at that stage.</seg>
<seg id="2216">The efficient way to get control over the number in a profession is therefore to get control of entry into professional schools.</seg>
<seg id="2217">Control over admission to medical school and later licensure enables the profession to limit entry in two ways.</seg>
<seg id="2218">The obvious one is simply by turning down many applicants.</seg>
<seg id="2219">The less obvious, but probably far more important one, is by establishing standards for admission and licensure that make entry so difficult as to discourage young people from ever trying to get admission.</seg>
<seg id="2220">Though most state laws require only two years of college prior to medical school, nearly 100 per cent of the entrants have had four years of college.</seg>
<seg id="2221">Similarly, medical training proper has been lengthened, particularly through more stringent internship arrangements.</seg>
<seg id="2222">As an aside, the lawyers have never been as successful as the physicians in getting control at the point of admission to professional school, though they are moving in that direction.</seg>
<seg id="2223">The reason is amusing.</seg>
<seg id="2224">Almost every school on the American Bar Association's list of approved schools is a full time day school;</seg>
<seg id="2225">almost no night schools are approved.</seg>
<seg id="2226">Many state legislators, on the other hand, are graduates of night law schools.</seg>
<seg id="2227">If they voted to restrict admission to the profession to graduates of approved schools, in effect they would be voting that they themselves were not qualified.</seg>
<seg id="2228">Their reluctance to condemn their own competence has been the main factor that has tended to limit the extent to which law has been able to succeed in imitating medicine.</seg>
<seg id="2229">I have not myself done any extensive work on requirements for admission to law for many years but I understand that this limitation is breaking down.</seg>
<seg id="2230">The greater affluence of students means that a much larger fraction are going to full time law schools and this is changing the composition of the legislatures.</seg>
<seg id="2231">To return to medicine, it is the provision about graduation from approved schools that is the most important source of professional control over entry.</seg>
<seg id="2232">The profession has used this control to limit numbers.</seg>
<seg id="2233">To avoid misunderstanding let me emphasize that I am not saying that individual members of the medical profession, the leaders of the medical profession, or the people who are in charge of the Council on Medical Education and Hospitals deliberately go out of their way to limit entry in order to raise their own incomes.</seg>
<seg id="2234">That is not the way it works.</seg>
<seg id="2235">Even when such people explicitly comment on the desirability of limiting numbers to raise incomes they will always justify the policy on the grounds that if "too" many people are let in, this will lower their incomes so that they will be driven to resort to unethical practices in order to earn a "proper" income.</seg>
<seg id="2236">The only way, they argue, in which ethical practices can be maintained is by keeping people at a standard of income which is adequate to the merits and needs of the medical profession.</seg>
<seg id="2237">I must confess that this has always seemed to me objectionable on both ethical and factual grounds.</seg>
<seg id="2238">It is extraordinary that leaders of medicine should proclaim publicly that they and their colleagues must be paid to be ethical.</seg>
<seg id="2239">And if it were so, I doubt that the price would have any limit.</seg>
<seg id="2240">There seems little correlation between poverty and honesty.</seg>
<seg id="2241">One would rather expect the opposite;</seg>
<seg id="2242">dishonesty may not always pay but surely it sometimes does.</seg>
<seg id="2243">Control of entry is explicitly rationalized along these lines only at times like the Great Depression when there is much unemployment and relatively low incomes.</seg>
<seg id="2244">In ordinary times, the rationalization for restriction is different.</seg>
<seg id="2245">It is that the members of the medical profession want to raise what they regard as the standards of "quality" of the profession.</seg>
<seg id="2246">The defect in this rationalization is a common one, and one that is destructive of a proper understanding of the operation of an economic system, namely, the failure to distinguish between technical efficiency and economic efficiency.</seg>
<seg id="2247">A story about lawyers will perhaps illustrate the point.</seg>
<seg id="2248">At a meeting of lawyers at which problems of admission were being discussed, a colleague of mine, arguing against restrictive admission standards, used an analogy from the automobile industry.</seg>
<seg id="2249">Would it not, he said, be absurd if the automobile industry were to argue that no one should drive a low quality car and therefore that no automobile manufacturer should be permitted to produce a car that did not come up to the Cadillac standard.</seg>
<seg id="2250">One member of the audience rose and approved the analogy, saying that, of course, the country cannot afford anything but Cadillac lawyers!</seg>
<seg id="2251">This tends to be the professional attitude.</seg>
<seg id="2252">The members look solely at technical standards of performance, and argue in effect that we must have only firstrate physicians even if this means that some people get no medical serviceˇŞthough of course they never put it that way.</seg>
<seg id="2253">Nonetheless, the view that people should get only the "optimum" medical service always lead to a restrictive policy, a policy that keeps down the number of physicians.</seg>
<seg id="2254">I would not, of course, want to argue that this is the only force at work, but only that this kind of consideration leads many well-meaning physicians to go along with policies that they would reject out-of-hand if they did not have this kind of comforting rationalization.</seg>
<seg id="2255">It is easy to demonstrate that quality is only a rationalization and not the underlying reason for restriction.</seg>
<seg id="2256">The power of the Council on Medical Education and Hospitals of the American Medical Association has been used to limit numbers in ways that cannot possibly have any connection whatsoever with quality.</seg>
<seg id="2257">The simplest example is their recommendation to various states that citizenship be made a requirement for the practice of medicine.</seg>
<seg id="2258">I find it inconceivable to see how this is relevant to medical performance.</seg>
<seg id="2259">A similar requirement that they have tried to impose on occasion is that examination for licensure must be taken in English.</seg>
<seg id="2260">A dramatic piece of evidence on the power and potency of the Association as well as on the lack of relation to quality is proved by one figure that I have always found striking.</seg>
<seg id="2261">After 1933, when Hitler came to power in Germany, there was a tremendous outflow of professional people from Germany, Austria and so on, including of course, physicians who wanted to practice in the United States.</seg>
<seg id="2262">The number of physicians trained abroad who were admitted to practice in the United States in the five years after 1933 was the same as in the five years before.</seg>
<seg id="2263">This was clearly not the result of the natural course of events.</seg>
<seg id="2264">The threat of these additional physicians led to a stringent tightening of requirements for foreign physicians that imposed extreme costs upon them.It is clear that licensure is the key to the medical profession's ability to restrict the number of physicians who practice medicine.</seg>
<seg id="2265">It is also the key to its ability to restrict technological and organizational changes in the way medicine is conducted.</seg>
<seg id="2266">The American Medical Association has been consistently against the practice of group medicine, and against prepaid medical plans.</seg>
<seg id="2267">These methods of practice may have good features and bad features, but they are technological innovations that people ought to be free to try out if they wish.</seg>
<seg id="2268">There is no basis for saying conclusively that the optimum technical method of organizing medical practice is practice by an independent physician.</seg>
<seg id="2269">Maybe it is group practice, maybe it is by corporations.</seg>
<seg id="2270">One ought to have a system under which all varieties can be tried.</seg>
<seg id="2271">The American Medical Association has resisted such attempts and has been able effectively to inhibit them.</seg>
<seg id="2272">It has been able to do so because licensure has indirectly given it control of admission to practice in hospitals.</seg>
<seg id="2273">The Council on Medical Education and Hospitals approves hospitals as well as medical schools.</seg>
<seg id="2274">In order for a physician to get admission to practice in an "approved" hospital, he must generally be approved by his county medical association or by the hospital board.</seg>
<seg id="2275">Why can't unapproved hospitals be set up?</seg>
<seg id="2276">Because under present economic conditions, in order for a hospital to operate it must have a supply of interns.</seg>
<seg id="2277">Under most state licensure laws, candidates must have some internship experience to be admitted to practice, and internship must be in an "approved" hospital.</seg>
<seg id="2278">The list of "approved" hospitals is generally identical with that of the Council on Medical Education and Hospitals.</seg>
<seg id="2279">Consequently, the licensure law gives the profession control over hospitals as well as over schools.</seg>
<seg id="2280">This is the key to the AMA's largely successful opposition to various types of group practice.</seg>
<seg id="2281">In a few cases, the groups have been able to survive.</seg>
<seg id="2282">In the District of Columbia, they succeeded because they were able to bring suit against the American Medical Association under the federal Sherman antitrust laws, and won the suit.</seg>
<seg id="2283">In a few other cases, they have succeeded for special reasons.</seg>
<seg id="2284">There is, however, no doubt that the tendency toward group practice has been greatly retarded by the AMA's opposition.</seg>
<seg id="2285">It is interesting, and this is an aside, that the medical association is against only one type of group practice, namely, prepaid group practice.</seg>
<seg id="2286">The economic reason seems to be that this eliminates the possibility of engaging in discriminatory pricing.</seg>
<seg id="2287">It is clear that licensure has been at the core of the restriction of entry and that this involves a heavy social cost, both to the individuals who want to practice medicine but are prevented from doing so and to the public deprived of the medical care it wants to buy and is prevented from buying.</seg>
<seg id="2288">Let me now ask the question:</seg>
<seg id="2289">Does licensure have the good effects that it is said to have?</seg>
<seg id="2290">In the first place, does it really raise standards of competence?</seg>
<seg id="2291">It is by no means clear that it does raise the standards of competence in the actual practice of the profession for several reasons.</seg>
<seg id="2292">In the first place, whenever you establish a block to entry into any field, you establish an incentive to find ways of getting around it, and of course medicine is no exception.</seg>
<seg id="2293">The rise of the professions of osteopathy and of chiropractic is not unrelated to the restriction of entry into medicine.</seg>
<seg id="2294">On the contrary, each of these represented, to some extent, an attempt to find away around restriction of entry.</seg>
<seg id="2295">Each of these, in turn, is proceeding to get itself licensed, and to impose restrictions.</seg>
<seg id="2296">The effect is to create different levels and kinds of practice, to distinguish between what is called medical practice and substitutes such as osteopathy, chiropractic, faith healing and so on.</seg>
<seg id="2297">These alternatives may well be of lower quality than medical practice would have been without the restrictions on entry into medicine.</seg>
<seg id="2298">More generally, if the number of physicians is less than it otherwise would be, and if they are all fully occupied, as they generally are, this means that there is a smaller total of medical practice by trained physiciansˇŞfewer medical man-hours of practice, as it were.</seg>
<seg id="2299">The alternative is untrained practice by somebody;</seg>
<seg id="2300">it may and in part must be by people who have no professional qualifications at all.</seg>
<seg id="2301">Moreover, the situation is much more extreme.</seg>
<seg id="2302">If "medical practice" is to be limited to licensed practitioners, it is necessary to define what medical practice is, and featherbedding is not something that is restricted to the railroads.</seg>
<seg id="2303">Under the interpretation of the statutes forbidding unauthorized practice of medicine, many things are restricted to licensed physicians that could perfectly well be done by technicians, and other skilled people who do not have a Cadillac medical training.</seg>
<seg id="2304">I am not enough of a technician to list the examples at all fully.</seg>
<seg id="2305">I only know that those who have looked into the question say that the tendency is to include in "medical practice" a wider and wider range of activities that could perfectly well be performed by technicians.</seg>
<seg id="2306">Trained physicians devote a considerable part of their time to things that might well be done by others.</seg>
<seg id="2307">The result is to reduce drastically the amount of medical care.</seg>
<seg id="2308">The relevant average quality of medical care, if one can at all conceive of the concept, cannot be obtained by simply averaging the quality of care that is given;</seg>
<seg id="2309">that would be like judging the effectiveness of a medical treatment by considering only the survivors;</seg>
<seg id="2310">one must also allow for the fact that the restrictions reduce the amount of care.</seg>
<seg id="2311">The result may well be that the average level of competence in a meaningful sense has been reduced by the restrictions.</seg>
<seg id="2312">Even these comments do not go far enough, because they consider the situation at a point in time and do not allow for changes over time.</seg>
<seg id="2313">Advances in any science or field often result from the work of one out of a large number of crackpots and quacks and people who have no standing in the profession.</seg>
<seg id="2314">In the medical profession, under present circumstances, it is very difficult to engage in research or experimentation unless you are a member of the profession.</seg>
<seg id="2315">If you are a member of the profession and want to stay in good standing in the profession, you are seriously limited in the kind of experimentation you can do.</seg>
<seg id="2316">A "faith healer" may be just a quack who is imposing himself on credulous patients, but maybe one in a thousand or in many thousands will produce an important improvement in medicine.</seg>
<seg id="2317">There are many different routes to knowledge and learning and the effect of restricting the practice of what is called medicine and defining it as we tend to do to a particular group, who in the main have to conform to the prevailing orthodoxy, is certain to reduce the amount of experimentation that goes on and hence to reduce the rate of growth of knowledge in the area.</seg>
<seg id="2318">What is true for the content of medicine is true also for its organization, as has already been suggested.</seg>
<seg id="2319">I shall expand further on this point below.</seg>
<seg id="2320">There is still another way in which licensure, and the associated monopoly in the practice of medicine, tend to render standards of practice low.</seg>
<seg id="2321">I have already suggested that it renders the average quality of practice low by reducing the number of physicians, by reducing the aggregate number of hours available from trained physicians for more rather than less important tasks, and by reducing the incentive for research and development.</seg>
<seg id="2322">It renders it low also by making it much more difficult for private individuals to collect from physicians for malpractice.</seg>
<seg id="2323">One of the protections of the individual citizen against incompetence is protection against fraud and the ability to bring suit in the court against malpractice.</seg>
<seg id="2324">Some suits are brought, and physicians complain a great deal about how much they have to pay for malpractice insurance.</seg>
<seg id="2325">Yet suits for malpractice are fewer and less successful than they would be were it not for the watchful eye of the medical associations.</seg>
<seg id="2326">It is not easy to get a physician to testify against a fellow physician when he faces the sanction of being denied the right to practice in an "approved" hospital.</seg>
<seg id="2327">The testimony generally has to come from members of panels set up by medical associations themselves, always, of course, in the alleged interest of the patients.</seg>
<seg id="2328">When these effects are taken into account, I am myself persuaded that licensure has reduced both the quantity and quality of medical practice;</seg>
<seg id="2329">that it has reduced the opportunities available to people who would like to be physicians, forcing them to pursue occupations they regard as less attractive;</seg>
<seg id="2330">that it has forced the public to pay more for less satisfactory medical service, and that it has retarded technological development both in medicine itself and in the organization of medical practice.</seg>
<seg id="2331">I conclude that licensure should be eliminated as a requirement for the practice of medicine.</seg>
<seg id="2332">When all this is said, many a reader, I suspect, like many a person with whom I have discussed these issues, will say, "But still, how else would I get any evidence on the quality of a physician.</seg>
<seg id="2333">Granted all that you say about costs, is not licensure the only way of providing the public with some assurance of at least minimum quality ?"</seg>
<seg id="2334">The answer is partly that people do not now choose physicians by picking names at random from a list of licensed physicians;</seg>
<seg id="2335">partly, that a man's ability to pass an examination twenty or thirty years earlier is hardly assurance of quality now;</seg>
<seg id="2336">hence, licensure is not now the main or even a major source of assurance of at least minimum quality.</seg>
<seg id="2337">But the major answer is very different.</seg>
<seg id="2338">It is that the question itself reveals the tyranny of the status quo and the poverty of our imagination in fields in which we are laymen, and even in those in which we have some competence, by comparison with the fertility of the market.</seg>
<seg id="2339">Let me illustrate by speculating on how medicine might have developed and what assurances of quality would have emerged, if the profession had not exerted monopoly power.</seg>
<seg id="2340">Suppose that anyone had been free to practice medicine without restriction except for legal and financial responsibility for any harm done to others through fraud and negligence.</seg>
<seg id="2341">I conjecture that the whole development of medicine would have been different.</seg>
<seg id="2342">The present market for medical care, hampered as it has been, gives some hints of what the difference would have been.</seg>
<seg id="2343">Group practice in conjunction with hospitals would have grown enormously.</seg>
<seg id="2344">Instead of individual practice plus large institutional hospitals conducted by governments or eleemosynary institutions, there might have developed medical partnerships or corporationsˇŞmedical teams.</seg>
<seg id="2345">These would have provided central diagnostic and treatment facilities, including hospital facilities.</seg>
<seg id="2346">Some presumably would have been prepaid, combining in one package present hospital insurance, health insurance, and group medical practice.</seg>
<seg id="2347">Others would have charged separate fees for separate services.</seg>
<seg id="2348">And of course, most might have used both methods of payment.</seg>
<seg id="2349">These medical teamsˇŞdepartment stores of medicine, if you willˇŞwould be intermediaries between the patients and the physician.</seg>
<seg id="2350">Being long-lived and immobile, they would have a great interest in establishing a reputation for reliability and quality.</seg>
<seg id="2351">For the same reason, consumers would get to know their reputation.</seg>
<seg id="2352">They would have the specialized skill to judge the quality of physicians;</seg>
<seg id="2353">indeed, they would be the agent of the consumer in doing so, as the department store is now for many a product.</seg>
<seg id="2354">In addition, they could organize medical care efficiently, combining medical men of different degrees of skill and training, using technicians with limited training for tasks for which they were suited, and reserving highly skilled and competent specialists for the tasks they alone could perform.</seg>
<seg id="2355">The reader can add further flourishes for himself, drawing in part, as I have done, on what now goes on at the leading medical clinics.</seg>
<seg id="2356">Of course, not all medical practice would be done through such teams.</seg>
<seg id="2357">Individual private practice would continue, just as the small store with a limited clientele exists alongside the department store, the individual lawyer alongside the great many-partnered firm.</seg>
<seg id="2358">Men would establish individual reputations and some patients would prefer the privacy and intimacy of the individual practitioner.</seg>
<seg id="2359">Some areas would be too small to be served by medical teams.</seg>
<seg id="2360">And so on.I would not even want to maintain that the medical teams would dominate the field.</seg>
<seg id="2361">My aim is only to show by example that there are many alternatives to the present organization of practice.</seg>
<seg id="2362">The impossibility of any individual or small group conceiving of all the possibilities, let alone evaluating their merits,is the great argument against central governmental planning and against arrangements such as professional monopolies that limit the possibilities of experimentation.</seg>
<seg id="2363">On the other side, the great argument for the market is its tolerance of diversity;</seg>
<seg id="2364">its ability to utilize a wide range of special knowledge and capacity.</seg>
<seg id="2365">It renders special groups impotent to prevent experimentation and permits the customers and not the producers to decide what will serve the customers best.</seg>
<seg id="2366">Chapter XˇŞ The Distribution of Income</seg>
<seg id="2367">A central element in the development of a collectivist sentiment in this century, at least in Western countries, has been a belief in equality of income as a social goal and a willingness to use the arm of the state to promote it.</seg>
<seg id="2368">Two very different questions must be asked in evaluating this egalitarian sentiment and the egalitarian measures it has produced.</seg>
<seg id="2369">The first is normative and ethical:</seg>
<seg id="2370">what is the justification for state intervention to promote equality?</seg>
<seg id="2371">The second is positive and scientific: what has been the effect of the measures actually taken?</seg>
<seg id="2372">The Ethics of Distribution</seg>
<seg id="2373">The ethical principle that would directly justify the distribution of income in a free market society is, ''To each according to what he and the instruments he owns produces."</seg>
<seg id="2374">The operation of even this principle implicitly depends on state action.</seg>
<seg id="2375">Property rights are matters of law and social convention.</seg>
<seg id="2376">As we have seen, their definition and enforcement is one of the primary functions of the state.</seg>
<seg id="2377">The final distribution of income and wealth under the full operation of this principle may well depend markedly on the rules of property adopted.</seg>
<seg id="2378">What is the relation between this principle and another that seems ethically appealing, namely, equality of treatment?</seg>
<seg id="2379">In part, the two principles are not contradictory.</seg>
<seg id="2380">Payment in accordance with product may be necessary to achieve true equality of treatment.</seg>
<seg id="2381">Given individuals whom we are prepared to regard as alike in ability and initial resources, if some have a greater taste for leisure and others for marketable goods, inequality of return through the market is necessary to achieve equality of total return or equality of treatment.</seg>
<seg id="2382">One man may prefer a routine job with much time off for basking in the sun to a more exacting job paying a higher salary;</seg>
<seg id="2383">another man may prefer the opposite.</seg>
<seg id="2384">If both were paid equally in money, their incomes in a more fundamental sense would be unequal.</seg>
<seg id="2385">Similarly, equal treatment requires that an individual be paid more for a dirty, unattractive job than for a pleasant rewarding one.</seg>
<seg id="2386">Much observed inequality is of this kind.</seg>
<seg id="2387">Differences of money income offset differences in other characteristics of the occupation or trade.</seg>
<seg id="2388">In the jargon of economists, they are "equalizing differences" required to make the whole of the "net advantages," pecuniary and non-pecuniary, the same.</seg>
<seg id="2389">Another kind of inequality arising through the operation of the market is also required, in a somewhat more subtle sense, to produce equality of treatment, or to put it differently to satisfy men's tastes.</seg>
<seg id="2390">It can be illustrated most simply by a lottery.</seg>
<seg id="2391">Consider a group of individuals who initially have equal endowments and who all agree voluntarily to enter a lottery with very unequal prizes.</seg>
<seg id="2392">The resultant inequality of income is surely required to permit the individuals in question to make the most of their initial equality.</seg>
<seg id="2393">Redistribution of the income after the event is equivalent to denying them the opportunity to enter the lottery.</seg>
<seg id="2394">This case is far more important in practice than would appear by taking the notion of a "lottery" literally.</seg>
<seg id="2395">Individuals choose occupations, investments, and the like partly in accordance with their taste for uncertainty.</seg>
<seg id="2396">The girl who tries to become a movie actress rather than a civil servant is deliberately choosing to enter a lottery, so is the individual who invests in penny uranium stocks rather than government bonds.</seg>
<seg id="2397">Insurance is a way of expressing a taste for certainty.</seg>
<seg id="2398">Even these examples do not indicate fully the extent to which actual inequality may be the result of arrangements designed to satisfy men's tastes.</seg>
<seg id="2399">The very arrangements for paying and hiring people are affected by such preferences.</seg>
<seg id="2400">If all potential movie actresses had a great dislike of uncertainty, there would tend to develop "cooperatives" of movie actresses, the members of which agreed in advance to share income receipts more or less evenly, thereby in effect providing themselves insurance through the pooling of risks.</seg>
<seg id="2401">If such a preference were widespread, large diversified corporations combining risky and non-risky ventures would become the rule.</seg>
<seg id="2402">The wild-cat oil prospector, the private proprietorship, the small partnership, would all become rare.</seg>
<seg id="2403">Indeed, this is one way to interpret governmental measures to redistribute income through progressive taxes and the like.</seg>
<seg id="2404">It can be argued that for one reason or another, costs of administration perhaps, the market cannot produce the range of lotteries or the kind of lottery desired by the members of the community, and that progressive taxation is, as it were, a government enterprise to do so.</seg>
<seg id="2405">I have no doubt that this view contains an element of truth.</seg>
<seg id="2406">At the same time, it can hardly justify present taxation, if only because the taxes are imposed after it is already largely known who have drawn the prizes and who the blanks in the lottery of life, and the taxes are voted mostly by those who think they have drawn the blanks.</seg>
<seg id="2407">One might, along these lines, justify one generation's voting the tax schedules to be applied to an as yet unborn generation.</seg>
<seg id="2408">Any such procedure would, I conjecture, yield income tax schedules much less highly graduated than present schedules are, at least on paper.</seg>
<seg id="2409">Though much of the inequality of income produced by payment in accordance with product reflects "equalizing" differences or the satisfaction of men's tastes for uncertainty, a large part reflects initial differences in endowment, both of human capacities and of property.</seg>
<seg id="2410">This is the part that raises the really difficult ethical issue.</seg>
<seg id="2411">It is widely argued that it is essential to distinguish between inequality in personal endowments and in property, and between inequalities arising from inherited wealth and from acquired wealth.</seg>
<seg id="2412">Inequality resulting from differences in personal capacities, or from differences in wealth accumulated by the individual in question, are considered appropriate, or at least not so clearly inappropriate as differences resulting from inherited wealth.</seg>
<seg id="2413">This distinction is untenable.</seg>
<seg id="2414">Is there any greater ethical justification for the high returns to the individual who inherits from his parents a peculiar voice for which there is a great demand than for the high returns to the individual who inherits property?</seg>
<seg id="2415">The sons of Russian commissars surely have a higher expectation of incomeˇŞperhaps also of liquidationˇŞthan the sons of peasants.</seg>
<seg id="2416">Is this any more or less justifiable than the higher income expectation of the son of an American millionaire?</seg>
<seg id="2417">We can look at this same question in another way.</seg>
<seg id="2418">A parent who has wealth that he wishes to pass on to his child can do so in different ways.</seg>
<seg id="2419">He can use a given sum of money to finance his child's training as, say, a certified public accountant, or to set him up in business, or to set up a trust fund yielding him a property income.</seg>
<seg id="2420">In any of these cases, the child will have a higher income than he otherwise would.</seg>
<seg id="2421">But in the first case, his income will be regarded as coming from human capacities;</seg>
<seg id="2422">in the second, from profits;</seg>
<seg id="2423">in the third, from inherited wealth.</seg>
<seg id="2424">Is there any basis for distinguishing among these categories of receipts on ethical grounds?</seg>
<seg id="2425">Finally, it seems illogical to say that a man is entitled to what he has produced by personal capacities or to the produce of the wealth he has accumulated, but that he is not entitled to pass any wealth on to his children;</seg>
<seg id="2426">to say that a man may use his income for riotous living but may not give it to his heirs.</seg>
<seg id="2427">Surely, the latter is one way to use what he has produced.</seg>
<seg id="2428">The fact that these arguments against the so-called capitalist ethic are invalid docs not of course demonstrate that the capitalist ethic is an acceptable one.</seg>
<seg id="2429">I find it difficult to justify either accepting or rejecting it, or to justify any alternative principle.</seg>
<seg id="2430">I am led to the view that it cannot in and of itself be regarded as an ethical principle;</seg>
<seg id="2431">that it must be regarded as instrumental or a corollary of some other principle such as freedom.</seg>
<seg id="2432">Some hypothetical examples may illustrate the fundamental difficulty.</seg>
<seg id="2433">Suppose there are four Robinson Crusoes, independently marooned on four islands in the same neighborhood.</seg>
<seg id="2434">One happened to land on a large and fruitful island which enables him to live easily and well.</seg>
<seg id="2435">The others happened to land on tiny and rather barren islands from which they can barely scratch a living.</seg>
<seg id="2436">One day, they discover the existence of one another.</seg>
<seg id="2437">Of course, it would be generous of the Crusoe on the large island if he invited the others to join him and share its wealth.</seg>
<seg id="2438">But suppose he does not.</seg>
<seg id="2439">Would the other three be justified in joining forces and compelling him to share his wealth with them?</seg>
<seg id="2440">Many a reader will be tempted to say yes.</seg>
<seg id="2441">But before yielding to this temptation, consider precisely the same situation in different guise.</seg>
<seg id="2442">Suppose you and three friends are walking along the street and you happen to spy and retrieve a $ 20 bill on the pavement.</seg>
<seg id="2443">It would be generous of you, of course, if you were to divide it equally with them, or at least blow them to a drink.</seg>
<seg id="2444">But suppose you do not.</seg>
<seg id="2445">Would the other three be justified in joining forces and compelling you to share the $ 20 equally with them?</seg>
<seg id="2446">I suspect most readers will be tempted to say no.</seg>
<seg id="2447">And on further reflection, they may even conclude that the generous course of action is not itself clearly the "right" one.</seg>
<seg id="2448">Are we prepared to urge on ourselves or our fellows that any person whose wealth exceeds the average of all persons in the world should immediately dispose of the excess by distributing it equally to all the rest of the world's inhabitants ?</seg>
<seg id="2449">We may admire and praise such action when undertaken by a few.</seg>
<seg id="2450">But a universal "potlatch" would make a civilized world impossible.</seg>
<seg id="2451">In any event, two wrongs do not make a right.</seg>
<seg id="2452">The unwillingness of the rich Robinson Crusoe or the lucky finder of the $ 20 bill to share his wealth does not justify the use of coercion by the others.</seg>
<seg id="2453">Can we justify being judges in our own case, deciding on our own when we are entitled to use force to extract what we regard as our due from others?</seg>
<seg id="2454">Or what we regard as not their due?</seg>
<seg id="2455">Most differences of status or position or wealth can be regarded as the product of chance at a far enough remove.</seg>
<seg id="2456">The man who is hard working and thrifty is to be regarded as "deserving";</seg>
<seg id="2457">yet these qualities owe much to the genes he was fortunate (or unfortunate?) enough to inherit.</seg>
<seg id="2458">Despite the lip service that we all pay to "merit" as compared to "chance," we are generally much readier to accept inequalities arising from chance than those clearly attributable to merit.</seg>
<seg id="2459">The college professor whose colleague wins a sweepstake will envy him but is unlikely to bear him any malice or to feel unjustly treated.</seg>
<seg id="2460">Let the colleague receive a trivial raise that makes his salary higher than the professor's own, and the professor is far more likely to feel aggrieved.</seg>
<seg id="2461">After all, the goddess of chance, as of justice, is blind.</seg>
<seg id="2462">The salary raise was a deliberate judgment of relative merit.</seg>
<seg id="2463">The Instrumental Role of Distribution</seg>
<seg id="2464">According to ProductThe operative function of payment in accordance with product in a market society is not primarily distributive, but allocative.</seg>
<seg id="2465">As was pointed out in chapter i, the central principle of a market economy is co-operation through voluntary exchange.</seg>
<seg id="2466">Individuals co-operate with others because they can in this way satisfy their own wants more effectively.</seg>
<seg id="2467">But unless an individual receives the whole of what he adds to the product, he will enter into exchanges on the basis of what he can receive rather than what he can produce.</seg>
<seg id="2468">Exchanges will not take place that would have been mutually beneficial if each party received what he contributed to the aggregate product.</seg>
<seg id="2469">Payment in accordance with product is therefore necessary in order that resources be used most effectively, at least under a system depending on voluntary co-operation.</seg>
<seg id="2470">Given sufficient knowledge, it might be that compulsion could be substituted for the incentive of reward, though I doubt that it could.</seg>
<seg id="2471">One can shuffle inanimate objects around;</seg>
<seg id="2472">one can compel individuals to be at certain places at certain times;</seg>
<seg id="2473">but one can hardly compel individuals to put forward their best efforts.</seg>
<seg id="2474">Put another way, the substitution of compulsion for co-operation changes the amount of resources available.</seg>
<seg id="2475">Though the essential function of payment in accordance with product in a market society is to enable resources to be allocated efficiently without compulsion, it is unlikely to be tolerated unless it is also regarded as yielding distributive justice.</seg>
<seg id="2476">No society can be stable unless there is a basic core of value judgments that are unthinkingly accepted by the great bulk of its members.</seg>
<seg id="2477">Some key institutions must be accepted as "absolutes," not simply as instrumental.</seg>
<seg id="2478">I believe that payment in accordance with product has been, and, in large measure, still is, one of these accepted value judgments or institutions.</seg>
<seg id="2479">One can demonstrate this by examining the grounds on which the internal opponents of the capitalist system have attacked the distribution of income resulting from it.</seg>
<seg id="2480">It is a distinguishing feature of the core of central values of a society that it is accepted alike by its members, whether they regard themselves as proponents or as opponents of the system of organization of the society.</seg>
<seg id="2481">Even the severest internal critics of capitalism have implicitly accepted payment in accordance with product as ethically fair.</seg>
<seg id="2482">The most far-reaching criticism has come from the Marxists.</seg>
<seg id="2483">Marx argued that labor was exploited.</seg>
<seg id="2484">Because labor produced the whole of the product but got only part of it;</seg>
<seg id="2485">the rest is Marx's "surplus value."</seg>
<seg id="2486">Even if the statements of fact implicit in this assertion were accepted, the value judgment follows only if one accepts the capitalist ethic.</seg>
<seg id="2487">Labor is "exploited" only if labor is entitled to what it produces.</seg>
<seg id="2488">If one accepts instead the socialist premise, "to each according to his need, from each according to his ability"ˇŞwhatever that may meanˇŞit is necessary to compare what labor produces, not with what it gets but with its "ability'', and to compare what labor gets, not with what it produces but with its "need."</seg>
<seg id="2489">Of course, the Marxist argument is invalid on other grounds as well.</seg>
<seg id="2490">There is, first, the confusion between the total product of all co-operating resources and the amount added to product ˇŞin the economist's jargon, marginal product.</seg>
<seg id="2491">Even more striking, there is an unstated change in the meaning of "labor" in passing from the premise to the conclusion.</seg>
<seg id="2492">Marx recognized the role of capital in producing the product but regarded capital as embodied labor.</seg>
<seg id="2493">Hence, written out in full, the premises of the Marxist syllogism would run:</seg>
<seg id="2494">"Present and past labor produce the whole of the product.</seg>
<seg id="2495">Present labor gets only part of the product."</seg>
<seg id="2496">The logical conclusion is presumably "Past labor is exploited," and the inference for action is that past labor should get more of the product, though it is by no means clear how, unless it be in elegant tombstones.</seg>
<seg id="2497">The achievement of allocation of resources without compulsion is the major instrumental role in the market place of distribution in accordance with product.</seg>
<seg id="2498">But it is not the only instrumental role of the resulting inequality.</seg>
<seg id="2499">We have noted in chapter i the role that inequality plays in providing independent foci of power to offset the centralization of political power, as well as the role that it plays in promoting civil freedom by providing "patrons" to finance the dissemination of unpopular or simply novel ideas.</seg>
<seg id="2500">In addition, in the economic sphere, it provides "patrons" to finance experimentation and the development of new productsˇŞto buy the first experimental automobiles and television sets, let alone impressionist paintings.</seg>
<seg id="2501">Finally, it enables distribution to occur impersonally without the need for "authority"ˇŞa special facet of the general role of the market in effecting co-operation and co-ordination without coercion.</seg>
<seg id="2502">Facts of Income Distribution</seg>
<seg id="2503">A capitalist system involving payment in accordance with product can be, and in practice is, characterized by considerable inequality of income and wealth.</seg>
<seg id="2504">This fact is frequently misinterpreted to mean that capitalism and free enterprise produce wider inequality than alternative systems and, as a corollary, that the extension and development of capitalism has meant increased inequality.</seg>
<seg id="2505">This misinterpretation is fostered by the misleading character of most published figures on the distribution of income, in particular their failure to distinguish short run from long-run inequality.</seg>
<seg id="2506">Let us look at some of the broader facts about the distribution of income.</seg>
<seg id="2507">One of the most striking facts which runs counter to many people's expectation has to do with the sources of income.</seg>
<seg id="2508">The more capitalistic a country is, the smaller the fraction of income paid for the use of what is generally regarded as capital, and the larger the fraction paid for human services.</seg>
<seg id="2509">In underdeveloped countries like India, Egypt, and so on, something like half of total income is property income.</seg>
<seg id="2510">In the United States, roughly one-fifth is property income.</seg>
<seg id="2511">And in other advanced capitalist countries, the proportion is not very different.</seg>
<seg id="2512">Of course, these countries have much more capital than the primitive countries but they are even richer in the productive capacity of their residents;</seg>
<seg id="2513">hence, the larger income from property is a smaller fraction of the total.</seg>
<seg id="2514">The great achievement of capitalism has not been the accumulation of property, it has been the opportunities it has offered to men and women to extend and develop and improve their capacities.</seg>
<seg id="2515">Yet the enemies of capitalism are fond of castigating it as materialist, and its friends all too often apologize for capitalism's materialism as a necessary cost of progress.</seg>
<seg id="2516">Another striking fact, contrary to popular conception, is that capitalism leads to less inequality than alternative systems of organization and that the development of capitalism has greatly lessened the extent of inequality.</seg>
<seg id="2517">Comparisons over space and time alike confirm this view.</seg>
<seg id="2518">There is surely drastically less inequality in Western capitalist societies like the Scandinavian countries, France, Britain, and the United States, than in a status society like India or a backward country like Egypt.</seg>
<seg id="2519">Comparison with communist countries like Russia is more difficult because of paucity and unreliability of evidence.</seg>
<seg id="2520">But if inequality is measured by differences in levels of living between the privileged and other classes, such inequality may well be decidedly less in capitalist than in communist countries.</seg>
<seg id="2521">Among the Western countries alone, inequality appears to be less, in any meaningful sense, the more highly capitalist the country is:</seg>
<seg id="2522">less in Britain than in France, less in the United States than in Britain ˇŞthough these comparisons are rendered difficult by the problem of allowing for the intrinsic heterogeneity of populations;</seg>
<seg id="2523">for a fair comparison, for example, one should perhaps compare the United States, not with the United Kingdom alone but with the United Kingdom plus the West Indies plus its African possessions.</seg>
<seg id="2524">With respect to changes over time, the economic progress achieved in the capitalist societies has been accompanied by a drastic diminution in inequality.</seg>
<seg id="2525">As late as 1848, John Stuart Mill could write, "Hitherto [1848] it is questionable if all the mechanical inventions yet made have lightened the day's toil of any human being.</seg>
<seg id="2526">They have enabled a greater population to live the same life of drudgery and imprisonment, and an increased number of manufacturers and others to make fortunes.</seg>
<seg id="2527">They have increased the comforts of the middle classes.</seg>
<seg id="2528">But they have not yet begun to effect those great changes in human destiny, which it is in their nature and in their futurity to accomplish."</seg>
<seg id="2529">This statement was probably not correct even for Mill's day, but certainly no one could write this today about the advanced capitalist countries.</seg>
<seg id="2530">It is still true about the rest of the world.</seg>
<seg id="2531">The chief characteristic of progress and development over the past century is that it has freed the masses from backbreaking toil and has made available to them products and services that were formerly the monopoly of the upper classes, without in any corresponding way expanding the products and services available to the wealthy.</seg>
<seg id="2532">Medicine aside, the advances in technology have for the most part simply made available to the masses of the people luxuries that were always available in one form or another to the truly wealthy.</seg>
<seg id="2533">Modern plumbing, central heating, automobiles, television, radio, to cite just a few examples, provide conveniences to the masses equivalent to those that the wealthy could always get by the use of servants, entertainers, and so on.</seg>
<seg id="2534">Detailed statistical evidence on these phenomena, in the form of meaningful and comparable distributions of income, is hard to come by, though such studies as have been made confirm the broad conclusions just outlined.</seg>
<seg id="2535">Such statistical data, however, can be extremely misleading.</seg>
<seg id="2536">They cannot segregate differences in income that are equalizing from those that are not.</seg>
<seg id="2537">For example, the short working life of a baseball player means that the annual income during his active years must be much higher than in alternative pursuits open to him to make it equally attractive financially.</seg>
<seg id="2538">But such a difference affects the figures in exactly the same way as any other difference</seg>
<seg id="2539">The income unit for which the figures are given is also of great importance.</seg>
<seg id="2540">A distribution for individual income recipients always shows very much greater apparent inequality than a distribution for family units:</seg>
