<seg id="2541">many of the individuals are housewives working part-time or receiving a small amount of property income, or other family members in a similar position.</seg>
<seg id="2542">Is the distribution that is relevant for families one in which the families are classified by total family income?</seg>
<seg id="2543">Or by income per person?</seg>
<seg id="2544">Or per equivalent unit?</seg>
<seg id="2545">This is no mere quibble.</seg>
<seg id="2546">I believe that the changing distribution of families by number of children is the most important single factor that has reduced inequality of levels of living in this country during the past half century.</seg>
<seg id="2547">It has been far more important than graduated inheritance and income taxes.</seg>
<seg id="2548">The really low levels of living were the joint product of relatively low family incomes and relatively large numbers of children.</seg>
<seg id="2549">The average number of children has declined and, even more important, this decline has been accompanied and largely produced by a virtual elimination of the very large family.</seg>
<seg id="2550">As a result, families now tend to differ much less with respect to number of children.</seg>
<seg id="2551">Yet this change would not be reflected in a distribution of families by the size of total family income.</seg>
<seg id="2552">A major problem in interpreting evidence on the distribution of income is the need to distinguish two basically different kinds of inequality;</seg>
<seg id="2553">temporary, short-run differences in income, and differences in long-run income status.</seg>
<seg id="2554">Consider two societies that have the same distribution of annual income.</seg>
<seg id="2555">In one there is great mobility and change so that the position of particular families in the income hierarchy varies widely from year to year.</seg>
<seg id="2556">In the other, there is great rigidity so that each family stays in the same position year after year.</seg>
<seg id="2557">Clearly, in any meaningful sense, the second would be the more unequal society.</seg>
<seg id="2558">The one kind of inequality is a sign of dynamic change, social mobility, equality of opportunity;</seg>
<seg id="2559">the other, of a status society.</seg>
<seg id="2560">The confusion of these two kinds of inequality is particularly important, precisely because competitive free-enterprise capitalism tends to substitute the one for the other.</seg>
<seg id="2561">Non-capitalist societies tend to have wider inequality than capitalist, even as measured by annual income;</seg>
<seg id="2562">in addition, inequality in them tends to be permanent, whereas capitalism undermines status and introduces social mobility.</seg>
<seg id="2563">Government Measures Used to alter the Distribution of Income</seg>
<seg id="2564">The methods that governments have used most widely to alter the distribution of income have been graduated income and inheritance taxation.</seg>
<seg id="2565">Before considering their desirability, it is worth asking whether they have succeeded in their aim.</seg>
<seg id="2566">No conclusive answer can be given to this question with our present knowledge.</seg>
<seg id="2567">The judgment that follows is a personal, though I hope not utterly uninformed, opinion, stated, for sake of brevity, more dogmatically than the nature of the evidence justifies.</seg>
<seg id="2568">My impression is that these tax measures have had a relatively minor, though not negligible, effect in the direction of narrowing the differences between the average position of groups of families classified by some statistical measures of income.</seg>
<seg id="2569">However, they have also introduced essentially arbitrary inequalities of comparable magnitude between persons within such income classes.</seg>
<seg id="2570">As a result, it is by no means clear whether the net effect in terms of the basic objective of equality of treatment or equality of outcome has been to increase or decrease equality.</seg>
<seg id="2571">The tax rates are on paper both high and highly graduated.</seg>
<seg id="2572">But their effect has been dissipated in two different ways.</seg>
<seg id="2573">First, part of their effect has been simply to make the pre-tax distribution more unequal.</seg>
<seg id="2574">This is the usual incidence effect of taxation.</seg>
<seg id="2575">By discouraging entry into activities highly taxedˇŞin this case activities with large risk and non-pecuniary disadvantagesˇŞthey raise returns in those activities.</seg>
<seg id="2576">Second, they have stimulated both legislative and other provisions to evade the taxˇŞso-called "loopholes" in the law such as percentage depletion, exemption of interest on state and municipal bonds, specially favorable treatment of capital gains, expense accounts, other indirect ways of payment, conversion of ordinary income to capital gains, and so on in bewildering number and kind.</seg>
<seg id="2577">The effect has been to make the actual rates imposed far lower than the nominal rates and, perhaps more important, to make the incidence of the taxes capricious and unequal.</seg>
<seg id="2578">People at the same economic level pay very different taxes depending on the accident of the source of their income and the opportunities they have to evade the tax.</seg>
<seg id="2579">If present rates were made fully effective, the effect on incentives and the like might well be so serious as to cause a radical loss in the productivity of the society.</seg>
<seg id="2580">Tax avoidance may therefore have been essential for economic wellbeing.</seg>
<seg id="2581">If so, the gain has been bought at the cost of a great waste of resources, and of the introduction of widespread inequity.</seg>
<seg id="2582">A much lower set of nominal rates, plus a more comprehensive base through more equal taxation of all sources of income could be both more progressive in average incidence, more equitable in detail, and less wasteful of resources.</seg>
<seg id="2583">This judgment that the personal income tax has been arbitrary in its impact and of limited effectiveness in reducing inequality is widely shared by students of the subject, including many who strongly favor the use of graduated taxation to reduce inequality.</seg>
<seg id="2584">They too urge that the top bracket rates be drastically reduced and the base broadened.</seg>
<seg id="2585">A further factor that has reduced the impact of the graduated tax structure on inequality of income and wealth is that these taxes are much less taxes on being wealthy than on becoming wealthy.</seg>
<seg id="2586">While they limit the use of the income from existing wealth, they impede even more strikinglyˇŞso far as they are effectiveˇŞthe accumulation of wealth.</seg>
<seg id="2587">The taxation of the income from the wealth does nothing to reduce the wealth itself, it simply reduces the level of consumption and additions to wealth that the owners can support.</seg>
<seg id="2588">The tax measures give an incentive to avoid risk and to embody existing wealth in relatively stable forms, which reduces the likelihood that existing accumulations of wealth will be dissipated.</seg>
<seg id="2589">On the other side, the major route to new accumulations is through large current incomes of which a large fraction is saved and invested in risky activities, some of which will yield high returns.</seg>
<seg id="2590">If the income tax were effective, it would close this route.</seg>
<seg id="2591">In consequence, its effect would be to protect existing holders of wealth from the competition of newcomers.</seg>
<seg id="2592">In practice, this effect is largely dissipated by the avoidance devices already referred to.</seg>
<seg id="2593">It is notable how large a fraction of the new accumulations have been in oil, where the percentage depletion allowances provide a particularly easy route to the receipt of tax-free income.</seg>
<seg id="2594">In judging the desirability of graduated income taxation it seems to me important to distinguish two problems, even though the distinction cannot be precise in application:</seg>
<seg id="2595">first, the raising of funds to finance the expenses of those governmental activities it is decided to undertake (including perhaps measures to eliminate poverty discussed in chapter xii);</seg>
<seg id="2596">second, the imposition of taxes for redistributive purposes alone.</seg>
<seg id="2597">The former might well call for some measure of graduation, both on grounds of assessing costs in accordance with benefits and on grounds of social standards of equity.</seg>
<seg id="2598">But the present high nominal rates on top brackets of income and inheritance can hardly be justified on this groundˇŞif only because their yield is so low.</seg>
<seg id="2599">I find it hard, as a liberal, to see any justification for graduated taxation solely to redistribute income.</seg>
<seg id="2600">This seems a clear case of using coercion to take from some in order to give to others and thus to conflict head-on with individual freedom.</seg>
<seg id="2601">All things considered, the personal income tax structure that seems to me best is a flat-rate tax on income above an exemption, with income defined very broadly and deductions allowed only for strictly defined expenses of earning income.</seg>
<seg id="2602">As already suggested in chapter v, I would combine this program with the abolition of the corporate income tax, and with the requirement that corporations be required to attribute their income to stockholders, and that stockholders be required to include such sums on their tax returns.</seg>
<seg id="2603">The most important other desirable changes are the elimination of percentage depletion on oil and other raw materials, the elimination of tax exemption of interest on state and local securities, the elimination of special treatment of capital gains, the coordination of income, estate, and gift taxes, and the elimination of numerous deductions now allowed.</seg>
<seg id="2604">An exemption, it seems to me, can be a justified degree of graduation (see further discussion in chapter xii).</seg>
<seg id="2605">It is very different for 90 per cent of the population to vote taxes on themselves and an exemption for 10 per cent than for 90 per cent to vote punitive taxes on the other 10 per centˇŞ which is in effect what has been done in the United States.</seg>
<seg id="2606">A proportional flat-rate-tax would involve higher absolute payments by persons with higher incomes for governmental services, which is not clearly inappropriate on grounds of benefits conferred.</seg>
<seg id="2607">Yet it would avoid a situation where any large numbers could vote to impose on others taxes that did not also affect their own tax burden.</seg>
<seg id="2608">The proposal to substitute a flat-rate income tax for the present graduated rate structure will strike many a reader as a radical proposal.</seg>
<seg id="2609">And so it is in terms of concept.</seg>
<seg id="2610">For this very reason, it cannot be too strongly emphasized that it is not radical in terms of revenue yield, redistribution of income, or any other relevant criterion.</seg>
<seg id="2611">Our present income tax rates range from 20 per cent to 91 per cent, with the rate reaching 50 per cent on the excess of taxable incomes over $ 18,000 for single taxpayers or $ 36,000 for married taxpayers filing joint returns.</seg>
<seg id="2612">Yet a flat rate of 23 1/2 per cent on taxable income as presently reported and presently defined, that is, above present exemptions and after all presently allowable deductions, would yield as much revenue as the present highly graduated rate.</seg>
<seg id="2613">In fact, such a flat rate, even with no change whatsoever in other features of the law, would yield a higher revenue because a larger amount of taxable income would be reported for three reasons:</seg>
<seg id="2614">there would be less incentive than now to adopt legal but costly schemes that reduce the amount of taxable income reported (so-called tax avoidance);</seg>
<seg id="2615">there would be less incentive to fail to report income that legally should be reported (tax evasion);</seg>
<seg id="2616">the removal of the disincentive effects of the present structure of rates would produce a more efficient use of present resources and a higher income.</seg>
<seg id="2617">If the yield of the present highly graduated rates is so low,</seg>
<seg id="2618">This point is so important that it may be worth giving the figures and calculations.</seg>
<seg id="2619">This does not mean that they do no harm.</seg>
<seg id="2620">On the contrary.</seg>
<seg id="2621">The yield is so low partly because some of the most competent men in the country devote their energies to devising ways to keep it so low;</seg>
<seg id="2622">and because many other men shape their activities with one eye on tax effects.</seg>
<seg id="2623">All this is sheer waste.</seg>
<seg id="2624">And what do we get for it?</seg>
<seg id="2625">At most, a feeling of satisfaction on the part of some that the state is redistributing income.</seg>
<seg id="2626">And even this feeling is founded on ignorance of the actual effects of the graduated tax structure, and would surely evaporate if the facts were known.</seg>
<seg id="2627">To return to the distribution of income, there is a clear justification for social action of a very different kind than taxation to affect the distribution of income.</seg>
<seg id="2628">Much of the actual inequality derives from imperfections of the market.</seg>
<seg id="2629">Many of these have themselves been created by government action or could be removed by government action.</seg>
<seg id="2630">There is every reason to adjust the rules of the game so as to eliminate these sources of inequality.</seg>
<seg id="2631">For example, special monopoly privileges granted by government, tariffs, and other legal enactments benefiting particular groups, are a source of inequality.</seg>
<seg id="2632">The removal of these, the liberal will welcome.</seg>
<seg id="2633">The extension and widening of educational opportunities has been a major factor tending to reduce inequalities.</seg>
<seg id="2634">Measures such as these have the operational virtue that they strike at the sources of inequality rather than simply alleviating the symptoms.</seg>
<seg id="2635">The distribution of income is still another area in which government has been doing more harm by one set of measures than it has been able to undo by others.</seg>
<seg id="2636">It is another example of the justification of government intervention in terms of alleged defects of the private enterprise system when many of the phenomena of which champions of big government complain are themselves the creation of government, big and small.</seg>
<seg id="2637">Chapter XIˇŞ Social Welfare Measures</seg>
<seg id="2638">The humanitarian and egalitarian sentiment which helped produce the steeply graduated individual income tax has also produced a host of other measures directed at promoting the ''welfare" of particular groups.</seg>
<seg id="2639">The most important single set of measures is the bundle misleadingly labeled "social security."</seg>
<seg id="2640">Others are public housing, minimum wage laws, farm price supports, medical care for particular groups, special aid programs, and so on.</seg>
<seg id="2641">I shall first discuss briefly a few of the latter, mostly to indicate how different their actual effects may be from those intended, and shall then discuss at somewhat greater length the largest single component of the social security program, old age and survivor's insurance.</seg>
<seg id="2642">Miscellaneous Welfare Measure</seg>
<seg id="2643">I.Public Housing</seg>
<seg id="2644">One argument frequently made for public housing is based on an alleged neighborhood effect:</seg>
<seg id="2645">slum districts in particular, and other low quality housing to a lesser degree, are said to impose higher costs on the community in the form of fire and police protection.</seg>
<seg id="2646">This literal neighborhood effect may well exist.</seg>
<seg id="2647">But insofar as it does, it alone argues, not for public housing, but for higher taxes on the kind of housing that adds to social costs since this would tend to equalize private and social cost.</seg>
<seg id="2648">It will be answered at once that the extra taxes would bear on low-income people and that this is undesirable.</seg>
<seg id="2649">The answer means that public housing is proposed not on the ground of neighborhood effects but as a means of helping low-income people.</seg>
<seg id="2650">If this be the case, why subsidize housing in particular?</seg>
<seg id="2651">If funds are to be used to help the poor, would they not be used more effectively by being given in cash rather than in kind?</seg>
<seg id="2652">Surely, the families being helped would rather have" a given sum in cash than in the form of housing.</seg>
<seg id="2653">They could themselves spend the money on housing if they so desired.</seg>
<seg id="2654">Hence, they would never be worse off if given cash;</seg>
<seg id="2655">if they regarded other needs as more important, they would be better off.</seg>
<seg id="2656">The cash subsidy would solve the neighborhood effect as well as the subsidy in kind, since if it were not used to buy housing it would be available to pay extra taxes justified by the neighborhood effect.</seg>
<seg id="2657">Public housing cannot therefore be justified on the grounds either of neighborhood effects or of helping poor families.</seg>
<seg id="2658">It can be justified, if at all, only on grounds of paternalism;</seg>
<seg id="2659">that the families being helped "need" housing more than they "need" other things but would themselves either not agree or would spend the money unwisely.</seg>
<seg id="2660">The liberal will be inclined to reject this argument for responsible adults.</seg>
<seg id="2661">He cannot completely reject it in the more indirect form in which it affects children;</seg>
<seg id="2662">namely, that parents will neglect the welfare of the children, who ''need" the better housing.</seg>
<seg id="2663">But he will surely demand evidence much more persuasive and to the point than the kind usually given before he can accept this final argument as adequate justification for large expenditures on public housing.</seg>
<seg id="2664">So much could have been said in the abstract, in advance of actual experience with public housing.</seg>
<seg id="2665">Now that we have had experience, we can go much farther.</seg>
<seg id="2666">In practice, public housing has turned out to have effects very different indeed from those intended.</seg>
<seg id="2667">Far from improving the housing of the poor, as its proponents expected, public housing has done just the reverse.</seg>
<seg id="2668">The number of dwelling units destroyed in the course of erecting public housing projects has been far larger than the number of new dwelling units constructed.</seg>
<seg id="2669">But public housing as such has done nothing to reduce the number of persons to be housed.</seg>
<seg id="2670">The effect of public housing has therefore been to raise the number of persons per dwelling unit.</seg>
<seg id="2671">Some families have probably been better housed than they would otherwise have beenˇŞthose who were fortunate enough to get occupancy of the publicly built units.</seg>
<seg id="2672">But this has only made the problem for the rest all the worse, since the average density of all together went up.</seg>
<seg id="2673">Of course, private enterprise offset some of the deleterious effect of the public housing program by conversion of existing quarters and construction of new ones for either the persons directly displaced or, more generally, the persons displaced at one or two removes in the game of musical chairs set in motion by the public housing projects.</seg>
<seg id="2674">However, these private resources would have been available in the absence of the public housing program.</seg>
<seg id="2675">Why did the public housing program have this effect?</seg>
<seg id="2676">For the general reason we have stressed time and again.</seg>
<seg id="2677">The general interest that motivated many to favor instituting the program is diffuse and transitory.</seg>
<seg id="2678">Once the program was adopted, it was bound to be dominated by the special interests that it could serve.</seg>
<seg id="2679">In this case, the special interests were those local groups that were anxious to have blighted areas cleared and refurbished, either because they owned property there or because the blight was threatening local or central business districts.</seg>
<seg id="2680">Public housing served as a convenient means to accomplish their objective, which required more destruction than construction.</seg>
<seg id="2681">Even so, "urban blight" is still with us in undiminished force, to judge by the growing pressure for federal funds to deal with it.</seg>
<seg id="2682">Another gain its proponents expected from public housing was the reduction of juvenile delinquency by improving housing conditions.</seg>
<seg id="2683">Here again, the program in many instances had precisely the opposite effect, entirely aside from its failure to improve average housing conditions.</seg>
<seg id="2684">The income limitations quite properly imposed for the occupancy of public housing at subsidized rentals have led to a very high density of "broken" families ˇŞin particular, divorced or widowed mothers with children.</seg>
<seg id="2685">Children of broken families are especially likely to be "problem" children and a high concentration of such children is likely to increase juvenile delinquency.</seg>
<seg id="2686">One manifestation has been the very adverse effect on schools in the neighborhood of a public housing project.</seg>
<seg id="2687">Whereas a school can readily absorb a few "problem" children it is very difficult for it to absorb a large number.</seg>
<seg id="2688">Yet in some cases, broken families are a third or more of the total in a public housing project and the project may account for a majority of the children in the school.</seg>
<seg id="2689">Had these families been assisted through cash grants, they would have been spread much more thinly through the community.</seg>
<seg id="2690">2.Minimum wage laws</seg>
<seg id="2691">Minimum wage laws are about as clear a case as one can find of a measure the effects of which are precisely the opposite of those intended by the men of good will who support it.</seg>
<seg id="2692">Many proponents of minimum wage laws quite properly deplore extremely low rates;</seg>
<seg id="2693">they regard them as a sign of poverty;</seg>
<seg id="2694">and they hope, by outlawing wage rates below some specified level, to reduce poverty.</seg>
<seg id="2695">In fact, insofar as minimum wage laws have any effect at all, their effect is clearly to increase poverty.</seg>
<seg id="2696">The state can legislate a minimum wage rate.</seg>
<seg id="2697">It can hardly require employers to hire at that minimum all who were formerly employed at wages below the minimum.</seg>
<seg id="2698">It is clearly not in the interest of employers to do so.</seg>
<seg id="2699">The effect of the minimum wage is therefore to make unemployment higher than it otherwise would be.</seg>
<seg id="2700">Insofar as the low wage rates are in fact a sign of poverty, the people who are rendered unemployed are precisely those who can least afford to give up the income they had been receiving, small as it may appear to the people voting for the minimum wage.</seg>
<seg id="2701">This case is in one respect very much like public housing.</seg>
<seg id="2702">In both, the people who are helped are visibleˇŞthe people whose wages are raised;</seg>
<seg id="2703">the people who occupy the publicly built units.</seg>
<seg id="2704">The people who are hurt are anonymous and their problem is not clearly connected to its cause:</seg>
<seg id="2705">the people who join the ranks of the unemployed or, more likely, are never employed in particular activities because of the existence of the minimum wage and are driven to even less remunerative activities or to the relief rolls;</seg>
<seg id="2706">the people who are pressed ever closer together in the spreading slums that seem to be rather a sign of the need for more public housing than a consequence of the existing public housing.</seg>
<seg id="2707">A large part of the support for minimum wage laws comes not from disinterested men of good will but from interested parties.</seg>
<seg id="2708">For example, northern trade unions and northern firms threatened by southern competition favor minimum wage laws to reduce the competition from the South.</seg>
<seg id="2709">3.Farm price supports</seg>
<seg id="2710">Farm price supports are another example.</seg>
<seg id="2711">Insofar as they can be justified at all on grounds other than the political fact that rural areas are over-represented in the electoral college and Congress, it must be on the belief that farmers on the average have low incomes.</seg>
<seg id="2712">Even if this be accepted as a fact, farm price supports do not accomplish the intended purpose of helping the farmers who need help.</seg>
<seg id="2713">In the first place, benefits arc, if anything, inverse to need, since they are in proportion to the amount sold on the market.</seg>
<seg id="2714">The impecunious farmer not only sells less on the market than the wealthier farmer;</seg>
<seg id="2715">in addition, he gets a larger fraction of his income from products grown for his own use, and these do not qualify for the benefits.</seg>
<seg id="2716">In the second place, the benefits, if any, to farmers from the price-support program are much smaller than the total amount spent.</seg>
<seg id="2717">This is clearly true of the amount spent for storage and similar costs which does not go to the farmer at allˇŞindeed the suppliers of storage capacity and facilities may well be the major beneficiaries.</seg>
<seg id="2718">It is equally true of the amount spent to purchase agricultural products.</seg>
<seg id="2719">The farmer is thereby induced to spend additional sums on fertilizer, seed, machinery, etc.</seg>
<seg id="2720">At most, only the excess adds to his income.</seg>
<seg id="2721">And finally, even this residual of a residual overstates the gain since the effect of the program has been to keep more people on the farm than would otherwise have stayed there.</seg>
<seg id="2722">Only the excess, if any, of what they can earn on the farm with the price-support program over what they can earn off the farm, is a net benefit to them.</seg>
<seg id="2723">The main effect of the purchase program has simply been to make farm output larger, not to raise the income per farmer.</seg>
<seg id="2724">Some of the costs of the farm purchase program are so obvious and well-known as to need little more than mention:</seg>
<seg id="2725">the consumer has paid twice, once in taxes for farm benefit payments, again by paying a higher price for food;</seg>
<seg id="2726">the farmer has been saddled with onerous restrictions and detailed centralized control;</seg>
<seg id="2727">the nation has been saddled with a spreading bureaucracy.</seg>
<seg id="2728">There is, however, one set of costs which is less well known.</seg>
<seg id="2729">The farm program has been a major hindrance in the pursuit of foreign policy.</seg>
<seg id="2730">In order to maintain a higher domestic than world price, it has been necessary to impose quotas on imports for many items.</seg>
<seg id="2731">Erratic changes in our policy have had serious adverse effects on other countries.</seg>
<seg id="2732">A high price for cotton encouraged other countries to enlarge their cotton production.</seg>
<seg id="2733">When our high price led to an unwieldy stock of cotton, we proceeded to sell overseas at low prices and imposed heavy losses on the producers whom we had by our earlier actions encouraged to expand output.</seg>
<seg id="2734">The list of similar cases could be multiplied.</seg>
<seg id="2735">Old Age and Survivor's Insurance</seg>
<seg id="2736">The "social security" program is one of those things on which the tyranny of the status quo is beginning to work its magic.</seg>
<seg id="2737">Despite the controversy that surrounded its inception, it has come to be so much taken for granted that its desirability is hardly questioned any longer.</seg>
<seg id="2738">Yet it involves a large-scale invasion into the personal lives of a large fraction of the nation without, so far as I can see, any justification that is at all persuasive, not only on liberal principles, but on almost any other.</seg>
<seg id="2739">I propose to examine the biggest phase of it, that which involves payments to the aged.</seg>
<seg id="2740">As an operational matter, the program known as old age and survivor's insurance (OASI) consists of a special tax imposed on payrolls, plus payments to persons who have reached a specified age, of amounts determined by the age at which payments begin, family status, and prior earning record.</seg>
<seg id="2741">As an analytical matter, OASI consists of three separable elements:</seg>
<seg id="2742">1. The requirement that a wide class of persons must purchase specified annuities, i.e., compulsory provision for old age.</seg>
<seg id="2743">2.The requirement that the annuity must be purchased from the government;</seg>
<seg id="2744">i.e., nationalization of the provision of these annuities.</seg>
<seg id="2745">3.A scheme for redistributing income, insofar as the value of the annuities to which people are entitled when they enter the system is not equal to the taxes they will pay.</seg>
<seg id="2746">Clearly, there is no necessity for these elements to be combined.</seg>
<seg id="2747">Each individual could be required to pay for his own annuity;</seg>
<seg id="2748">the individual could be permitted to purchase an annuity from private firms;</seg>
<seg id="2749">yet individuals could be required to purchase specified annuities.</seg>
<seg id="2750">The government could go into the business of selling annuities without compelling individuals to buy specified annuities and require the business to be self-supporting.</seg>
<seg id="2751">And clearly the government can and does engage in redistribution without using the device of annuities.</seg>
<seg id="2752">Let us therefore consider each of these elements in turn to see how far, if at all, each can be justified.</seg>
<seg id="2753">It will facilitate our analysis, I believe, if we consider them in reverse order.</seg>
<seg id="2754">1.Income redistribution.</seg>
<seg id="2755">The present OASI program involves two major kinds of redistribution;</seg>
<seg id="2756">from some OASI beneficiaries to others;</seg>
<seg id="2757">from the general taxpayer to OASI beneficiaries.</seg>
<seg id="2758">The first kind of redistribution is primarily from those who entered the system relatively young, to those who entered it at an advanced age.</seg>
<seg id="2759">The latter are receiving, and will for some time be receiving, a greater amount as benefits than the taxes they paid could have purchased.</seg>
<seg id="2760">Under present tax and benefit schedules, on the other hand, those who entered the system at a young age will receive decidedly less.</seg>
<seg id="2761">I do not see any groundsˇŞliberal or otherˇŞon which this particular redistribution can be defended.</seg>
<seg id="2762">The subsidy to the beneficiaries is independent of their poverty or wealth;</seg>
<seg id="2763">the man of means receives it as much as the indigent.</seg>
<seg id="2764">The tax which pays the subsidy is a flat-rate tax on earnings up to a maximum.</seg>
<seg id="2765">It constitutes a larger fraction of low incomes than of high.</seg>
<seg id="2766">What conceivable justification is there for taxing the young to subsidize the old regardless of the economic status of the old;</seg>
<seg id="2767">for imposing a higher rate of tax for this purpose on the low incomes than on the high;</seg>
<seg id="2768">or, for that matter, for raising the revenues to pay the subsidy by a tax on payrolls?</seg>
<seg id="2769">The second kind of redistribution arises because the system is not likely to be fully self-financing.</seg>
<seg id="2770">During the period when many were covered and paying taxes, and few had qualified for benefits, the system appeared to be self-financing and indeed to be having a surplus.</seg>
<seg id="2771">But this appearance depends on neglecting the obligation being accumulated with respect to the persons paying the tax.</seg>
<seg id="2772">It is doubtful that the taxes paid have sufficed to finance the accumulated obligation.</seg>
<seg id="2773">Many experts assert that even on a cash basis, a subsidy will be required.</seg>
<seg id="2774">And such a subsidy generally has been required in similar systems in other countries.</seg>
<seg id="2775">This is a highly technical matter which we cannot and need not go into here and about which there can be honest differences of opinion.</seg>
<seg id="2776">For our purpose, it is enough to ask only the hypothetical question whether a subsidy from the general taxpayer could be justified if it is required.</seg>
<seg id="2777">I see no grounds on which such a subsidy can be justified.</seg>
<seg id="2778">We may wish to help poor people.</seg>
<seg id="2779">Is there any justification for helping people whether they are poor or not because they happen to be a certain age?</seg>
<seg id="2780">Is this not an entirely arbitrary redistribution?</seg>
<seg id="2781">The only argument I have ever come across to justify the redistribution involved in OASI is one that I regard as thoroughly immoral despite its wide use.</seg>
<seg id="2782">This argument is that OASI redistribution on the average helps low-income people more than high-income people despite a large arbitrary element;</seg>
<seg id="2783">that it would be better to do this redistribution more efficiently;</seg>
<seg id="2784">but that the community will not vote for the redistribution directly though it will vote for it as part of a social security package.</seg>
<seg id="2785">In essence, what this argument says is that the community can be fooled into voting for a measure that it opposes by presenting the measure in a false guise.</seg>
<seg id="2786">Needless to say, the people who argue this way are the loudest in their condemnation of "misleading" commercial advertising!</seg>
<seg id="2787">2.Nationalization of the provision of required annuities</seg>
<seg id="2788">Suppose we avoid redistribution by requiring each person to pay for the annuity he gets, in the sense of course, that the premium suffices to cover the present value of the annuity, account being taken both of mortality and interest returns.</seg>
<seg id="2789">What justification is there then for requiring him to purchase it from a governmental concern?</seg>
<seg id="2790">If redistribution is to be accomplished, clearly the taxing power of the government must be used.</seg>
<seg id="2791">But if redistribution is to be no part of the program and, as we have just seen, it is hard to see any justification for making it part, why not permit individuals who wish to do so to purchase their annuities from private concerns?</seg>
<seg id="2792">A close analogy is provided by state laws requiring compulsory purchase of automobile liability insurance.</seg>
<seg id="2793">So far as I know, no state which has such a law even has a state insurance company, let alone compels automobile owners to buy their insurance from a government agency.</seg>
<seg id="2794">Possible economies of scale are no argument for nationalizing the provision of annuities.</seg>
<seg id="2795">If they are present, and the government sets up a concern to sell annuity contracts, it may be able to undersell competitors by virtue of its size.</seg>
<seg id="2796">In that case, it will get the business without compulsion.</seg>
<seg id="2797">A case can be made for using federal funds to supplement schooling expenditures in the states with the lowest incomes, on the grounds that the children schooled may migrate to other states.</seg>
<seg id="2798">One possible advantage of nationalizing the provision of annuities is to facilitate the enforcement of compulsory purchase of annuities.</seg>
<seg id="2799">However, this seems a rather trivial advantage.</seg>
<seg id="2800">It would be easy to devise alternative administrative arrangements, such as requiring individuals to include a copy of a receipt for premium payments along with their income tax returns;</seg>
<seg id="2801">or having their employers certify to their having met the requirement.</seg>
<seg id="2802">The administrative problem would surely be minor compared with that imposed by the present arrangements.</seg>
<seg id="2803">The costs of nationalization seem clearly to outweigh any such trivial advantage.</seg>
<seg id="2804">Here, as elsewhere, individual freedom to choose, and competition of private enterprises for custom, would promote improvements in the kinds of contracts available, and foster variety and diversity to meet individual need.</seg>
<seg id="2805">On the political level, there is the obvious gain from avoiding an expansion in the scale of governmental activity and the indirect threat to freedom of every such expansion.</seg>
<seg id="2806">Some less obvious political costs arise from the character of the present program.</seg>
<seg id="2807">The issues involved become very technical and complex.</seg>
<seg id="2808">The layman is often incompetent to judge them.</seg>
<seg id="2809">Nationalization means that the bulk of the "experts" become employees of the nationalized system, or university people closely linked with it.</seg>
<seg id="2810">Inevitably, they come to favor its expansion, not, I hasten to add, out of narrow self-interest but because they are operating within a framework in which they take for granted governmental administration and are familiar only with its techniques.</seg>
<seg id="2811">The only saving grace in the United States so far has been the existence of private insurance companies involved in similar activities.</seg>
<seg id="2812">Effective control by Congress over the operations of such agencies as the Social Security Administration becomes essentially impossible as a result of the technical character of their task and their near-monopoly of experts.</seg>
<seg id="2813">They become self-governing bodies whose proposals are in the main rubber-stamped by Congress.</seg>
<seg id="2814">The able and ambitious men who make their careers in them are naturally anxious to expand the scope of their agencies and it is exceedingly difficult to prevent them from doing so.</seg>
<seg id="2815">If the expert says yea, who is there competent to say nay?</seg>
<seg id="2816">So we have seen an increasing fraction of the population drawn into the social security system, and now that there remain few possibilities of expansion in that direction, we are seeing a move toward the addition of new programs, such as medical care.</seg>
<seg id="2817">I conclude that the case against the nationalization of the provision of annuities is exceedingly strong, not only in terms of liberal principles but even in terms of the values expressed by proponents of the welfare state.</seg>
<seg id="2818">If they believe that the government can provide the service better than the market, they should favor a government concern to issue annuities in open competition with other concerns.</seg>
<seg id="2819">If they are right, the government concern will prosper.</seg>
<seg id="2820">If they are wrong, the welfare of the people will be advanced by having a private alternative.</seg>
<seg id="2821">Only the doctrinaire socialist, or the believer in centralized control for its own sake, can, so far as I can see, take a stand on principle in favor of nationalization of the provision of annuities.</seg>
<seg id="2822">3.Compulsory Purchase of Annuities</seg>
<seg id="2823">Having cleared away the underbrush, we are now ready to face the key issue:</seg>
<seg id="2824">compelling individuals to use some of their current income to purchase annuities to provide for their old age.</seg>
<seg id="2825">One possible justification for such compulsion is strictly paternalistic.</seg>
<seg id="2826">People could if they wished decide to do individually what the law requires them to do as a group.</seg>
<seg id="2827">But they are separately shortsighted and improvident.</seg>
<seg id="2828">"We" know better than "they" that it is in their own good to provide for their old age to a greater extent than they would voluntarily;</seg>
<seg id="2829">we cannot persuade them individually;</seg>
<seg id="2830">but we can persuade 51 per cent or more to compel all to do what is in their own good.</seg>
<seg id="2831">This paternalism is for responsible people, hence docs not even have the excuse of concern for children or madmen.</seg>
<seg id="2832">This position is internally consistent and logical.</seg>
<seg id="2833">A thoroughgoing paternalist who holds it cannot be dissuaded by being shown that he is making a mistake in logic.</seg>
<seg id="2834">He is our opponent on grounds of principle, not simply a well-meaning but misguided friend.</seg>
<seg id="2835">Basically, he believes in dictatorship, benevolent and maybe majoritarian, but dictatorship none the less.</seg>
<seg id="2836">Those of us who believe in freedom must believe also in the freedom of individuals to make their own mistakes.</seg>
<seg id="2837">If a man knowingly prefers to live for today, to use his resources for current enjoyment, deliberately choosing a penurious old age, by what right do we prevent him from doing so?</seg>
<seg id="2838">We may argue with him, seek to persuade him that he is wrong, but are we entitled to use coercion to prevent him from doing what he chooses to do?</seg>
<seg id="2839">Is there not always the possibility that he is right and that we are wrong?</seg>
<seg id="2840">Humility is the distinguishing virtue of the believer in freedom; arrogance, of the paternalist.</seg>
<seg id="2841">Few people are thoroughgoing paternalists.</seg>
<seg id="2842">It is a position that is most unattractive if examined in the cold light of the day.</seg>
<seg id="2843">Yet the paternalistic argument has played so large a role in measures like social security that it seems worth making it explicit.</seg>
<seg id="2844">A possible justification on liberal principles for compulsory purchase of annuities is that the improvident will not suffer the consequence of their own action but will impose costs on others.</seg>
<seg id="2845">We shall not, it is said, be willing to see the indigent aged suffer in dire poverty.</seg>
<seg id="2846">We shall assist them by private and public charity.</seg>
<seg id="2847">Hence the man who does not provide for his old age will become a public charge.</seg>
<seg id="2848">Compelling him to buy an annuity is justified not for his own good but for the good of the rest of us.</seg>
<seg id="2849">The weight of this argument clearly depends on fact.</seg>
<seg id="2850">If 90 per cent of the population would become charges on the public at age 65 in the absence of compulsory purchase of annuities, the argument would have great weight.</seg>
<seg id="2851">If only 1 per cent would, the argument has none.</seg>
<seg id="2852">Why restrict the freedom of 99 per cent to avoid the costs that the other 1 per cent would impose on the community?</seg>
<seg id="2853">The belief that a large fraction of the community would become public charges if not compelled to purchase annuities owed its plausibility, at the time OASI was enacted, to the Great Depression.</seg>
<seg id="2854">In every year from 1931 through 1940, more than one-seventh of the labor force was unemployed.</seg>
<seg id="2855">And unemployment was proportionately heavier among the older workers.</seg>
<seg id="2856">This experience was unprecedented and has not been repeated since.</seg>
<seg id="2857">It did not arise because people were improvident and failed to provide for their old age.</seg>
<seg id="2858">It was a consequence, as we have seen, of government mismanagement.</seg>
<seg id="2859">OASI is a cure, if cure it be at all, for a very different malady and one of which we have had no experience.</seg>
<seg id="2860">The unemployed of the 1930's certainly created a serious problem of the relief of distress, of many people becoming public charges.</seg>
<seg id="2861">But old-age was by no means the most serious problem.</seg>
<seg id="2862">Many people in productive ages were on the relief or assistance rolls.</seg>
<seg id="2863">And the steady spread of OASI, until today more than sixteen million persons receive benefits, has not prevented a continued growth in the number receiving public assistance.</seg>
<seg id="2864">Private arrangements for the care of the aged have altered greatly over time.</seg>
<seg id="2865">Children were at one time the major means whereby people provided for their own old age.</seg>
<seg id="2866">As the community became more affluent, the mores changed.</seg>
<seg id="2867">The responsibilities imposed on children to care for their parents declined and more and more people came to make provision for old age in the form of accumulating property or acquiring private pension rights.</seg>
<seg id="2868">More recently, the development of pension plans over and above OASI has accelerated.</seg>
<seg id="2869">Indeed, some students believe that a continuation of present trends points to a society in which a large fraction of the public scrimps in their productive years to provide themselves with a higher standard of life in old age than they ever enjoyed in the prime of life.</seg>
<seg id="2870">Some of us may think such a trend perverse, but if it reflects the tastes of the community, so be it.</seg>
<seg id="2871">Compulsory purchase of annuities has therefore imposed large costs for little gain.</seg>
<seg id="2872">It has deprived all of us of control over a sizable fraction of our income, requiring us to devote it to a particular purpose, purchase of a retirement annuity, in a particular way, by buying it from a government concern.</seg>
<seg id="2873">It has inhibited competition in the sale of annuities and the development of retirement arrangements.</seg>
<seg id="2874">It has given birth to a large bureaucracy that shows tendencies of growing by what it feeds on, of extending its scope from one area of our life to another.</seg>
<seg id="2875">And all this, to avoid the danger that a few people might become charges on the public.</seg>
<seg id="2876">Chapter XIIˇŞ The Alleviation of Poverty</seg>
<seg id="2877">The extraordinary economic growth experienced by Western countries during the past two centuries and the wide distribution of the benefits of free enterprise have enormously reduced the extent of poverty in any absolute sense in the capitalistic countries of the West.</seg>
<seg id="2878">But poverty is in part a relative matter, and even in these countries, there are clearly many people living under conditions that the rest of us label as poverty.</seg>
<seg id="2879">One recourse, and in many ways the most desirable, is private charity.</seg>
<seg id="2880">It is noteworthy that the heyday of laissez-faire, the middle and late nineteenth century in Britain and the United States, saw an extraordinary proliferation of private eleemosynary organizations and institutions.</seg>
<seg id="2881">One of the major costs of the extension of governmental welfare activities has been the corresponding decline in private charitable activities.</seg>
<seg id="2882">It can be argued that private charity is insufficient because the benefits from it accrue to people other than those who make the giftsˇŞagain, a neighborhood effect.</seg>
<seg id="2883">I am distressed by the sight of poverty;</seg>
<seg id="2884">I am benefited by its alleviation;</seg>
<seg id="2885">but I am benefited equally whether I or someone else pays for its alleviation;</seg>
<seg id="2886">the benefits of other people's charity therefore partly accrue to me.</seg>
<seg id="2887">To put it differently, we might all of us be willing to contribute to the relief of poverty, provided everyone else did.</seg>
<seg id="2888">We might not be willing to contribute the same amount without such assurance.</seg>
<seg id="2889">In small communities, public pressure can suffice to realize the proviso even with private charity.</seg>
<seg id="2890">In the large impersonal communities that are increasingly coming to dominate our society, it is much more difficult for it to do so.</seg>
<seg id="2891">Suppose one accepts, as I do, this line of reasoning as justifying governmental action to alleviate poverty;</seg>
<seg id="2892">to set, as it were, a floor under the standard of life of every person in the community.</seg>
<seg id="2893">There remain the questions, how much and how.</seg>
<seg id="2894">I see no way of deciding ''how much" except in terms of the amount of taxes weˇŞby which I mean the great bulk of usˇŞare willing to impose on ourselves for the purpose.</seg>
<seg id="2895">The question, "how," affords more room for speculation.</seg>
<seg id="2896">Two things seem clear.</seg>
<seg id="2897">First, if the objective is to alleviate poverty, we should have a program directed at helping the poor.</seg>
<seg id="2898">There is every reason to help the poor man who happens to be a farmer, not because he is a farmer but because he is poor.</seg>
<seg id="2899">The program, that is, should be designed to help people as people not as members of particular occupational groups or age groups or wage-rate groups or labor organizations or industries.</seg>
<seg id="2900">This is a defect of farm programs, general old-age benefits, minimum-wage laws, pro-union legislation, tariffs, licensing provisions of crafts or professions, and so on in seemingly endless profusion.</seg>
<seg id="2901">Second, so far as possible the program should, while operating through the market, not distort the market or impede its functioning.</seg>
<seg id="2902">This is a defect of price supports, minimum-wage laws, tariffs and the like.</seg>
<seg id="2903">The arrangement that recommends itself on purely mechanical grounds is a negative income tax.</seg>
<seg id="2904">We now have an exemption of $ 600 per person under the federal income tax (plus a minimum 10 per cent flat deduction).</seg>
<seg id="2905">If an individual receives $ 100 taxable income, i.e., an income of $ 100 in excess of the exemption and deductions, he pays a tax.</seg>
<seg id="2906">Under the proposal, if his taxable income minus $ 100, i.e., $ 100 less than the exemption plus deductions, he would pay a negative tax, i.e., receive a subsidy.</seg>
<seg id="2907">If the rate of subsidy were, say, 50 per cent, he would receive $ 50.</seg>
<seg id="2908">If he had no income at all, and, for simplicity, no deductions, and the rate were constant, he would receive $ 300.</seg>
<seg id="2909">He might receive more than this if he had deductions, for example, for medical expenses, so that his income less deductions, was negative even before subtracting the exemption.</seg>
<seg id="2910">The rates of subsidy could, of course, be graduated just as the rates of tax above the exemption are.</seg>
<seg id="2911">In this way, it would be possible to set a floor below which no man's net income (defined now to include the subsidy) could fallˇŞin the simple example $ 300 per person.</seg>
<seg id="2912">The precise floor set would depend on what the community could afford.</seg>
<seg id="2913">The advantages of this arrangement are clear.</seg>
<seg id="2914">It is directed specifically at the problem of poverty.</seg>
<seg id="2915">It gives help in the form most useful to the individual, namely, cash.</seg>
<seg id="2916">It is general and could be substituted for the host of special measures now in effect.</seg>
<seg id="2917">It makes explicit the cost borne by society.</seg>
<seg id="2918">It operates outside the market.</seg>
<seg id="2919">Like any other measures to alleviate poverty, it reduces the incentives of those helped to help themselves, but it does not eliminate that incentive entirely, as a system of supplementing incomes up to some fixed minimum would.</seg>
<seg id="2920">An extra dollar earned always means more money available for expenditure.</seg>
<seg id="2921">No doubt there would be problems of administration, but these seem to me a minor disadvantage, if they be a disadvantage at all.</seg>
<seg id="2922">The system would fit directly into our current income tax system and could be administered along with it.</seg>
<seg id="2923">The present tax system covers the bulk of income recipients and the necessity of covering all would have the by-product of improving the operation of the present income tax.</seg>
<seg id="2924">More important, if enacted as a substitute for the present rag bag of measures directed at the same end, the total administrative burden would surely be reduced.</seg>
<seg id="2925">A few brief calculations suggest also that this proposal could be far less costly in money, let alone in the degree of governmental intervention involved, than our present collection of welfare measures.</seg>
<seg id="2926">Alternatively, these calculations can be regarded as showing how wasteful our present measures are, judged as measures for helping the poor.</seg>
<seg id="2927">In 1961, government amounted to something like $ 33 billion (federal, state, and local) on direct welfare payments and programs of all kinds:</seg>
<seg id="2928">old age assistance, social security benefit payments, aid to dependent children, general assistance, farm price support programs, public housing, etc.</seg>
<seg id="2929">I have excluded veterans' benefits in making this calculation.</seg>
<seg id="2930">I have also made no allowance for the direct and indirect costs of such measures as minimum-wage laws, tariffs, licensing provisions, and so on, or for the costs of public health activities, state and local expenditures on hospitals, mental institutions, and the like.</seg>
<seg id="2931">There are approximately 57 million consumer units (unattached individuals and families) in the United States.</seg>
<seg id="2932">The 1961 expenditures of $ 33 billion would have financed outright cash grants of nearly $ 6,000 per consumer unit to the 10 per cent with the lowest incomes.</seg>
<seg id="2933">Such grants would have raised their incomes above the average for all units in the United States.</seg>
<seg id="2934">Alternatively, these expenditures would have financed grants of nearly $ 3,000 per consumer unit to the 20 per cent with the lowest incomes.</seg>
<seg id="2935">Today, fewer than one-eighth of consumer units have an income, adjusted for the change in the level of prices, as low as that of the lowest third in the middle 1930's.</seg>
<seg id="2936">Clearly, these are all far more extravagant programs than can be justified to "alleviate poverty" even by a rather generous interpretation of that term.</seg>
<seg id="2937">A program which supplemented the incomes of the 20 per cent of the consumer units with the lowest incomes so as to raise them to the lowest income of the rest would cost less than half of what we are now spending.</seg>
<seg id="2938">The major disadvantage of the proposed negative income tax is its political implications.</seg>
<seg id="2939">It establishes a system under which taxes are imposed on some to pay subsidies to others.</seg>
<seg id="2940">And presumably, these others have a vote.</seg>
<seg id="2941">There is always the danger that instead of being an arrangement under which the great majority tax themselves willingly to help an unfortunate minority, it will be converted into one under which a majority imposes taxes for its own benefit on an unwilling minority.</seg>
<seg id="2942">Because this proposal makes the process so explicit, the danger is perhaps greater than with other measures.</seg>
<seg id="2943">I see no solution to this problem except to rely on the self-restraint and good will of the electorate.</seg>
<seg id="2944">Writing about a corresponding problemˇŞBritish old-age pensionsˇŞin 1914, Dicey said, "Surely a sensible and a benevolent man may well ask himself whether England as a whole will gain by enacting that the receipt of poor relief, in the shape of a pension, shall be consistent with the pensioner's retaining the right to join in the election of a Member of Parliament."</seg>
<seg id="2945">The verdict of experience in Britain on Dicey's question must as yet be regarded as mixed.</seg>
<seg id="2946">England did move to universal suffrage without the disfranchisement of either pensioners or other recipients of state aid.</seg>
<seg id="2947">And there has been an enormous expansion of taxation of some for the benefit of others, which must surely be regarded as having retarded Britain's growth, and so may not even have benefited most of those who regard themselves as on the receiving end.</seg>
<seg id="2948">But these measures have not destroyed, at least as yet, Britain's liberties or its predominantly capitalistic system.</seg>
<seg id="2949">And, more important, there have been some signs of a turning of the tide and of the exercise of self-restraint on the part of the electorate.</seg>
<seg id="2950">in his freedom to make the most of his capacities and opportunities according to his own lights, subject only to the proviso that he not interfere with the freedom of other individuals to do the same.</seg>
<seg id="2951">This implies a belief in the equality of men in one sense; in their inequality in another.</seg>
<seg id="2952">Each man has an equal right to freedom.</seg>
<seg id="2953">This is an important and fundamental right precisely because men are different, because one man will want to do different things with his freedom than another, and in the process can contribute more than another to the general culture of the society in which many men live.</seg>
<seg id="2954">The liberal will therefore distinguish sharply between equality of rights and equality of opportunity, on the one hand, and material equality or equality of outcome on the other.</seg>
<seg id="2955">He may welcome the fact that a free society in fact tends toward greater material equality than any other yet tried.</seg>
<seg id="2956">But he will regard this as a desirable by-product of a free society, not its major justification.</seg>
<seg id="2957">He will welcome measures that promote both freedom and equalityˇŞsuch as measures to eliminate monopoly power and to improve the operation of the market.</seg>
<seg id="2958">He will regard private charity directed at helping the less fortunate as an example of the proper use of freedom.</seg>
<seg id="2959">And he may approve state action toward ameliorating poverty as a more effective way in which the great bulk of the community can achieve a common objective.</seg>
<seg id="2960">He will do so with regret, however, at having to substitute compulsory for voluntary action.</seg>
<seg id="2961">The egalitarian will go this far, too.</seg>
<seg id="2962">But he will want to go further.</seg>
<seg id="2963">He will defend taking from some to give to others, not as a more effective means whereby the "some" can achieve an objective they want to achieve, but on grounds of "justice."</seg>
<seg id="2964">At this point, equality comes sharply into conflict with freedom;</seg>
<seg id="2965">one must choose.</seg>
<seg id="2966">One cannot be both an egalitarian, in this sense, and a liberal.</seg>
<seg id="2967">Chapter XIIIˇŞ Conclusion</seg>
<seg id="2968">In the 1920's and the 1930's, intellectuals in the United States were overwhelmingly persuaded that capitalism was a defective system inhibiting economic well-being and thereby freedom, and that the hope for the future lay in a greater measure of deliberate control by political authorities over economic affairs.</seg>
<seg id="2969">The conversion of the intellectuals was not achieved by the example of any actual collectivist society, though it undoubtedly was much hastened by the establishment of a communist society in Russia and the glowing hopes placed in it.</seg>
<seg id="2970">The conversion of the intellectuals was achieved by a comparison between the existing state of affairs, with all its injustices and defects, and a hypothetical state of affairs as it might be.</seg>
<seg id="2971">The actual was compared with the ideal.</seg>
<seg id="2972">At the time, not much else was possible.</seg>
<seg id="2973">True, mankind had experienced many epochs of centralized control, of detailed intervention by the state into economic affairs.</seg>
<seg id="2974">But there had been a revolution in politics, in science, and in technology.</seg>
<seg id="2975">Surely, it was argued, we can do far better with a democratic political structure, modern tools, and modern science than was possible in earlier ages.</seg>
<seg id="2976">The attitudes of that time are still with us.</seg>
<seg id="2977">There is still a tendency to regard any existing government intervention as desirable, to attribute all evils to the market, and to evaluate new proposals for government control in their ideal form, as they might work if run by able, disinterested men, free from the pressure of special interest groups.</seg>
<seg id="2978">The proponents of limited government and free enterprise are still on the defensive.</seg>
<seg id="2979">Yet, conditions have changed.</seg>
<seg id="2980">We now have several decades of experience with governmental intervention.</seg>
<seg id="2981">It is no longer necessary to compare the market as it actually operates and government intervention as it ideally might operate.</seg>
<seg id="2982">We can compare the actual with the actual.</seg>
<seg id="2983">If we do so, it is clear that the difference between the actual operation of the market and its ideal operationˇŞgreat though it undoubtedly isˇŞis as nothing compared to the difference between the actual effects of government intervention and their intended effects.</seg>
<seg id="2984">Who can now see any great hope for the advancement of men's freedom and dignity in the massive tyranny and despotism that hold sway in Russia?</seg>
<seg id="2985">Wrote Marx and Engels in The Communist Manifesto:</seg>
<seg id="2986">"The proletarians have nothing to lose but their chains.</seg>
<seg id="2987">They have a world to win."</seg>
<seg id="2988">Who today can regard the chains of the proletarians in the Soviet Union as weaker than the chains of the proletarians in the United States, or Britain or France or Germany or any Western state?</seg>
<seg id="2989">Let us look closer to home.</seg>
<seg id="2990">Which if any of the great "reforms" of past decades has achieved its objectives?</seg>
<seg id="2991">Have the good intentions of the proponents of these reforms been realized?</seg>
<seg id="2992">Regulation of the railroads to protect the consumer quickly became an instrument whereby the railroads could protect themselves from the competition of newly emerging rivalsˇŞat the expense, of course, of the consumer.</seg>
<seg id="2993">An income tax initially enacted at low rates and later seized upon as a means to redistribute income in favor of the lower classes has become a facade, covering loopholes and special provisions that render rates that arc highly graduated on paper largely ineffective.</seg>
<seg id="2994">A flat rate of 23 1/2 per cent on presently taxable income would yield as much revenue as the present rates graduated from 20 to 91 per cent.</seg>
<seg id="2995">An income tax intended to reduce inequality and promote the diffusion of wealth has in practice fostered reinvestment of corporate earnings, thereby favoring the growth of large corporations, inhibiting the operation of the capital market, and discouraging the establishment of new enterprises.</seg>
<seg id="2996">Monetary reforms, intended to promote stability in economic activity and prices, exacerbated inflation during and after World War I and fostered a higher degree of instability thereafter than had ever been experienced before.</seg>
<seg id="2997">The monetary authorities they established bear primary responsibility for converting a serious economic contraction into the catastrophe of the Great Depression from 1929¨C33.</seg>
<seg id="2998">A system established largely to prevent bank panics produced the most severe banking panic in American history.</seg>
<seg id="2999">An agricultural program intended to help impecunious farmers and to remove what were alleged to be basic dislocations in the organization of agriculture has become a national scandal that has wasted public funds, distorted the use of resources, riveted increasingly heavy and detailed controls on farmers, interfered seriously with United States foreign policy, and withal has done little to help the impecunious farmer.</seg>
<seg id="3000">A housing program intended to improve the housing conditions of the poor, to reduce juvenile delinquency, and to contribute to the removal of urban slums, has worsened the housing conditions of the poor, contributed to juvenile delinquency, and spread urban blight.</seg>
<seg id="3001">In the 1930's, "labor" was synonomous with "labor union" to the intellectual community;</seg>
<seg id="3002">faith in the purity and virtue of labor unions was on a par with faith in home and motherhood.</seg>
<seg id="3003">Extensive legislation was enacted to favor labor unions and to foster "fair" labor relation.</seg>
<seg id="3004">Labor unions waxed in strength.</seg>
<seg id="3005">By the 1950's, "labor union" was almost a dirty word;</seg>
<seg id="3006">it as no longer synonomous with "labor," no longer automatically to be taken for granted as on the side of the angels.</seg>
<seg id="3007">Social security measures were enacted to make receipt of assistance a matter of right, to eliminate the need for direct relief and assistance.</seg>
<seg id="3008">Millions now receive social security benefits.</seg>
<seg id="3009">Yet the relief rolls grow and the sums spent on direct assistance mount.</seg>
<seg id="3010">The list can easily be lengthened:</seg>
<seg id="3011">the silver purchase program of the 1930's, public power projects, foreign aid programs of the post-war years, F.C.C., urban redevelopment programs, the stockpiling programˇŞthese and many more have had effects very different and generally quite opposite from those intended.</seg>
<seg id="3012">There have been some exceptions.</seg>
<seg id="3013">The expressways crisscrossing the country, magnificent dams spanning great rivers, orbiting satellites are all tributes to the capacity of government to command great resources.</seg>
<seg id="3014">The school system, with all its defects and problems, with all the possibility of improvement through bringing into more effective play the forces of the market, has widened the opportunities available to American youth and contributed to the extension of freedom.</seg>
<seg id="3015">It is a testament to the public-spirited efforts of the many tens of thousands who have served on local school boards and to the willingness of the public to bear heavy taxes for what they regarded as a public purpose.</seg>
<seg id="3016">The Sherman antitrust laws, with all their problems of detailed administration, have by their very existence fostered competition.</seg>
<seg id="3017">Public health measures have contributed to the reduction of infectious disease.</seg>
<seg id="3018">Assistance measures have relieved suffering and distress.</seg>
<seg id="3019">Local authorities have often provided facilities essential to the life of communities.</seg>
<seg id="3020">Law and order have been maintained, though in many a large city the performance of even this elementary function of government has been far from satisfactory.</seg>
<seg id="3021">As a citizen of Chicago, I speak feelingly.</seg>
<seg id="3022">If a balance be struck, there can be little doubt that the record is dismal.</seg>
<seg id="3023">The greater part of the new ventures undertaken by government in the past few decades have failed to achieve their objectives.</seg>
<seg id="3024">The United States has continued to progress;</seg>
<seg id="3025">its citizens have become better fed, better clothed, better housed, and better transported;</seg>
<seg id="3026">class and social distinctions have narrowed;</seg>
<seg id="3027">minority groups have become less disadvantaged;</seg>
<seg id="3028">popular culture has advanced by leaps and bounds.</seg>
<seg id="3029">All this has been the product of the initiative and drive of individuals co-operating through the free market.</seg>
<seg id="3030">Government measures have hampered not helped this development.</seg>
<seg id="3031">We have been able to afford and surmount these measures only because of the extraordinary fecundity of the market.</seg>
<seg id="3032">The invisible hand has been more potent for progress than the visible hand for retrogression.</seg>
<seg id="3033">Is it an accident that so many of the governmental reforms of recent decades have gone awry, that the bright hopes have turned to ashes?</seg>
<seg id="3034">Is it simply because the programs are faulty in detail?</seg>
<seg id="3035">I believe the answer is clearly in the negative.</seg>
<seg id="3036">The central defect of these measures is that they seek through government to force people to act against their own immediate interests in order to promote a supposedly general interest.</seg>
<seg id="3037">They seek to resolve what is supposedly a conflict of interest, or a difference in view about interests, not by establishing a framework that will eliminate the conflict, or by persuading people to have different interests, but by forcing people to act against their own interest.</seg>
<seg id="3038">They substitute the values of outsiders for the values of participants;</seg>
<seg id="3039">either some telling others what is good for them, or the government taking from some to benefit others.</seg>
<seg id="3040">These measures are therefore countered by one of the strongest and most creative forces known to manˇŞthe attempt by millions of individuals to promote their own interests, to live their lives by their own values.</seg>
<seg id="3041">This is the major reason why the measures have so often had the opposite of the effects intended.</seg>
<seg id="3042">It is also one of the major strengths of a free society and explains why governmental regulation docs not strangle it.</seg>
<seg id="3043">The interests of which I speak are not simply narrow self-regarding interests.</seg>
<seg id="3044">On the contrary, they include the whole range of values that men hold dear and for which they are willing to spend their fortunes and sacrifice their lives.</seg>
<seg id="3045">The Germans who lost their lives opposing Adolph Hitler were pursuing their interests as they saw them.</seg>
<seg id="3046">So also are the men and women who devote great effort and time to charitable, educational, and religious activities.</seg>
<seg id="3047">Naturally, such interests are the major ones for few men.</seg>
<seg id="3048">It is the virtue of a free society that it nonetheless permits these interests full scope and does not subordinate them to the narrow materialistic interests that dominate the bulk of mankind.</seg>
<seg id="3049">That is why capitalist societies are less materialistic than collectivist societies.</seg>
<seg id="3050">Why is it, in light of the record, that the burden of proof still seems to rest on those of us who oppose new government programs and who seek to reduce the already unduly large role of government?</seg>
<seg id="3051">Let Dicey answer:</seg>
<seg id="3052">''The beneficial effect of State intervention, especially in the form of legislation, is direct, immediate, and, so to speak, visible, whilst its evil effects are gradual and indirect, and lie out of sight. . . .</seg>
<seg id="3053">Nor. . . do most people keep in mind that State inspectors may be incompetent, careless, or even occasionally corrupt . . . ;</seg>
<seg id="3054">few are those who realize the undeniable truth that State help kills self-help.</seg>
<seg id="3055">Hence the majority of mankind must almost of necessity look with undue favor upon governmental intervention.</seg>
<seg id="3056">This natural bias can be counteracted only by the existence, in a given society, . . . of a presumption or prejudice in favor of individual liberty, that is, of laissez-faire.</seg>
<seg id="3057">The mere decline, therefore, of faith in self-helpˇŞand that such a decline has taken place is certainˇŞis of itself sufficient to account for the growth of legislation tending towards socialism."1</seg>
<seg id="3058">The preservation and expansion of freedom are today threatened from two directions.</seg>
<seg id="3059">The one threat is obvious and clear.</seg>
<seg id="3060">It is the external threat coming from the evil men in the Kremlin who promise to bury us.</seg>
<seg id="3061">The other threat is far more subtle.</seg>
<seg id="3062">It is the internal threat coming from men of good intentions and good will who wish to reform us.</seg>
<seg id="3063">Impatient with the slowness of persuasion and example to achieve the great social changes they envision, they are anxious to use the power of the state to achieve their ends and confident of their own ability to do so.</seg>
<seg id="3064">Yet if they gained the power, they would fail to achieve their immediate aims and, in addition, would produce a collective state from which they would recoil in horror and of which they would be among the first victims.</seg>
<seg id="3065">Concentrated power is not rendered harmless by the good intentions of those who create it.</seg>
<seg id="3066">The two threats unfortunately reinforce one another.</seg>
<seg id="3067">Even if we avoid a nuclear holocaust, the threat from the Kremlin requires us to devote a sizable fraction of our resources to our military defense.</seg>
<seg id="3068">The importance of government as a buyer of so much of our output, and the sole buyer of the output of many firms and industries, already concentrates a dangerous amount of economic power in the hands of the political authorities, changes the environment in which business operates and the criteria relevant for business success, and in these and other ways endangers a free market.</seg>
<seg id="3069">This danger we cannot avoid.</seg>
<seg id="3070">But we needlessly intensify it by continuing the present widespread governmental intervention in areas unrelated to the military defense of the nation and by undertaking ever new governmental programsˇŞfrom medical care for the aged to lunar exploration.</seg>
<seg id="3071">As Adam Smith once said, "There is much ruin in a nation".</seg>
<seg id="3072">Our basic structure of values and the interwoven network of free institutions will withstand much.</seg>
<seg id="3073">I believe that we shall be able to preserve and extend freedom despite the size of the military programs and despite the economic powers already concentrated in Washington.</seg>
<seg id="3074">But we shall be able to do so only if we awake to the threat that we face, only if we persuade our fellow men that free institutions offer a surer, if perhaps at times a slower, route to the ends they seek than the coercive power of the state.</seg>
<seg id="3075">The glimmerings of change that are already apparent in the intellectual climate are a hopeful augury.</seg>
